Introduction 
Welcome to the 2026 edition of The Theses! 
 
2025 was perhaps one of the most polarizing years for the crypto industry. Your ultimate view of the year depends largely on who you are. If 
you’re reading this report while sitting in an oﬃce overlooking Wall Street, it’s been your best year in crypto…ever. And if you’re at the opposite 
end of the spectrum, perhaps a trench warrior spending 12+ hours a day lurking in Telegram and Discord chats, you’re probably longing for the 
times of yesterday. If anything, 2025 proved that our industry is just as volatile as the price charts we spend all day looking at: 
● We started oﬀ the year with, in retrospect, the most exhilarating token launch of all time: TRUMP. Although the euphoria from it quickly 
subsided, it was a valuable stress test and helped prove just how far onchain infrastructure has come. 
● The largest government in the world proclaimed Bitcoin a unique store of value, made it oﬃcial policy to hold Bitcoin as a reserve asset, 
and even left the door open for future budget-neutral acquisitions. Just two years ago, this would have seemed like a fantasy beyond our 
wildest imaginations. 
● The identity crisis that has plagued Ethereum for the past two years ﬁnally reached its climax, and Ethereum is better for it. Ethereum is 
now solidifying its position as the center of institutional adoption. 
● DATs became the hot topic, and their collective bid pushed the asset class to new all-time highs. 
● The ﬁrst landmark U.S. crypto legislation, GENIUS, was signed into law, laying the groundwork for what the U.S. Treasury Secretary 
expects could scale into trillions of dollars in stablecoins. 
● The largest crypto deleveraging event in history occurred on October 10, with many assets dropping by 50% or more in minutes, and some 
assets even brieﬂy trading at zero (literal zero). Since then, crypto has struggled to keep pace with other asset classes it has historically 
correlated with, including gold, silver, and equities. 
● The businesses of tomorrow are being built onchain. On a per-employee basis, Hyperliquid and Tether may be among the most proﬁtable 
companies ever. Applications like Polymarket and pump.fun crossed the chasm and entered the zeitgeist. 
Through it all, Messari Research has kept you up to speed. We released hallmark reports on two of the year’s biggest megatrends: stablecoins and 
AI. Our early 2024 Hyperliquid call was vindicated, and we covered its ascension throughout the year. As the industry began questioning how to 
value cryptoassets, we published valuation frameworks across a wide range of sectors, including L1s, DePIN, lending protocols, and more niche 
assets, such as launchpads and AI hedge funds. We remained nimble enough to cover everything from Trump’s tariﬀ policy and its downstream 
eﬀects on crypto to how tokenization could revolutionize the collectibles industry and bring Pokémon cards onchain. Our Protocol Services team 
has partnered with over 150 protocols, providing objective insights to advance the space.

Cryptomoney is the Foundation of the 
Industry 
Authors: AJC, Drexel Bakker, and Youssef Haidar 
Key Themes 
● Bitcoin has ﬁrmly established itself apart from all other cryptoassets and is undoubtedly the leading form of cryptomoney. 
● BTC underperformance in the second half of this year can be partly attributed to increased sell pressure from older, large-balance holders. 
We don’t see any strong reasons for this underperformance to become a long-term structural issue and expect BTC’s monetary narrative to 
remain intact for the foreseeable future. 
● L1 valuations are becoming increasingly detached from their fundamentals. L1 revenues have declined signiﬁcantly year-over-year, 
meaning their valuations are increasingly predicated upon the notion of a monetary premium. Barring a few outliers, we expect L1s to 
underperform BTC. 
● ETH remains the most hotly contested asset. Concerns over value accrual haven’t been fully alleviated, but the second half of 2025 proved 
that the market is willing to treat it as cryptomoney in the same vein as BTC. A “second life” for Ethereum DATs could emerge if a crypto 
bull market returns in 2026. 
● ZEC is increasingly being priced as a form of private cryptomoney rather than a niche privacy coin, positioning it as a complementary 
hedge to BTC in an era of rising surveillance, institutional capture, and ﬁnancial repression. 
               
 


---

## Page 4

3 
● Applications may begin to opt for their own monetary systems rather than relying on the native assets of the networks they exist on. 
Applications with social elements and strong network eﬀects are prime candidates to do so. 
Introduction 
It’s no mistake that we’re opening this year’s Theses with a discussion on the most fundamentally important part of the crypto revolution: money. 
When we initially began planning for the Theses over the summer, we never would’ve imagined how far the pendulum would swing to negative 
sentiment. 
 
In November 2025, the Crypto Fear and Greed Index sank to a 10, “Extreme Fear.” Prior to this year, the only other times it printed a 10 or lower 
were: 
● The Luna collapse and 3AC contagion in May-June 2022 
● The mass liquidation cascade in May 2021 
               
 


---

## Page 5

4 
● The COVID crash in March 2020 
● Various points during the 2018-2019 bear market 
Sentiment in crypto has only been lower a handful of times in its entire history, and only when the industry was genuinely collapsing in on itself, 
and the future was truly in doubt. Yet that is clearly not what’s happening today. No major exchanges have disappeared with user funds, no blatant 
ponzi schemes are commanding decabillion valuations, and total market cap isn’t breaking below prior cycle highs. 
Instead, crypto is being recognized and integrated at the highest levels of global institutions. The SEC is openly expecting all U.S. markets to be 
onchain within two years. Stablecoin supply is at an all-time high. All the ‘someday’ adoption narratives we repeated for a decade are ﬁnally 
materializing, and yet, it has almost never felt worse. Almost every other week, a viral post circulates on X from someone convinced they wasted 
their life on crypto, or declaring that everything this industry built will simply be forked, co-opted, and captured by incumbent institutions. 
Amidst this disconnect between collapsing sentiment and rising institutional adoption, it’s the perfect time to revisit crypto from ﬁrst principles. 
And the original principle, the one that has since spawned this chaotic, beautiful industry we’ve all come to love, is simple: build a better, 
alternative monetary system than the ﬁat system we have today. That ideal has been embedded in the industry’s DNA since the genesis Bitcoin 
block, which famously included the message: “The Times 03/Jan/2009 Chancellor on brink of second bailout for banks.” 
               
 


---

## Page 6

5 
 
These origins matter because, somewhere along the way, many people have forgotten what crypto was for. Bitcoin wasn’t created to give banks 
better settlement rails. It wasn’t created to shave a few basis points oﬀ FX transfers. And it wasn’t created to power an endless slot machine of 
speculative tokens. It was created as a response to a failing monetary system. 
So if we’re going to make sense of where crypto stands today, we need to return to the core issue at the center of the entire industry: why 
cryptomoney matters at all. 
Why Cryptomoney? 
For most of modern history, people have had no meaningful choice in the kind of money they use. Under today’s ﬁat-based global monetary order, 
people are eﬀectively captive to the currency of their home country and the decisions of that country’s central bank. The state decides the money 
you earn in, the money you save in, and the money you pay taxes in. Whether that currency inﬂates, depreciates, or is mismanaged, you’re stuck 
with it. And across nearly every type of political and economic system, free-market, authoritarian, or developing, the same pattern emerges. 
Government debt is a one-way street. 
               
 


---

## Page 7

6 
 
Over the past quarter-century, the world’s largest economies have all taken on substantially more debt relative to their GDP. The United States and 
China, the two largest economies, have had their general government debt-to-GDP ratios grow by 127% and 289%, respectively. Regardless of 
political structure or growth model, rising government indebtedness has become a structural feature of the global ﬁnancial system. And when debt 
grows faster than economic output, the costs fall most heavily on savers. Inﬂation and low real interest rates erode the value of ﬁat savings, 
transferring wealth from individual savers to the state. 
               
 


---

## Page 8

7 
 
Cryptomoney oﬀers an alternative to this system by separating the state from money. Throughout history, governments have altered monetary 
rules through inﬂation, capital controls, or restrictive regulations when it serves their interests. Cryptomoney places monetary governance in the 
hands of a decentralized network rather than a central authority. In doing so, it reintroduces the option of monetary choice. Savers can opt into a 
monetary asset that aligns with their priorities, needs, and wants, rather than being trapped in one that often works against their long-term ﬁnancial 
well-being. 
But choice is only meaningful if what you’re choosing has real advantages. Cryptomoney does, and its value rests on several properties that 
distinguish it from everything that came before it. The ﬁrst cornerstone of cryptomoney’s value is its predictable, rules-based monetary policy. 
These rules are not promises from an institution but properties of software run by thousands of independent participants. Changing them requires 
a broad consensus rather than the discretion of a small group, making arbitrary monetary shifts extraordinarily diﬃcult. In contrast to ﬁat systems, 
where the money supply expands in response to political and economic pressures, cryptomoney operates on rules that can’t be quietly changed 
behind closed doors, as they’re public, predictable, and enforced by consensus. 
Cryptomoney also transforms how individuals custody their wealth. In the ﬁat system, meaningful self-custody has become impractical; most 
               
 


---

## Page 9

8 
people rely on banks or other ﬁnancial intermediaries to store their savings. Even traditional non-sovereign alternatives, such as gold, typically end 
up in centralized vaults, reintroducing the same trust-based risks. In practice, this means that your assets can be delayed, restricted, or denied 
entirely if the custodian or the state decides to do so. 
 
Cryptomoney enables direct ownership, allowing individuals to hold and secure their assets without relying on custodians. As ﬁnancial 
restrictions, from bank withdrawal limits to capital controls, become more common globally, this capability becomes increasingly important. 
Finally, cryptomoney is designed for a global, digital world. It can move across borders instantly, in any amount, without institutional permission. 
This provides it with a signiﬁcant advantage over something like gold, as gold is diﬃcult to divide, verify, or transport, especially across borders. 
Cryptomoney can be sent globally in minutes, at any scale, and without reliance on a centralized intermediary. This ensures that individuals can 
move or deploy their wealth freely, regardless of their geographical location or the prevailing political environment. 
Stepping back, the case for cryptomoney is straightforward. It provides individuals with monetary choice, establishes predictable rules, eliminates 
single points of failure, and enables value to move anywhere in the world without restriction. In a system where government debt keeps climbing 
and savers absorb the consequences, the value of cryptomoney will only continue to grow. 
Bitcoin is the Leading Cryptomoney 
Bitcoin created the category of cryptomoney, so it makes sense to start there. Almost seventeen years later, BTC remains the largest and most 
widely recognized asset in the entire industry. And because money is ultimately a product of social consensus, not technical design choices, the 
real determinant of monetary status is whether the market assigns a long-lasting premium to an asset. By that measure, BTC’s position as the 
leading cryptomoney is clear. 
               
 


---

## Page 10

9 
 
We can see this most clearly in BTC’s performance over the past three years. From December 1, 2022, BTC has rallied by 429%, rising from 
$17,200 to $90,400. Along the way, it has set multiple new all-time highs, with the most recent one on Oct. 6, 2025 ($126,200). At the start of this 
period, BTC’s ~$318 billion market cap would have placed it well outside the ranks of the world’s largest assets. Today, its market cap stands at 
$1.81 trillion, making it the ninth most valuable asset globally. The market has not only rewarded BTC’s monetary properties with higher price 
appreciation, but has elevated it into the upper tier of global asset rankings. 
But the more telling development is how BTC has behaved compared to the rest of the crypto market. Historically, during crypto bull markets, 
Bitcoin Dominance (BTC.D) contracts as capital shifts further along the risk curve; however, the trend has completely reversed during this BTC 
bull run. Over the same three-year time period, BTC.D has increased from 36.6% to 57.3% (including stablecoins). BTC is diverging from the 
broader crypto market. 
               
 


---

## Page 11

10 
 
Of the top ﬁfteen cryptoassets from December 1, 2022, only two (XRP and SOL) have outperformed BTC, and only one, SOL, has done so in a 
meaningful way (+888% vs. BTC’s +429%). The rest of the market has lagged signiﬁcantly, with many large-cap assets barely positive or even 
negative over the same period. ETH is up just 135%, BNB 200%, and DOGE 44%, while names like POL (-85%), DOT (-59%), and ATOM 
(-77%) remain deeply underwater. What makes this even more notable is BTC’s size. As a trillion-dollar asset, it should take the most capital to 
move, yet it still outperformed nearly every major token. There is real, sustained buy pressure for BTC speciﬁcally, while most other assets are 
behaving like beta, rising only when BTC pulls the entire market upward. 
               
 


---

## Page 12

11 
 
A major driver of BTC’s sustained buying pressure has been the increasing adoption by institutions. The deﬁning feature of this institutional 
adoption has been the spot BTC ETFs. Demand for these vehicles has been so insatiable that BlackRock’s iShares Bitcoin Trust (IBIT) has broken 
multiple ETF records and has been lauded as the “Greatest Launch in ETF History.” IBIT reached $70 billion in AUM just 341 days after launch, 
shattering the SPDR Gold Shares (GLD) ETF’s previous record by 1,350 days. 
               
 


---

## Page 13

12 
 
Momentum from the 2024 ETF launches carried directly into 2025, with total ETF AUM rising 20% year-to-date, from roughly 1.1 million BTC 
to 1.32 million BTC. At current prices, that represents more than $120 billion worth of BTC held through these products and accounts for over 6% 
of BTC’s maximum supply. Rather than fading after the initial launch hype, ETF demand has become a continuous source of buying pressure, 
accumulating BTC regardless of market conditions. 
               
 


---

## Page 14

13 
 
Furthermore, institutional participation has extended far beyond ETFs. Digital Asset Treasuries (DATs) emerged as major buyers throughout 2025, 
further reinforcing BTC’s role as a treasury reserve asset. While Michael Saylor’s Strategy has long been the most visible example of corporate 
accumulation, there are now nearly 200 companies globally holding BTC on their balance sheets. Publicly traded ﬁrms alone own roughly 1.06 
million BTC, about 5% of the total supply, with Strategy accounting for 650,000, the largest share by a wide margin. 
Perhaps the most important development in 2025 that separated BTC from the rest of the crypto market was the establishment of the Strategic 
Bitcoin Reserve (SBR). The SBR formally recognized a distinction that markets already made between BTC and the rest of the crypto market, 
treating BTC as a strategic monetary commodity while placing all other digital assets into a separate stockpile for standard stewardship. In its 
announcement, the White House described BTC as a “unique store of value in the global ﬁnancial system” and likened it to “digital gold.” Lastly, 
and perhaps most importantly, the order directs the Treasury to develop strategies for acquiring additional BTC in the future. While no such 
purchases have occurred yet, the optionality alone signals that federal policy is now considering BTC in forward-looking, reserve-asset terms. If 
executed, such an acquisition program would further cement Bitcoin’s monetary status not just among cryptoassets, but all assets. 
               
 


---

## Page 15

14 
Is BTC Good Money? 
Although BTC has solidiﬁed its lead over other forms of cryptomoney, 2025 raised a fresh set of questions about its monetary properties. As the 
largest non-sovereign monetary asset, gold remains BTC’s most important benchmark. With geopolitical tensions rising and expectations of future 
monetary easing increasing, gold posted one of its strongest annual performances in decades. BTC, however, did not follow. 
 
While the BTC/XAU ratio brieﬂy hit new all-time highs in December 2024, it has since retraced by roughly 50%. That retracement is notable 
because it occurred as gold surged to new all-time highs against the U.S. Dollar. Year-to-date, gold is up over 60% to $4,150 per troy ounce. With 
gold’s total market value approaching $30 trillion and BTC still representing only a fraction of that, the divergence raises a reasonable question: 
How strong is BTC’s “digital gold” status if it is not keeping pace with gold during one of gold’s strongest cycles? 
               
 


---

## Page 16

15 
 
If Bitcoin is not trading in line with gold, the next place to look is how it behaved relative to traditional risk assets. Historically, BTC has exhibited 
periods of correlation with equity benchmarks, including SPY and QQQ. For example, from April 2020 to April 2025, BTC had a relatively 
strong average 90-day correlation with SPY of 0.52, and a relatively weak correlation with gold (0.18). Because of this, if equities were 
weakening, BTC’s lag relative to gold might be understandable. 
               
 


---

## Page 17

16 
 
But that is not the case. Year-to-date, XAU is up 60%, SPY is up 17.6%, and QQQ is up 21.6%, while BTC is down 2.9%. Considering that BTC 
is a much smaller and more volatile asset than gold and major equity indices, 2025’s underperformance relative to these benchmarks raises 
reasonable questions about its monetary narrative. With gold and equities pushing to new all-time highs, one might expect BTC to exhibit similar 
behavior, especially given its historical correlations. Yet, that is not the case. Why? 
First, it’s worth noting that this underperformance is a recent development rather than a yearlong theme. As recently as Aug. 14, 2025, BTC had 
posted higher year-to-date returns than XAU, SPY, and QQQ on an absolute basis. Its relative weakness did not begin to take shape until October. 
What stands out is not how long the underperformance has lasted, but how severe it has been. 
While several factors may have contributed to this severity, we believe the most important driver of BTC’s muted performance is the behavior of 
older, large-balance holders. As BTC has become increasingly institutionalized over the past two years, its liquidity proﬁle has changed 
meaningfully. Deep, regulated markets, especially through ETFs, now allow large holders to sell without imposing the kind of market impact that 
would have been unavoidable in earlier cycles. For many of these holders, this created the ﬁrst real opportunity to take proﬁts. 
               
 


---

## Page 18

17 
 
There is both ample anecdotal and onchain evidence to suggest that some long-dormant holders have taken this opportunity to reduce exposure. 
Earlier this year, Galaxy Digital facilitated the sale of 80,000 BTC from a “Satoshi-era investor.” This single transaction represented 0.38% of 
Bitcoin’s total supply and originated from a single entity. 
               
 


---

## Page 19

18 
 
Bitcoin onchain metrics tell a similar story. Some of the largest and longest-standing holders, addresses holding between 1,000 and 100,000 BTC, 
have been net distributors throughout 2025. These addresses held 6.9 million BTC at the start of the year, almost one-third of all BTC in existence, 
and have been consistent sellers throughout the year. The 1,000-10,000 BTC cohort has seen net outﬂows of 417,300 BTC (-9% YTD), and the 
10,000-100,000 BTC cohort has recorded an additional 51,700 BTC in outﬂows (-2% YTD). 
As Bitcoin becomes more institutionalized and a greater share of ﬂows migrates oﬀchain, the informational value of onchain metrics will naturally 
diminish. Even so, taken together with anecdotal evidence such as the Satoshi-era sale, the data provide a reasonable basis to conclude that early, 
large holders have been net sellers in 2025, particularly in the second half of the year. 
               
 


---

## Page 20

19 
 
This wave of supply has also coincided with a meaningful slowdown in the buying ﬂows that have been the predominant drivers of BTC price 
appreciation over the past two years. Inﬂows from DATs dropped sharply in October, marking the ﬁrst month in 2025 in which DATs did not 
record net inﬂows of more than $1 billion. On the other hand, the spot ETFs, which had been consistent net buyers throughout the year, have been 
net sellers since October. With the two largest sources of steady BTC demand, DATs and ETFs, appearing exhausted for the moment, the market 
has had to absorb both declining inﬂows and increased distribution from older holders simultaneously. 
With all that, is there cause for concern? Has BTC’s moneyness been invalidated by the recent underperformance? In our opinion, the answer is 
no. As the saying goes, “when in doubt, zoom out.” It is hard to dismiss BTC solely due to a three-month stretch of weakness, especially when 
BTC has underperformed for far longer periods in the past, only to rebound and set new all-time highs against both USD and gold. While this 
underperformance is a setback for the time being, we do not view it as a structural issue. 
Looking ahead to 2026 is a diﬀerent challenge. As BTC becomes increasingly treated as a macro asset, legacy frameworks, such as the four-year 
cycle, matter less than they once did. BTC’s performance will be shaped by broader macro forces, which means the relevant questions become: 
Do central banks continue to accumulate gold? Does the AI-driven equity trade keep accelerating? Will Trump ﬁre Powell? If so, will Trump force 
               
 


---

## Page 21

20 
the new Fed chair to start buying BTC? These are diﬃcult variables to forecast, and we make no claim to know their outcomes. 
What we are conﬁdent in, however, is BTC’s long-term monetary trajectory. Over multi-year and multi-decade time horizons, we expect BTC to 
continue appreciating in monetary terms, against both USD and gold. Holding this view ultimately comes down to a simple question: “Is 
cryptomoney a superior form of money?” If the answer is yes, then the long-run direction of BTC becomes clear. 
Beyond BTC: What about the L1s? 
BTC has clearly established itself as the leading cryptomoney, but it is not the only cryptoasset with a monetary premium. Several Layer-1 (L1) 
tokens also trade with valuations that reﬂect some degree of monetary premium, or expectations that they may develop one over time. 
 
The total crypto market cap sits at $3.26 trillion. BTC accounts for $1.80 trillion of that, and of the remaining $1.45 trillion, roughly $0.83 trillion 
is concentrated in alternative L1s. Taken together, about $2.63 trillion, roughly 81% of all capital in crypto, is allocated to assets the market either 
already treats as money or believes could accrue a monetary premium. 
               
 


---

## Page 22

21 
Given that, whether you’re a trader, investor, capital allocator, or builder, understanding how the market assigns and withdraws monetary premium 
is essential. Nothing in crypto moves valuations more than the market’s willingness (or unwillingness) to treat an asset as money. For that reason, 
forecasting where monetary premiums will accrue in the future is arguably the single most important input to portfolio construction in this 
industry. 
As mentioned earlier, we anticipate that BTC will continue to capture market share from gold and other non-sovereign stores of wealth in the 
coming years. But where does that leave L1s? Does a rising tide lift all boats, or does BTC partly make up the ground on gold by pulling monetary 
premium away from alternative L1s? 
 
First, it’s helpful to look at where L1 valuations stand today. The top four L1s, ETH ($361.15 billion), XRP ($130.11 billion), BNB ($120.64 
billion), and SOL ($74.68 billion), combine for a market cap of $686.58 billion, accounting for 83% of the alternative L1 segment. After the top 
four, valuations drop oﬀ quickly (TRX sits at $26.67 billion), but they remain material. AVAX, the ﬁfteenth-largest L1, still commands a valuation 
north of $5 billion. 
Importantly, L1 market caps are not a pure reﬂection of an implied monetary premium. There are three main L1 valuation frameworks: (i) 
               
 


---

## Page 23

22 
Monetary Premium, (ii) Real Economic Value (REV), and (iii) Demand for Economic Security, so a project’s market cap is not solely the result of 
the market treating it as money. 
 
Despite these competing valuation frameworks, the market is increasingly valuing L1s through a monetary-premium lens rather than a 
revenue-driven one. Over the past few years, the collective price-to-sales (P/S) ratio of all L1s with a market cap above $1 billion has been slowly 
increasing from 200x to 400x. However, this headline number is misleading because it includes TRON and Hyperliquid. Over the last 30 days, 
TRX and HYPE generated 51% of the revenue in this group but account for only 4% of the market cap. 
Once you remove those two outliers, the real story becomes clear: L1 valuations have been rising even as revenues have been falling. The adjusted 
P/S ratio has consistently trended higher: 
● Nov. 30, 2021 - 40x 
● Nov. 30, 2022 - 212x 
● Nov. 30, 2023 - 137x 
               
 


---

## Page 24

23 
● Nov. 30, 2024 - 205x 
● Nov. 30, 2025 - 536x 
A REV-based interpretation would argue that the market is simply pricing in future revenue growth, but this explanation falls apart under basic 
scrutiny. Among this same basket of L1s (still excluding TRON and Hyperliquid), revenues have declined in every year except one: 
● 2021 - $12.33 billion 
● 2022 - $4.89 billion (down 60% YoY) 
● 2023 - $2.72 billion (down 44% YoY) 
● 2024 - $3.55 billion (up 31% YoY) 
● 2025 - $1.70 billion annualized (down 52% YoY) 
In our view, the simplest and most straightforward explanation is that these valuations are being driven by monetary premiums, rather than by 
current or future revenues. 
 
Diving deeper into SOL’s outperformance, it appears that it may have underperformed relative to its ecosystem growth. Over the same period that 
               
 


---

## Page 25

24 
SOL outperformed BTC by 87%, Solana’s fundamentals went parabolic. DeFi TVL increased by 2,988%, fees by 1,983%, and DEX volume by 
3,301%. By any reasonable measure, Solana’s ecosystem has grown anywhere from 20x to 30x since December 1, 2022. Yet SOL, the asset meant 
to capture that growth, only managed to outperform BTC by 87%. 
Read that again. 
To generate meaningful outperformance against BTC, an L1 didn’t need its ecosystem to grow by 200-300%. It needed 2,000-3,000% growth just 
to produce high-double-digit outperformance. 
Based on all the above, we believe that while L1s are still being valued on the hope of future monetary premiums, the market’s conﬁdence in those 
expectations is quietly eroding. Meanwhile, the market is not losing faith in BTC’s monetary premium, and if anything, BTC’s lead is widening. 
And while cryptomoney doesn’t technically need fees or revenue to justify its valuation, those metrics do matter for L1s. Unlike BTC, their 
narrative depends on building ecosystems (applications, users, throughput, economic activity, etc.) that supposedly support the token. However, if 
an L1’s ecosystem is showing year-over-year declines in usage, partly reﬂected in falling fees and revenue, then that token loses the one 
competitive advantage it had over BTC. Without real economic growth, the cryptomoney story for these L1s becomes increasingly diﬃcult for the 
market to believe. 
Going forward, we don’t expect this trend to reverse in 2026 or in the years to come. With a few possible exceptions, we expect alternative L1s to 
continue losing market share to BTC. Their valuations, driven largely by expectations of a future monetary premium, will steadily compress as the 
market increasingly recognizes that BTC has the strongest claim to cryptomoney of them all. While Bitcoin will face challenges in the years 
ahead, those issues are too far out and too dependent on unknown variables to oﬀer meaningful support to the monetary premiums of competing 
L1 assets today. For L1s, the burden of proof has shifted. Their narratives are no longer compelling when set against BTC, and they can’t rely on 
broad market euphoria to justify their valuations indeﬁnitely. 
Devil’s Advocate: The Case for L1s against BTC 
Even though we do not expect L1s to outperform BTC in the near term, it would be a mistake to assume their monetary premiums must converge 
to zero. Markets rarely assign multi-hundred-billion-dollar valuations to assets without some underlying rationale, and the persistence of these 
valuations suggests that investors believe certain L1s may retain an enduring role within the broader cryptomoney landscape. In other words, 
while BTC has clearly separated itself as the dominant monetary asset in crypto, the possibility remains that some L1s could carve out their own 
long-term monetary niches, particularly if Bitcoin fails to address several structural challenges that lie further out on the horizon. 
Quantum Threat 
The most pressing threat to BTC’s monetary status is the “quantum threat.” If quantum computers become powerful enough, they could break 
Bitcoin’s elliptic curve digital signature algorithm (ECDSA), allowing an attacker to derive private keys from public keys. In theory, this would 
               
 


---

## Page 26

25 
jeopardize any address whose public key has been exposed onchain, including reused addresses and old UTXOs that were created before best 
practices were widely adopted. 
According to Nic Carter, approximately 4.8 million BTC (23% of the supply) reside in exposed addresses that would be vulnerable to a theoretical 
quantum attack. Of that amount, 1.7 million BTC (8% of supply) reside in legacy p2pk addresses that are almost certainly “dead coins,” coins 
whose owners are no longer alive, active, or in possession of their keys. These coins represent the biggest unresolved dilemma. 
If quantum computing ever becomes a credible risk, Bitcoin will need to introduce support for quantum-resistant signature schemes. Without such 
a transition, BTC’s monetary value would collapse, and the old maxim would ﬂip into “your keys, not your coins.” Thus, we ﬁnd it very unlikely 
that the Bitcoin network won’t upgrade to address the quantum threat. However, the real issue is not upgrading Bitcoin, but what to do with the 
“dead coins.” Even if a new quantum-resistant address format is introduced, these coins will likely remain vulnerable forever. 
These are the two most commonly discussed approaches: 
● Do nothing. Eventually, anyone with quantum capabilities could seize these coins and reintroduce up to 8% of the supply into circulation, 
likely to holders who never owned them. This would almost certainly depress the price of BTC and undermine monetary conﬁdence. 
● Burn the coins. After a predetermined block height, vulnerable coins could be rendered unspendable and eﬀectively removed from the 
supply. But this approach introduces its own trade-oﬀs as it violates Bitcoin’s norm of censorship resistance, and risks setting a precedent 
that coins can be “voted” out of existence. 
Thankfully, quantum computing is unlikely to pose an immediate threat to Bitcoin. Estimates vary widely, but even aggressive projections place 
the earliest plausible risk window around 2030. Given this timeline, we expect little to no meaningful progress on the quantum issue in 2026. It 
remains a long-term governance challenge rather than an urgent engineering problem. 
What happens beyond that horizon is harder to predict. The biggest unresolved question is how the network will handle the dead coins that cannot 
migrate to a quantum-resistant address format. We do not know which path Bitcoin will ultimately take, but we are conﬁdent that the network will 
make whatever choices are necessary to preserve, and maximize, the monetary value of BTC. 
Both of the major options have a credible claim to that goal. The ﬁrst, doing nothing, upholds Bitcoin’s censorship resistance at the cost of 
introducing new supply that the market must absorb. The second, burning the coins, raises questions about Bitcoin’s censorship resistance, but 
prevents BTC from potentially falling into the hands of illicit actors. 
Regardless of which approach Bitcoin ultimately takes, the quantum issue represents a genuine long-term governance challenge. If quantum 
computing becomes a credible threat and Bitcoin fails to upgrade, BTC’s monetary status collapses. And if that failure were to occur, it would 
create a realistic opening for an alternative cryptomoney with a stronger quantum-resistance path to capture BTC’s ﬂeeting monetary premium. 
Lack of Programmability 
One limitation of the Bitcoin network is its lack of general-purpose programmability. Bitcoin is intentionally not Turing-complete; its scripting 
               
 


---

## Page 27

26 
language is limited by design, which restricts the complexity of onchain transaction logic. Unlike in other ecosystems where smart contracts can 
natively verify and enforce signature conditions, Bitcoin cannot yet validate external messages or coordinate trust-minimized bridging without 
oﬀchain infrastructure. Because of this, entire categories of applications, such as DEXs, onchain derivatives, and privacy tools, are nearly 
impossible to build on the Bitcoin L1. 
 
Although some proponents argue that this design reduces the attack surface and preserves Bitcoin’s monetary simplicity, there is undoubtedly a 
segment of BTC holders who want access to programmable environments. As of writing, 370,300 BTC (equivalent to $33.64 billion) have been 
bridged to other networks. Of this amount, 365,000 BTC (roughly 99% of all bridged BTC) is secured through custodial solutions or solutions that 
introduce trust-based assumptions. In other words, for BTC to be used productively in more expressive ecosystems, users have reintroduced the 
same set of risks that Bitcoin was originally designed to eliminate. 
Attempts to solve this problem within the Bitcoin ecosystem, through federated sidechains, early L2s, and trust-minimized multisigs, have not 
meaningfully reduced the reliance on meaningful trust assumptions. Users want to deploy BTC in expressive environments, but in the absence of a 
               
 


---

## Page 28

27 
trustless way to move BTC between chains, they default to centralized custodians. 
As BTC continues to grow and function more like a macro asset, demand for ways to use BTC productively will only increase. Whether it’s 
posting BTC as collateral, borrowing against it, swapping into other assets, or interacting with more expressive and programmable ﬁnancial 
systems, users naturally want to do more with their money than simply hold it. Under Bitcoin’s current design, these use cases introduce 
signiﬁcant long-tail risk as it is eﬀectively impossible to use BTC in a programmable or leveraged context without handing custody to a 
centralized intermediary. 
For these reasons, we believe that it will be necessary for the Bitcoin network to fork to enable these use cases to take place in a trustless 
and permissionless manner. While we don’t think this materializes through the Bitcoin network becoming a smart contract platform, we believe 
it makes the most sense to introduce an opcode, such as OP_CAT, that can enable trustless BTC bridging. 
OP_CAT is appealing because it represents a small consensus change that can unlock trustless BTC movement between chains. It is not a step 
toward turning Bitcoin into a smart-contract platform, but rather a simple opcode that, when combined with Taproot and existing Script primitives, 
allows Bitcoin to enforce spending conditions directly on the base layer. This capability would enable trustless BTC bridges without relying on 
custodians, federations, or external validator sets, addressing the core risk that has led to hundreds of thousands of BTC being held in custodial 
wrappers today. 
Unlike the quantum threat, Bitcoin’s lack of programmability is not an existential risk to BTC’s moneyness. What it does limit, however, is the 
total addressable market for BTC as cryptomoney. The demand for programmable money is already visible as more than 370,000 BTC (1.76% of 
total supply) sit in bridged environments, and over $120 billion in assets are deployed across DeFi. That demand is only going to increase as 
crypto proliferates and more and more ﬁnancial activity moves onchain. Bitcoin simply doesn’t oﬀer a trustless path to use BTC inside 
programmable ecosystems today, and if the market deems those risks unacceptable, then programmable L1 assets like ETH and SOL will be the 
ones to capture that demand. 
Security Budget 
The last structural concern for Bitcoin is its security budget. This issue has been discussed for over a decade, and while opinions vary widely on 
its severity, it remains one of the most contested long-term questions regarding Bitcoin’s monetary integrity. At its core, the security budget refers 
to the total amount of compensation miners receive for securing the network, which is currently a combination of block subsidies and transaction 
fees. As block rewards continue to halve every four years, Bitcoin must eventually rely primarily, and then solely, on fees to incentivize miners to 
protect the chain. 
               
 


---

## Page 29

28 
 
For a time, amidst the surging popularity of ordinals and runes, it appeared that perhaps transaction fees alone could potentially be enough to 
compensate miners to secure the chain. In April 2024, Bitcoin generated $281.4 million in chain fees, its second-highest monthly total ever. Now, 
more than a year and a half later, fee revenue has fallen oﬀ a cliﬀ. In fact, there was only $4.87 million in chain fees in November, the lowest 
monthly total since December 2019. 
Although the dropoﬀ in fees is startling, it may not be a cause for immediate concern. Bitcoin’s block subsidy still provides a meaningful incentive 
for miners, and this will remain true for the next few decades. Even as late as 2050, the network will emit roughly 50 BTC per week, a non-trivial 
amount of issuance for miners to compete for. As long as subsidies dominate miner revenue, the chain’s security is unlikely to be at risk. 
Nevertheless, it is becoming increasingly unlikely that chain fees will be able to replace current block subsidies entirely. 
That said, the security budget debate is more nuanced than simply asking whether fees will fully replace today’s block subsidies. Fees don’t need 
to equal the full subsidy; they only need to exceed the cost of a credible attack, an amount that is inherently unknowable and may change 
dramatically as mining technology and energy markets evolve. A future where mining costs fall signiﬁcantly would also lower the required fee 
ﬂoor. This could play out across a wide range of scenarios. In the moderate case, incremental ASIC improvements and cheaper access to stranded 
               
 


---

## Page 30

29 
renewables reduce miners’ marginal costs. In the extreme case, breakthroughs in energy production, such as commercial fusion or ultra-low-cost 
nuclear, could push electricity prices down by orders of magnitude, dramatically altering the economics of sustaining hashpower. 
Even acknowledging that there are too many unknown variables to calculate what Bitcoin’s security budget “needs” to be, it is still worth 
considering the hypothetical in which miner rewards eventually fall short of what is required to economically guarantee the network’s security. In 
that scenario, the incentives that underpin Bitcoin’s credible neutrality begin to break down, and the chain’s guarantees would rely increasingly on 
social expectations rather than enforceable economic constraints. 
One possibility is that certain actors, exchanges, custodians, nation-states, or large holders would mine at a loss to protect the asset they rely on. 
However, while defensive mining may preserve the chain, it may also weaken the social consensus on BTC’s moneyness. If users come to believe 
that BTC relies on coordinated support from large actors, its monetary neutrality, and therefore its monetary premium, could come under pressure. 
It’s also entirely possible that no entities step up to defend Bitcoin at an economic loss. In that scenario, Bitcoin could become susceptible to a 
51% attack. Although a 51% attack wouldn’t permanently destroy Bitcoin (Proof-of-Work chains such as Ethereum Classic and Monero have 
survived 51% attacks), it would unquestionably call Bitcoin’s safety into doubt. 
With so many unknowns shaping Bitcoin’s long-term security budget, no one can say with conﬁdence how the system will evolve decades from 
now. While this uncertainty poses no present risk to BTC, it does create a long-tail outcome the market must price. In that sense, the residual 
monetary premium on some L1s can be viewed as a hedge against the remote possibility that Bitcoin’s economic security becomes challenged 
over time. 
The ETH Debate: Is it Cryptomoney? 
Of all the major assets in crypto, none has sparked more sustained debate than ETH. BTC’s role as the dominant cryptomoney is broadly 
uncontested, but ETH’s role is anything but settled. To some, ETH is the only credible non-sovereign monetary asset besides BTC; to others, it 
represents a business with declining revenues, tightening margins, and relentless competition from faster, cheaper L1s. 
               
 


---

## Page 31

30 
 
This debate seemingly reached its climax in the ﬁrst half of this year. In March, XRP brieﬂy surpassed ETH in fully diluted valuation (Notably, 
ETH is fully circulating, whereas only ~60% of XRP’s supply is circulating). 
On March 16, ETH’s FDV stood at $227.65 billion, while XRP’s reached $239.23 billion, an outcome that virtually no one would have considered 
possible a year earlier. Then, on April 8, 2025, the ETH/BTC ratio fell below 0.02 for the ﬁrst time since February 2020. In other words, the 
entirety of ETH’s outperformance against BTC from the last cycle had fully unwound. By that point, sentiment around ETH had deteriorated to its 
lowest level in years. 
               
 


---

## Page 32

31 
 
To make matters worse, price action was only part of the story. Ethereum’s share of L1 fees has declined steadily as rival ecosystems strengthened. 
Solana reestablished itself in 2024, Hyperliquid broke out in 2025, and together they pushed Ethereum’s fee share down to 17%, fourth among L1s 
and a dramatic fall from the top spot it held just a year earlier. Fees aren’t everything, but they are a clear signal of where economic activity is 
migrating, and the competitive landscape Ethereum faces today is the ﬁercest in its history. 
               
 


---

## Page 33

32 
 
Yet history shows that crypto’s most signiﬁcant reversals often begin when sentiment is at its bleakest. By the time ETH was being written oﬀ as a 
failed asset, most of its perceived “failures” had already been priced in. 
In May 2025, the ﬁrst real signs emerged that the market had become too pessimistic. Both ETH/BTC and ETH’s USD price began to reverse 
sharply during this period. The ETH/BTC ratio climbed from a low of 0.017 in April to 0.042 in August, a 139% move, while ETH itself rose 
191% over the same stretch, from $1,646 to $4,793. That momentum ultimately culminated in a new all-time high on August 24, when ETH 
reached $4,946. 
Following this repricing, it became clear that ETH’s broader trajectory had shifted toward renewed strength. Leadership changes at the Ethereum 
Foundation (discussed later) and the arrival of ETH-focused Digital Asset Treasuries brought a degree of conviction that had been absent for much 
of the prior year. 
               
 


---

## Page 34

33 
 
Prior to this rally, the contrast between BTC and ETH was nowhere more visible than in their respective ETF markets. When spot ETH ETFs 
launched in July 2024, ﬂows were anemic. Over their ﬁrst six months, they pulled in just $2.41 billion, an underwhelming ﬁgure when set against 
BTC’s record-breaking ETF performance. 
However, concerns over ETF ﬂows completely reversed alongside ETH’s revival. Over the year, spot ETH ETFs pulled in $9.72 billion, compared 
to $21.78 billion for BTC. Given that BTC’s market cap is nearly ﬁve times larger, the diﬀerence in ﬂows, being only 2.2x, was far narrower than 
many expected. Put diﬀerently, once adjusted for market cap, ETH saw more ETF demand than BTC, a sharp reversal from the narrative that 
institutions had no real appetite for ETH. In some instances, ETH outpaced BTC outright. From May 26 to August 25, ETH ETFs pulled in 
$10.20 billion, more than BTC’s $9.79 billion over the same period, marking the ﬁrst time institutional demand clearly tilted toward ETH. 
               
 


---

## Page 35

34 
 
When viewed by ETF issuers, BlackRock extended its dominance in the ETF market, ﬁnishing 2025 with 3.7 million ETH, representing 60% of 
the total spot ETH ETF market share. That ﬁgure was up from 1.1 million ETH at the end of 2024, a 241% increase, outpacing all other issuers in 
yearly growth. Overall, spot ETH ETFs ended the year with 6.2 million ETH, or about 5% of the total ETH supply. 
               
 


---

## Page 36

35 
 
Underneath ETH’s sharp recovery, the most important development was the rise of ETH-focused Digital Asset Treasuries. DATs created a steady, 
recurring source of demand that ETH had never seen before, anchoring the asset in a way that narratives or speculative ﬂows could not. If ETH’s 
price action marked the visible turning point, DAT accumulation was the deeper structural shift that made it possible. 
The DATs had a signiﬁcant impact on the price of ETH, accumulating a total of 4.8 million ETH, 4% of the total ETH supply, over the course of 
2025. The most proliﬁc of the ETH DATs was Tom Lee’s Bitmine (BMNR), a former Bitcoin mining company that began converting its treasury 
and capital to ETH in July 2025. Between July and November, Bitmine purchased 3.63 million ETH, making it the clear leader in the DAT market 
share with 75% of all DAT holdings. 
As powerful as ETH’s reversal was, the rally eventually cooled. By November 30, ETH had pulled back from its August highs to $2,991, 
signiﬁcantly below even last cycle’s prior all-time high of $4,878. ETH is in a far stronger position than it was in April, but the recovery has not 
erased the structural concerns that sparked the bear case in the ﬁrst place. If anything, the ETH debate has returned stronger than ever. 
On one side, ETH is displaying many of the same markers BTC exhibited during its own ascent to monetary status. ETF inﬂows are no longer 
anemic. Digital Asset Treasuries have emerged as a persistent source of demand. And, perhaps most importantly, an increasingly visible share of 
               
 


---

## Page 37

36 
the market is treating ETH as something categorically diﬀerent from other L1 tokens, an asset that a subset of the market now treats as part of the 
same monetary framework as BTC. 
However, the concerns that dragged ETH down earlier this year have yet to be alleviated. Ethereum’s core fundamentals have not fully recovered. 
Its share of L1 fees continues to face pressure from credible competitors, such as Solana and Hyperliquid. Activity on the base layer remains far 
below prior-cycle peaks. And despite ETH’s sharp rebound, BTC still sits comfortably above its previous all-time highs while ETH remains below 
them. Even during ETH’s strongest months, a signiﬁcant portion of holders used the rally as exit liquidity rather than as conﬁrmation of a 
long-term monetary thesis. 
The fundamental question at the center of this debate is not whether Ethereum is valuable, but rather how ETH, the asset, accrues value 
from Ethereum. 
Last cycle, a common assumption was that ETH would directly accrue value from the success of Ethereum. This is a key part of the “Ultrasound 
Money” argument: Ethereum would be so useful that it would burn vast quantities of ETH, rendering the asset a deﬂationary source of value. 
 
Now, we think we can say with considerable conﬁdence that this will not be the case. Ethereum’s fees have plummeted with no recovery in sight, 
               
 


---

## Page 38

37 
and its largest sources of growth, RWAs and institutions, primarily use USD as the base monetary asset, not ETH. 
The value of ETH will now depend on how ETH indirectly accrues value from Ethereum’s success. But indirect accrual is far less certain. It rests 
on the hope that as Ethereum becomes more systemically important, more users and capital will choose to treat ETH as cryptomoney and a store 
of value. 
But unlike direct, mechanical value accrual, there is no guarantee this happens. It relies entirely on social preference and collective belief, which 
isn’t inherently a ﬂaw (There’s a whole section prior to this explaining how BTC accrues value in this way). But, it does mean that ETH’s 
appreciation is no longer tied to Ethereum’s economic activity in a deterministic way. 
 
All of this brings the ETH debate back to its core tension. ETH may, in fact, be accruing a monetary premium, but that premium has remained 
downstream from BTC’s. The market is once again treating ETH as a levered expression of BTC’s monetary thesis, not as an independent 
monetary asset. ETH’s 90-day rolling correlation with BTC has hovered between 0.7 and 0.9 throughout 2025, while its rolling beta surged to 
multi-year highs, at times exceeding 1.8. ETH is now moving more aggressively than BTC, but has remained dependent on BTC. 
               
 


---

## Page 39

38 
This is a subtle, but extremely important distinction. ETH’s monetary relevance today exists because BTC’s monetary narrative remains intact. As 
long as the market believes in BTC as a non-sovereign store of value, there will be a marginal cohort of market participants willing to extend that 
belief to ETH. And if BTC continues to strengthen in 2026, ETH has a simple path to make up even more ground. 
 
Ethereum DATs are still early in their life cycle and, to date, have primarily facilitated ETH accumulation through common equity issuance. In a 
renewed crypto bull market, however, these entities could explore additional capital formation strategies similar to those Strategy has used to 
expand its BTC exposure, including convertible notes and preferred stock. 
For example, a DAT such as BitMine could raise a mix of low-coupon convertible debt and higher-yield preferred capital, deploy the proceeds 
directly into ETH purchases, and stake that ETH to generate recurring yield. Under reasonable assumptions, staking income could partially oﬀset 
ﬁxed interest and dividend obligations, allowing the treasury to continue accumulating ETH while increasing balance sheet leverage during 
favorable market conditions. This potential “second life” of the Ethereum DATs could serve as an additional force sustaining ETH’s higher beta to 
BTC in 2026, assuming a broader BTC bull market resumes. 
               
 


---

## Page 40

39 
Ultimately, the market continues to price ETH’s monetary premium as contingent on BTC’s. ETH is not yet an autonomous monetary asset with 
an independent macro foundation; rather, it is a growing secondary beneﬁciary of BTC’s monetary consensus. Its recent resurgence reﬂects a 
marginal cohort of believers willing to treat ETH more like BTC and less like a typical L1 token. Still, even amidst relative strength, the market’s 
belief in ETH is inseparable from the continued strength of BTC’s own narrative. 
In short, ETH’s monetary story is no longer broken, but neither is it settled. Under the existing market structure and in light of ETH’s heightened 
beta relative to BTC, ETH can appreciate meaningfully if BTC’s thesis continues to play out, and structural demand from DATs and corporate 
treasuries gives it real upside in that scenario. But, ultimately, ETH’s monetary trajectory still depends on BTC for the foreseeable future. Until 
ETH exhibits lower correlation and beta to BTC, something it has never done on a longer time frame, ETH’s premium will ﬂuctuate in the shadow 
of BTC. 
Zcash: A Hedge Against BTC? 
Among all the cryptoassets outside BTC and ETH, ZEC’s monetary perception shifted the most signiﬁcantly in 2025. For years, ZEC sat outside 
the cryptomoney hierarchy, viewed as a niche privacy coin rather than a monetary asset. However, as concerns surrounding surveillance and the 
institutionalization of Bitcoin accelerated, privacy resurfaced as a core monetary property, rather than a niche ideological preference. 
Bitcoin has proven that non-sovereign digital money can function at a global scale, but it failed to preserve the privacy that we have become 
accustomed to using physical cash. Every transaction is broadcast on a transparent public ledger that anyone with a block explorer can monitor. It 
is a bitter irony that a tool designed to subvert the state inadvertently created a ﬁnancial panopticon. 
Zcash combines Bitcoin’s monetary policy with the privacy properties of physical cash via zero-knowledge cryptography. No digital asset can 
oﬀer the same battle-tested and deterministic privacy guarantees as the latest Zcash shielded pool, making it a valuable form of private money that 
cannot be easily replicated. In our view, the market re-rated ZEC relative to BTC to reﬂect its status as an aspirational private 
cryptomoney, positioning ZEC as a hedge against the rise of the surveillance state and the institutionalization of Bitcoin. 
               
 


---

## Page 41

40 
 
Year-to-date ZEC surged 666% against BTC, pushing its market cap to $7.0 billion and brieﬂy surpassing XMR as the most valuable privacy coin 
by market cap. This relative strength signals that ZEC is being priced as a viable form of private cryptomoney alongside XMR. 
Privacy on Bitcoin 
Bitcoin is very unlikely to adopt a shielded pool architecture, rendering the notion that it will eventually absorb Zcash’s value proposition 
unfounded. Bitcoin is known for its conservative culture, which prioritizes ossiﬁcation to reduce attack surfaces and maintain monetary integrity. 
Embedding privacy at the protocol level would necessitate changes to Bitcoin’s core architecture, introducing the risk of an inﬂation bug that 
could harm its monetary integrity. Zcash is willing to take this risk as privacy is its main value proposition. 
Implementing zero-knowledge cryptography at the base layer would also reduce the scalability of the blockchain as it would necessitate the use of 
nulliﬁers and hashed notes to prevent double-spending, which raises long-term concerns about “state bloat.” Nulliﬁers create an append-only “list” 
that grows indeﬁnitely, potentially resulting in a scenario where running a node would be resource-intensive. Forcing nodes to store a massive, 
ever-growing nulliﬁer set would harm Bitcoin’s decentralization, as it would increase the requirements to run a node on the network over time. 
               
 


---

## Page 42

41 
As mentioned earlier, absent a soft fork that enables ZK veriﬁcation, such as OP_CAT, no Bitcoin L2 can inherit Bitcoin’s security while 
delivering Zcash-level privacy. You either introduce trusted intermediaries (federations), accept long and interactive withdrawal delays (BitVM 
model), or push execution and security to a separate system entirely (sovereign rollups). Unless that changes, there is simply no existing path to 
achieve Bitcoin’s security with Zcash’s privacy, positioning ZEC as a valuable form of private cryptomoney. 
A Hedge Against CBDCs 
The urgency for privacy is compounded by the arrival of Central Bank Digital Currencies (CBDCs). Half the countries in the world are either 
investigating or have implemented a CBDC. CBDCs are programmable, meaning issuers can not only track every transaction but also control how, 
when, and where funds are spent. Funds could be programmed to work only at approved merchants or within speciﬁc geographies. 
 
While this may sound like a dystopian fantasy, the weaponization of the banking system is grounded in reality: 
● Nigeria (2020): During the #EndSARS protests against police brutality, the Central Bank of Nigeria froze the bank accounts of prominent 
protest organizers and feminist groups, forcing the movement to rely on crypto to sustain itself. 
               
 


---

## Page 43

42 
● United States of America (2020-2025): Regulators and the largest banks have debanked lawful but politically disfavored sectors on the 
basis of “reputation risk” and ideology rather than safety and soundness, a pattern serious enough that the White House ordered a review 
and the OCC’s 2025 debanking study documented systematic restrictions on legal industries ranging from oil and gas to ﬁrearms, adult 
content, and crypto. 
● Canada (2022): During the Freedom Convoy protests, the Canadian government invoked the Emergencies Act to freeze the bank and 
crypto accounts of protesters and even small-dollar donors without a court order. The Royal Canadian Mounted Police even blacklisted 34 
self-custody crypto wallet addresses, ordering all regulated exchanges to cease facilitating transactions with them. This proved that Western 
democracies are willing to weaponize the ﬁnancial system against political dissent. 
In an era where money can be programmed to control you, ZEC oﬀers a deﬁnitive opt-out. However, Zcash isn't just for escaping CBDCs; it is 
becoming increasingly necessary to protect Bitcoin itself. 
A Hedge Against Bitcoin's Capture 
As inﬂuential advocates like Naval Ravikant and Balaji Srinivasan have argued, Zcash is an insurance policy for maintaining Bitcoin’s vision of 
ﬁnancial freedom. 
Bitcoin is rapidly consolidating under centralized entities. Between centralized exchanges (3.0 million BTC), ETFs (1.3 million BTC), and 
publicly traded companies (829,192 BTC), approximately 5.1 million BTC (24% of total supply) is currently held by third parties. 
               
 


---

## Page 44

43 
 
This concentration implies that 24% of the total BTC supply is vulnerable to regulatory seizure, mirroring the centralized conditions that enabled 
the U.S. Government’s conﬁscation of gold in 1933. Executive Order 6102 legally forced U.S. citizens to surrender all gold holdings above $100 
to the Federal Reserve. In exchange, they received paper currency at the oﬃcial rate of $20.67 per troy ounce. It was enforced through banking 
choke points rather than physical force. 
For Bitcoin, the mechanism would be identical. Regulators do not need your private keys to seize 24% of the supply; they only need legal 
authority over the custodians. In this scenario, the government issues an enforcement order to entities like BlackRock and Coinbase. Legally 
bound to comply, these companies would be forced to freeze and transfer the BTC they hold. Overnight, nearly a quarter of the BTC supply could 
be eﬀectively nationalized without a single line of code being broken. While this is certainly an edge case, it cannot be entirely ruled out. 
Given the blockchain’s transparency, it also means that self-custody is no longer a viable standalone defense. Any BTC withdrawn from a KYC’d 
exchange or brokerage account is susceptible to seizure, as a “paper trail” eventually leads the government to the coin’s ﬁnal destination. 
BTC holders can swap into Zcash to sever the chain of custody, “air-gapping” their wealth from surveillance. Once funds enter the shielded pool, 
the destination address becomes a cryptographic “black hole” to observers. Regulators can track funds leaving the Bitcoin network, but they 
               
 


---

## Page 45

44 
cannot see where they ultimately end up, rendering the assets invisible to state actors. While oﬀ-ramping to domestic banks remains a bottleneck, 
the assets themselves are censorship-resistant and diﬃcult to trace proactively. The strength of this anonymity depends entirely on operational 
security; reusing addresses or acquiring funds via KYC’d exchanges creates a permanent link before shielding ever occurs. 
Paving the Road to PMF 
The demand for private money has always existed; Zcash simply struggled to meet users where they were. For years, the protocol was burdened 
by intensive memory requirements, lengthy proving times, and cumbersome desktop setups, which made shielded transactions slow and 
intimidating for most users. A recent convergence of infrastructure breakthroughs has systematically dismantled these barriers, priming it for user 
adoption. 
 
The Sapling upgrade reduced memory requirements by 97% (to ~40 MB) and cut proving times by 81% (to ~7 seconds), paving the way for 
shielded transactions on mobile devices. 
               
 


---

## Page 46

45 
 
 
While Sapling solved for speed, trusted setups remained a concern within the privacy community. By implementing Halo 2, Orchard eliminated 
Zcash’s reliance on a trusted setup, making it fully trustless. It also introduced Uniﬁed Addresses, which bundle transparent and shielded pools 
into a single destination, removing the need for users to manually select between address types. 
These architectural improvements culminated in the release of Zashi, a mobile wallet developed by the Electric Coin Company in March 2024. 
Leveraging the abstraction of Uniﬁed Addresses, Zashi reduced the friction of shielded transactions to a few taps on a screen, making privacy the 
default user experience (UX). 
With the UX hurdle cleared, distribution remained an issue. Users still relied on CEXs to deposit and withdraw ZEC into their wallets. The 
               
 


---

## Page 47

46 
integration of NEAR Intents removed this dependency. NEAR Intents enable Zashi users to swap supported assets (e.g., BTC, ETH) directly into 
shielded ZEC without ever interacting with a CEX. NEAR Intents also allow users to pay any address across 20 blockchains in any supported 
asset while funding the transaction with shielded ZEC. 
Together, these initiatives allowed Zcash to bypass historical frictions and access global liquidity, meeting the market exactly where it is. 
Looking Ahead 
 
Since 2019, the rolling correlation between ZEC and BTC has been in a deﬁnitive downtrend, declining from highs of 0.90 to recent lows of 0.24. 
ZEC’s rolling beta to BTC has risen to all-time highs, meaning ZEC now ampliﬁes BTC’s price action even as their rolling correlation has fallen. 
This divergence signals that the market is beginning to assign an idiosyncratic premium to Zcash’s privacy guarantees. Looking forward, we 
expect ZEC’s performance to be deﬁned by this "privacy premium," the value the market ascribes to ﬁnancial anonymity in an era of increasing 
surveillance and the weaponization of the global ﬁnancial system. 
               
 


---

## Page 48

47 
We believe it is extremely unlikely that ZEC overtakes BTC. Bitcoin’s transparent supply and unassailable auditability have cemented it as the 
soundest form of cryptomoney. Zcash, by contrast, will continue to carry the inherent trade-oﬀs of a privacy coin. By encrypting the ledger to 
preserve privacy, it sacriﬁces auditability and introduces the theoretical risk of inﬂation bugs, undetected supply inﬂation within the shielded pool, 
that Bitcoin's transparent ledger explicitly eliminates. 
Regardless, ZEC can carve out its own niche independent of BTC. The two assets are not competing to solve the same problem, but rather address 
diﬀerent use cases within cryptomoney. BTC is positioned as sound cryptomoney optimized for transparency and security, while ZEC serves as 
private cryptomoney, optimized for conﬁdentiality and ﬁnancial privacy. In that sense, ZEC’s success does not require displacing BTC, but rather 
complementing it by oﬀering properties Bitcoin deliberately does not. 
Application Money Emerges in 2026 
One trend that we expect to continue accelerating in 2026 is the viability of application money. Application money is a monetary asset designed 
speciﬁcally to serve a singular purpose within an application, rather than being a general-purpose monetary asset such as BTC or L1 tokens. To 
understand why application money becomes viable in crypto environments, we must ﬁrst revisit two foundational principles about money itself: 
● Money’s value is derived from the goods and services it can be exchanged for. 
● Participants converge on shared monetary standards when the costs of switching between monetary systems are high. 
Historically, money emerged as a mechanism to store value created through economic activity. Because most goods and services are perishable or 
time-bound, money allows participants to preserve purchasing power and exchange it later. Importantly, money is not inherently valuable; its value 
is a reﬂection of what it can purchase within a given economic context. 
As economies expand and interact, multiple monetary systems follow a power law and tend to collapse into a single dominant standard. This 
convergence is driven by high frictions associated with switching between monetary systems, including slow settlement, limited liquidity, 
information asymmetry, intermediaries, and the physical diﬃculty of moving money across distances. Under these conditions, monetary network 
eﬀects reinforce the dominance of the most widely accepted currency, making independent monetary standards increasingly ineﬃcient. 
Crypto fundamentally alters these dynamics by dramatically reducing the aforementioned frictions. In these low-friction environments, smaller, 
application-speciﬁc monetary systems become viable, as users can seamlessly enter and exit them without incurring prohibitive costs. As a result, 
applications no longer need to rely on general-purpose monetary systems. Instead, they can create application-speciﬁc monetary systems 
that allow them to internalize and retain the value generated by their own economic activity. 
2025 provided multiple working proofs of concept for application-level monetary systems, most notably Virtuals and Zora. 
 
               
 


---

## Page 49

48 
Virtuals 
 
Virtuals is one of the ﬁrst examples of a crypto application, not a blockchain, successfully implementing its own monetary system. The platform 
allows users to create, deploy, and monetize AI agents without any technical expertise. Each AI agent launched on Virtuals issues a corresponding 
token, which is paired with the VIRTUAL token for liquidity. In this structure, VIRTUAL functions as the base monetary asset of the Virtuals 
ecosystem. Whenever a user acquires an agent’s token, VIRTUAL is deposited into that agent’s liquidity pool. Under this structure, the value of 
VIRTUAL is tied to the economic activity taking place on the platform; the more valuable and widely used the AI agents become, the stronger the 
underlying demand for VIRTUAL. In this sense, VIRTUAL operates as money as it is the unit through which all agent-level value circulates 
within the Virtuals economy. 
Zora 
               
 


---

## Page 50

49 
Zora is another example of a crypto application implementing an application money structure. Zora is a ﬁnancialized social media platform where 
both proﬁles and posts are tokenized. When a user creates a proﬁle, a corresponding creator coin is created. Every post they publish issues its own 
token, known as a content coin. Zora extends the quote pairing model popularized by Virtuals by pairing all creator coins with the ZORA token, 
and pairing each creator’s content coins with that creator coin. 
 
Zora also improves upon the application money model through its own integrated wallet, which completely abstracts away the monetary layer 
from the end user. Users can denominate in whichever asset they prefer, such as ETH or USDC, when purchasing creator or content coins, and the 
underlying swap from ETH/USDC → ZORA → creator coin → content coin is abstracted from the user experience. Furthermore, users can swap 
directly from one creator’s content coin to another creator’s content coin, further streamlining the ﬂow of assets. 
Application Money Design Choices 
There are two design patterns that enable application money models to function reliably. 
               
 


---

## Page 51

50 
First, automated market maker (AMM) liquidity pools enable continuous liquidity and enforce economic relationships by algorithmically setting 
asset prices and trade execution rules through smart contracts. In Virtuals, every agent token is paired against VIRTUAL; in Zora, every creator 
coin is paired against ZORA, and every content coin is paired against the creator coin. This structure binds the value of the application money to 
the activity the application is trying to cultivate. 
Second, successful application money systems abstract the monetary layer away from the user. Users do not want to change their monetary 
preferences for each application. Application money systems work best when they remove this burden entirely. Users can denominate in 
whichever asset they prefer, and only when they engage with the application is that asset converted into the application’s monetary asset, 
completely abstracted away from the user. 
When Application Money Makes Sense 
Just because crypto makes application money viable does not mean every application should adopt an application money model. Application 
money works best when network eﬀects amplify the value of the underlying application. In these cases, the monetary layer helps ensure that the 
value created on the application is captured within the ecosystem itself. 
For example, on Zora, when high-proﬁle users join, they bring attention, liquidity, and new onchain actions that ﬂow back into demand for ZORA. 
Since the liquidity of all creator coins is paired with ZORA, increases in ZORA’s price mechanically put upward pressure on creator-coin prices. 
               
 


---

## Page 52

51 
 
We can observe this eﬀect directly. On November 12, Uniswap founder Hayden Adams created a Zora account, thereby launching his creator coin. 
Right before the launch, ZORA traded at $0.055. In the hours following the launch, ZORA rose to a local high of $0.071, and eight hours after 
Hayden Adam’s launch, ZORA remained 23% higher than it was twelve hours prior. This price increase was also observed in creator coins on 
Zora. For example, DOCKER and COINAGE, two popular creator coins on Zora, rose 35% and 7% in the same twelve-hour period, respectively. 
For Zora to function eﬀectively as a social network, it must operate in this way. All else equal, an account on a network with one million 
participants is more valuable than an account on a network with one hundred participants. As participation increases, the value of the network 
rises, and the value of each individual account increases alongside it. By denominating activity in ZORA rather than a general-purpose asset like 
USDC, these network-driven pricing dynamics are internalized. If USDC were the base monetary asset, this value capture would not occur. 
Purchasing Hayden Adams’s creator coin would generate no direct demand for ZORA, nor would it meaningfully translate into demand for 
related assets such as DOCKER or COINAGE. As a result, ZORA serves as the mechanism through which Zora captures and compounds the 
value created by growing social activity on the network. 
               
 


---

## Page 53

52 
More Projects will Experiment with Application Money in 2026 
Early examples such as Virtuals and Zora show that, in low-friction crypto environments, applications can internalize economic activity rather 
than externalize it to general-purpose monetary assets. As tooling improves and these models become better understood, application money is 
likely to be increasingly viewed as a viable design choice for applications with strong native network eﬀects. 
For applications, the motivation is straightforward. Application money allows projects to directly capture the value generated by the activity they 
are trying to cultivate, whether that activity is social interaction, content creation, or coordination. In applications where participation compounds 
over time, relying on a neutral settlement asset can dilute these dynamics, whereas an application-speciﬁc monetary layer reinforces them. 
Just because application money can work, however, does not mean it is appropriate for every application. The model is most eﬀective in contexts 
where user growth ampliﬁes the value of each participant, rather than merely increasing aggregate activity. In these environments, an 
application-speciﬁc monetary layer can meaningfully reinforce the dynamics the product is designed to cultivate. Where this condition does not 
hold, introducing application money risks adding complexity without materially improving value capture. As an example, in ﬁnancially oriented 
protocols such as lending platforms or perpetual DEXs, user growth does not increase the value of individual positions in the same way. In those 
cases, introducing application money adds complexity without creating meaningful economic beneﬁt. 
Even for applications that do adopt application money, there are trade-oﬀs. Application money is likely to be more volatile than more established 
forms of money such as stablecoins, BTC, or even L1 tokens. If an application’s users are sensitive to volatility, an application money model could 
have the opposite of its intended eﬀect on user growth. Additionally, even though crypto signiﬁcantly reduces swapping costs, it is not costless. 
Slippage and fees can still represent meaningful costs, and if an application’s users are highly cost-sensitive, the application may need to 
reconsider whether application money is preferable to a general-purpose monetary asset. 
Finally, broader adoption of application money would have downstream implications for L1 value accrual. To the extent that economic activity is 
increasingly denominated in application-speciﬁc assets, a smaller share of value would ﬂow directly into demand for L1 tokens. In this scenario, 
L1s would continue to accrue value through fees, security, and execution, but applications would be less reliant on the base asset as a monetary 
standard. Over time, this dynamic could place pressure on L1 monetary premiums, particularly for chains whose value proposition is closely tied 
to their role as cryptomoney. 
A Parting Note on Stablecoins 
Finally, as we wrap up this section on cryptomoney, we want to acknowledge stablecoins. In the next section, we’ll explore how TradFi and crypto 
are increasingly converging, with stablecoins playing a central role in that process. 
This convergence is undoubtedly a positive development, and arguably one of the industry’s most important successes in 2025. Stablecoins have 
expanded access, improved eﬃciency, and delivered real-world utility to millions of people. That progress should not be minimized, but it is also 
worth remembering that this is not all crypto has to oﬀer the world. 
               
 


---

## Page 54

53 
Crypto’s original ambition was not simply to make today’s ﬁnancial infrastructure work better, but to make entirely new monetary systems 
possible. Stablecoins are a powerful tool within that broader vision, but they are not its endgame. As the industry continues to mature, the risk is 
not that stablecoins succeed, but that their success overshadows cryptomoney, the foundational innovation at the heart of crypto itself. 
The Convergence of TradFi x Crypto 
Authors: Qorban Ferrell, Jake Koch-Gallup 
Key Themes 
● The GENIUS Act recast stablecoins from crypto-native trading tools into instruments of U.S. monetary policy and unleashed competition 
across banks, ﬁntechs, and tech giants to control the rails for digital dollars. 
● Tether's $500 billion valuation reﬂects extraordinary proﬁtability, but the GENIUS Act invited JPMorgan and Google into the market to 
compete. We expect Tether to retain its dominance in dollarizing economies where compliance is less of a concern, while branded 
incumbents capture the developed world. 
● While banks retroﬁt stablecoins into existing payment ﬂows, Cloudﬂare and Google are building for agentic commerce that barely exists 
yet. We expect the merger of tech, ﬁnance, and AI to dominate 2026 as agent-driven transactions scale these rails. 
● Falling interest rates will direct capital toward crypto-native yields, including funding spreads, token arbitrage, and GPU-backed lending. 
The returns from real cash ﬂows, rather than token inﬂation, will support a sturdier yield cycle. 
● Tokenized RWAs reached $18 billion in 2025, concentrated in Treasuries and credit, where product-market ﬁt emerged ﬁrst. DTCC's SEC 
authorization to tokenize U.S. securities will expand this footprint and route trillions onto crypto rails. 
Welcome to the Big Leagues 
From the beginning, the crypto movement has faced an unrelenting uphill battle for legitimacy and adoption. The resistance was inevitable given 
that crypto represents a direct challenge to the state's monopoly over money. That threat was easy to dismiss when Bitcoin was small, but once it 
grew, ignoring it was no longer an option. 
Once it became impossible to ignore, every major expansion of the cryptoeconomy provoked an aggressive backlash from policymakers. In June 
2011, U.S. Senators Chuck Schumer and Joe Manchin publicly urged federal authorities to crack down on Bitcoin after media coverage of Silk 
Road, the anonymous marketplace that had become Bitcoin’s ﬁrst major use case. Law enforcement agencies and policy reports soon cast crypto 
chieﬂy as a conduit for cybercrime and money laundering. By December 2013, the People’s Bank of China barred ﬁnancial institutions from 
handling it, which triggered the ﬁrst of many price crashes driven by regulatory fear. This early cycle of scrutiny and restriction established a 
               
 


---

## Page 55

54
