pattern in which Bitcoin and the state consistently appeared as opposing interests rather than natural partners. 
Under Siege 
Alongside regulatory crackdowns, each major crypto cycle had been met with scorn from the most powerful ﬁgures in traditional ﬁnance. In 
September 2017, JPMorgan Chase CEO Jamie Dimon delivered one of the most notorious condemnations of Bitcoin, calling it “a fraud… worse 
than tulip bulbs. It won’t end well. Someone is going to get killed,” and even threatening to ﬁre any employee trading it “in a second” because 
“they are stupid.” That same year, BlackRock’s Larry Fink dismissed Bitcoin as an “index of money laundering.” In May 2018, Warren Buﬀett 
labeled Bitcoin “probably rat poison squared,” while his longtime partner Charlie Munger described those within the crypto industry as having 
“dementia.” Janet Yellen characterized Bitcoin as “highly speculative” and often used for “illicit transactions.” By 2022, skepticism had hardened 
into outright dismissal at the highest levels of central banking, with ECB President Christine Lagarde stating bluntly, “My very humble 
assessment is that it is worth nothing.” 
 
Hostility toward crypto peaked during the 2022–2024 campaign “Operation Choke Point 2.0,” a label Nic Carter coined for regulators’ 
               
 


---

## Page 56

55 
coordinated push to marginalize the industry. Between late 2021 and early 2023, the OCC’s Interpretive Letter 1179, the FDIC’s FIL‑16‑2022, and 
the Federal Reserve’s SR 22‑6 all created a framework that forced banks to seek supervisory “non‑objection” and give advance notice before 
engaging in crypto activities, while a January 2023 joint statement from all three agencies warned that crypto‑concentrated business models posed 
“signiﬁcant safety and soundness concerns.” The extent of this strategy only became clear in December 2024, when Coinbase obtained internal 
FDIC emails via FOIA showing at least 23 “pause letters” instructing banks to “pause all crypto asset‑related activity.” Coinbase’s chief legal 
oﬃcer concluded that this was “no conspiracy theory” but a concerted plan to deny banking services to a lawful American industry. 
The consequences of this coordinated eﬀort were immediate. Silvergate Bank announced voluntary liquidation in March 2023, citing mounting 
supervisory pressure. Days later, Signature Bank was seized, and board member Barney Frank alleged it was done “to send a very strong 
anti‑crypto message,” with the FDIC excluding $4 billion in crypto deposits from the sale. Smaller ﬁrms, such as Anchorage Digital, lost banking 
relationships and laid oﬀ staﬀ, and founders like Frax Finance’s Sam Kazemian were told by JPMorgan that their accounts would be closed if 
crypto was their primary source of income. For the ﬁrst time, the full weight of the U.S. banking‑regulatory apparatus had been directed at cutting 
crypto oﬀ from the ﬁnancial system. 
In parallel with the banking regulators' eﬀorts to cut crypto oﬀ from the ﬁnancial system, the SEC, under Chair Gary Gensler, waged a frontal 
assault from the securities side through what critics called “regulation by enforcement.” Sworn in on April 17, 2021, Gensler became the 
industry’s primary antagonist, insisting that existing securities laws already covered most crypto assets while declining to issue new, tailored rules. 
Instead, his SEC ramped up enforcement by bringing over 125 crypto-related cases (about 80% more than his predecessor) and extracting over $6 
billion in penalties, including a record haul in 2024 alone. High‑proﬁle lawsuits against Ripple, Binance, Coinbase, and Kraken created pervasive 
uncertainty about which tokens or business models could legally operate in the U.S., with outcomes that shut down products like Kraken’s U.S. 
staking service and branded major exchange tokens as unregistered securities. A wave of Wells notices to ﬁrms like Paxos, Uniswap Labs, 
Robinhood Crypto, Consensys/MetaMask, and OpenSea sent the message that even companies attempting to engage with regulators in good faith 
could be targeted. 
               
 


---

## Page 57

56 
 
Faced with this onslaught, major U.S. players began looking abroad. Coinbase launched an international exchange in Bermuda, Gemini expanded 
in Singapore, and CEOs openly discussed relocating if necessary, while Senator Cynthia Lummis warned that Congress’s failure to pass clear 
rules was “pushing the industry to other countries.” Combined with Operation Choke Point 2.0 on the banking front, Gensler’s SEC persuaded 
many industry builders that the United States had become a legal mineﬁeld for crypto businesses. 
2025 brings a full regulatory reversal 
After years of debanking, “regulation by enforcement,” and open hostility from regulators and Wall Street, the backlash ﬁnally boiled over in the 
2024 election cycle. The Biden administration’s hardline approach had alienated a sector with tens of millions of U.S. users and thousands of 
companies. That alienation created a clear political opening for anyone willing to run as a pro-crypto candidate. The industry responded by 
mobilizing at a scale never seen before. Firms like Coinbase and Ripple invested $119 million in federal races, making crypto the second-largest 
source of corporate election spending and turning super PACs, such as Fairshake, into kingmakers for pro-crypto candidates. 
               
 


---

## Page 58

57 
 
At the same time, politicians realized there were votes to be won by siding with an industry that felt under siege. Donald Trump leaned into that 
anger as he branded himself a “Crypto President” and promised to reverse the prior administration’s crackdown. Meanwhile, ﬁgures like Robert F. 
Kennedy Jr. ﬂoated pro-Bitcoin proposals as part of a broader populist economic message. By the time Trump took oﬃce in 2025, years of 
punitive policy had failed to kill crypto. Instead, those policies helped forge a well-funded political constituency that handed his administration 
both the mandate and the incentive to execute a complete reversal. 
The reversal came with startling speed as campaign promises on crypto were almost immediately translated into SEC policy. After years of 
Gensler-era “regulation by enforcement,” he announced his departure on Nov. 21, 2024, eﬀective at noon on Jan. 20, 2025, precisely as Trump 
was sworn in. The very next day, Commissioner Mark Uyeda took over as Acting Chair and wasted no time signaling a break with the past, 
publicly calling Gensler’s approach “a disaster for the entire industry.” 
On January 21, Uyeda’s ﬁrst act was to launch an SEC Crypto Task Force led by Commissioner Hester Peirce (“Crypto Mom”), with a mandate to 
deﬁne when digital assets are or are not securities, create clear registration pathways for token projects and exchanges, and explore retroactive 
relief for prior token oﬀerings. That same day, the SEC issued Staﬀ Accounting Bulletin 122, rescinding SAB 121’s requirement that custodians 
               
 


---

## Page 59

58 
book client crypto as liabilities on their own balance sheets, which was an accounting trick that had eﬀectively blocked banks from oﬀering 
large‑scale custody. With Paul Atkins conﬁrmed 52–44 on April 9 and sworn in as the 34th SEC Chair on April 21, the personnel and policy 
architecture of the Gensler era had been dismantled in a matter of months. 
Uyeda’s swift policy actions initiated the dismantling of the Gensler-era regime and the “Choke Point 2.0” debanking apparatus targeting crypto 
ﬁrms. On Jan. 23, 2025, Trump signed the executive order "Strengthening American Leadership in Digital Financial Technology," establishing the 
President's Working Group on Digital Asset Markets (chaired by AI and Crypto Czar David Sacks), banning CBDCs, protecting crypto ﬁrms' 
banking access, revoking Biden's digital assets order, and mandating regulatory recommendations. Within months, banking regulators withdrew 
restrictive guidance, the SEC dropped enforcement against Coinbase, Binance, Kraken, Ripple, and others, and the Justice Department dissolved 
its National Crypto Enforcement Team. By April 2025, the anti-crypto regulatory framework was dismantled. 
With regulatory hostility removed, the crypto industry gained newfound legitimacy. On March 6, 2025, Trump signed an executive order 
establishing the Strategic Bitcoin Reserve, directing the U.S government to retain approximately 200,000 BTC previously seized through criminal 
forfeitures. Legislative milestones soon followed, delivering the ﬁrst comprehensive federal framework for crypto. The GENIUS Act, signed into 
law in July 2025, requires stablecoins to maintain 100% reserve backing, provide monthly disclosures, and comply with anti-money laundering 
rules, all while clarifying that payment stablecoins are neither securities nor commodities. Meanwhile, the CLARITY Act, having passed the 
House, aims to assign regulatory authority between the SEC and CFTC based on asset classiﬁcation and is awaiting Senate approval. Further 
reinforcing Bitcoin’s strategic role, the BITCOIN Act, reintroduced in March 2025, proposes purchasing one million BTC over ﬁve years with a 
mandatory 20-year holding period, building directly on Trump’s executive order. 
               
 


---

## Page 60

59 
 
No Crying in the Casino 
The regulatory relief, however, came with a price. Just days before taking oﬃce, Donald Trump launched his own memecoin (TRUMP), with 
entities he controlled holding most of the supply. The token brieﬂy soared to a $15 billion market cap before collapsing by over 90%, leaving 
hundreds of thousands of investors deep in losses, while Trump-linked entities reportedly captured hundreds of millions in sales and fees. In 
parallel, the family’s DeFi venture, World Liberty Financial (WLFI), raised more than half a billion dollars through token sales, with the bulk of 
the funds ﬂowing to Trump-aligned entities, alongside a large personal allocation of WLFI tokens. 
               
 


---

## Page 61

60 
 
World Liberty Financial’s USD1 stablecoin soon reached a multi‑billion‑dollar market cap, supported by a $2 billion investment from Abu 
               
 


---

## Page 62

61 
Dhabi’s MGX shortly before the White House approved key U.S. chip exports to the UAE. Critics across the political spectrum seized on the 
overlap between policy and proﬁt, with Representative Jamie Raskin calling the network “the world’s most corrupt crypto startup operation.” 
The crypto industry achieved what it had long sought: regulatory clarity, bank access, and peace of mind with Washington. But it also became 
entangled in presidential business interests on a scale that would have been unthinkable a year earlier. 
Using crypto rails to entrench dollar supremacy 
Whatever one makes of the politics, the regulatory clarity of 2025 has unleashed a wave of institutional building that eclipses anything the industry 
managed under regulatory siege. And at the center of that construction sits stablecoins. Once dismissed as mere trading instruments for 
speculating on volatile tokens, dollar-pegged stablecoins have become the most consequential application of crypto: the bridge between the 
cryptoeconomy and legacy ﬁnance, and increasingly, a tool for projecting American monetary power abroad. 
 
With over $300 billion in circulation and $26 trillion in annual transaction volume, stablecoins now underpin both DeFi and the emerging 
               
 


---

## Page 63

62 
architecture of global dollar dominance. Passed in July 2025, the GENIUS Act made stablecoins part of the nation’s ﬁnancial infrastructure and 
sparked a land grab among banks, ﬁntechs, and tech companies to control the pipes for digital dollars. With regulatory clarity now in place, we 
anticipate that this competition will accelerate stablecoin adoption. 
The GENIUS Act (Guiding and Establishing National Innovation for U.S. Stablecoins) establishes the ﬁrst federal regulatory framework for 
payment stablecoins. The legislation passed with bipartisan support (68–30 in the Senate and 308–122 in the House), a rare consensus that 
stablecoins serve American strategic interests. The Act requires issuers to maintain one-to-one reserve backing in permitted assets such as U.S. 
Treasuries, demand deposits at insured institutions, Federal Reserve balances, and government money market funds. Issuers with more than $50 
billion in circulation must provide monthly public disclosures, executive certiﬁcations, and independent audits, with transparency standards that 
surpass those applied to traditional banks. 
 
Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent views stablecoins as a ﬁscal tool to reinforce the dollar’s position and boost demand for U.S. debt. At the 
Treasury Market Conference in November 2025, Bessent declared that stablecoins "will buttress the dollar's status as the global reserve currency" 
while driving "a surge in demand for U.S. Treasuries." He projects a $3.7 trillion stablecoin market by 2030, which would generate over $1 trillion 
               
 


---

## Page 64

63 
in new structural demand for U.S. government debt. Tether already holds $135 billion in Treasuries as the relationship between stablecoin issuers 
and American ﬁscal interests has become structural. In April 2025, Fed Chair Jerome Powell endorsed the framework, saying stablecoins "could 
actually have fairly wide appeal and should contain consumer protections." 
 
Stablecoins also serve as a vehicle for maintaining dollar dominance abroad. Dollar-pegged stablecoins account for 99% of the global supply, and 
               
 


---

## Page 65

64 
approximately 90% of USDT transactions occur outside the United States. In Sub-Saharan Africa, stablecoins account for 80% of all crypto 
transaction volume. In Turkey, stablecoin transactions account for 4.3% of the country's GDP. This amounts to de facto dollarization through 
private infrastructure, a phenomenon the European Parliament has described as "cryptomercantilism." This trend will persist as global liquidity 
strains drive capital irresistibly toward dollar assets, with stablecoins serving as the programmable infrastructure that ampliﬁes U.S. demand 
worldwide. 
 
Tether's treasury eclipses nation-states 
Tether is now the world’s 18th-largest holder of U.S. Treasuries, a position that places it alongside sovereign investors in the global dollar market. 
The company's Q3 2025 attestation revealed $135 billion in treasury holdings, which exceeds the holdings of Germany, Saudi Arabia, and Israel. 
               
 


---

## Page 66

65 
 
In 2025, USDT's market cap reached $184 billion and represents 60% of the stablecoin market. The company's ﬁnancial performance deﬁes 
traditional ﬁntech comparisons. Year-to-date proﬁts through Q3 2025 exceeded $10 billion, with a projected 2025 net income of approximately 
$15 billion at a 99% proﬁt margin. This proﬁtability derives almost entirely from reserve income on U.S. government securities. Tether eﬀectively 
operates as a $180 billion money market fund with crypto distribution rails. 
Tether is now seeking a private placement of $15-20 billion in exchange for approximately 3% equity, which implies a $500 billion valuation that 
would rank it among SpaceX and OpenAI as the world's most valuable private companies. Cantor Fitzgerald, which already owns approximately 
5% of Tether, is advising the transaction. 
Despite regulatory exclusion from the EU market under MiCA, Tether's dominance in emerging markets remains unassailable. The company 
relocated its headquarters to El Salvador in January 2025 and has hired Bo Hines, a former White House oﬃcial, to lead U.S. market re-entry 
through a new GENIUS Act-compliant stablecoin called USAT, to be issued through Anchorage Digital with Cantor Fitzgerald as reserve 
custodian. 
               
 


---

## Page 67

66 
 
Tether's position looks unassailable today, but it may not be by the end of 2026. The same proﬁtability that commands a $500 billion valuation has 
drawn the attention of institutions with far larger balance sheets and distribution networks. The GENIUS Act removed the regulatory uncertainty 
that kept them on the sidelines. Now JPMorgan, PayPal, Visa, and Google are all entering the stablecoin market. Tether will likely keep its 
dominance in the markets where it matters most, the dollarizing economies where regulatory status is irrelevant. But in the developed world, 
branded incumbents will erode Tether’s market share through superior compliance and integration. 
               
 


---

## Page 68

67 
 
Banks mobilize for the stablecoin era 
JPMorgan has operated a crypto-based settlement infrastructure since 2019, now branded as Kinexys Digital Payments. The platform has 
processed over $1.5 trillion cumulatively and handles approximately $2 billion daily in cross-border transactions for institutional clients, including 
Siemens, BlackRock, and Ant International. In June 2025, the bank launched JPMD (JPMorgan Deposit Token), which CEO Jamie Dimon 
described as "eﬀectively the same thing" as a stablecoin. Dimon stated in July 2025: "We're going to be involved in both JPMorgan Deposit coin 
and stablecoins to understand it, to be good at it." 
Bank of America CEO Brian Moynihan conﬁrmed in February 2025 that the bank will issue a stablecoin when regulatory clarity permits: "It's 
pretty clear there's going to be a stablecoin, which is going to be fully dollar-backed... It's no diﬀerent than a bank account... If they make that 
legal, we will go into that business." Citigroup CEO Jane Fraser conﬁrmed in July 2025 that Citi is "looking at the issuance of a Citi stablecoin," 
               
 


---

## Page 69

68 
while emphasizing the bank's existing Citi Token Services platform, which already processes billions in tokenized deposits across New York, 
London, and Hong Kong. 
A joint stablecoin initiative involving JPMorgan, Bank of America, Citigroup, and Wells Fargo operates through Early Warning Services (Zelle's 
operator) and The Clearing House. It entered early exploration in May 2025. Separately, a nine-bank global consortium including Goldman Sachs, 
Deutsche Bank, BNP Paribas, UBS, and others announced plans for a G7-currency-backed stablecoin on public networks. 
BNY Mellon has positioned itself as the dominant custodian for stablecoin infrastructure rather than an issuer. The bank serves as the primary 
custodian for Circle's USDC reserves and was selected in July 2025 as the primary custodian for Ripple's RLUSD. In November 2025, BNY 
launched the Dreyfus Stablecoin Reserves Fund (BSRXX), a money market fund speciﬁcally designed to hold GENIUS Act-compliant reserves 
for stablecoin issuers. 
Bank stablecoins will capture institutional ﬂows where counterparty risk controls and regulatory assurance are paramount. Corporate treasurers 
and asset managers will favor a JPMorgan or Citi stablecoin over USDT for the same reason they favor a JPMorgan prime brokerage account over 
an oﬀshore alternative. The main risk is that these products could remain closed-loop instruments. If bank stablecoins only move inside their 
issuers’ networks, they function as high-speed wires or intrabank settlement tokens, not as programmable, interoperable money. The joint 
initiative via Early Warning Services indicates that banks see value in shared rails, but it remains unclear whether competitive dynamics will allow 
meaningful interoperability. Fintechs, by contrast, are structurally better positioned to integrate across venues and counterparties without needing 
industry-wide coordination. 
Stripe and PayPal compete for payment stablecoin dominance 
In February 2025, Stripe completed its $1.1 billion acquisition of Bridge, the largest crypto deal by a major payments ﬁrm. Bridge delivers 
stablecoin infrastructure APIs that enable businesses to issue, manage, and accept stablecoins without directly interacting with digital assets. Its 
notable clients include SpaceX for global treasury operations and government entities handling Latin American aid disbursement. 
In September 2025, Stripe launched Open Issuance, which allows businesses to launch and manage their own branded stablecoins while capturing 
yield from reserves. The company is also developing Tempo, a high-performance Layer-1 network in partnership with Paradigm, speciﬁcally 
optimized for stablecoin merchant payments. Stripe CEO Patrick Collison characterized stablecoins as "room-temperature superconductors for 
ﬁnancial services." 
While Stripe builds the rails for others to issue stablecoins, PayPal has issued its own. PayPal's PYUSD is up over 600% year-over-year with a 
market cap of $3.8 billion. Issued by Paxos under NYDFS regulation, PYUSD has expanded from Ethereum to Solana, Arbitrum, and via 
LayerZero's omnichain protocol to Tron, Avalanche, Sei, and additional networks. With distribution across PayPal and Venmo's 400 million users, 
PYUSD oﬀers scale unmatched by crypto-native competitors. 
               
 


---

## Page 70

69 
 
Meanwhile, Visa and Mastercard are building infrastructure to settle stablecoins rather than mint them. Visa has expanded its stablecoin settlement 
platform to support USDC, PYUSD, USDG, and EURC across Ethereum, Solana, Stellar, and Avalanche. The network has settled over $200 
million in stablecoin payments, and CEO Ryan McInerney acknowledged the milestone while noting it remains "a very small portion of overall 
settlement volume." Visa's VTAP (Tokenized Asset Platform), launched in October 2024, enables banks to issue and manage their own digital 
tokens via API. 
Mastercard joined the Global Dollar Network (USDG) consortium in June 2025, alongside founding partners Robinhood, Kraken, Galaxy Digital, 
and Paxos. The network's unique structure shares reserve income among consortium members rather than concentrating yields with the issuer. 
Mastercard's Multi-Token Network now connects to JPMorgan's Kinexys, Standard Chartered, and Ondo Finance, with support for USDC, 
PYUSD, USDG, and Fiserv's new FIUSD stablecoin. Both Visa and Mastercard stand to settle tens of billions annually by 2027 as banks route 
wholesale payments onchain. 
Cloudﬂare and Google build the AI-native payment stack 
               
 


---

## Page 71

70 
The ﬁnancial players are retroﬁtting stablecoins into existing payment ﬂows. Meanwhile, Cloudﬂare and Google are building for a category of 
transactions that barely exists yet. Cloudﬂare announced NET Dollar in September 2025. NET is a U.S. Dollar-backed stablecoin built for "the 
agentic web" and signals technology infrastructure companies' strategic interest in stablecoin issuance. The product enables AI agent transactions, 
micropayments, and automated commerce. Autonomous agents can book ﬂights or access APIs through their programmable payments. 
Google Cloud launched the Agent Payments Protocol (AP2) in September 2025. AP2 is an open-source AI payments protocol with native 
stablecoin support developed alongside Coinbase, Ethereum Foundation, Salesforce, American Express, and PayPal. James Tromans, Google 
Cloud's Head of Web3, characterized it as "probably one of the biggest upgrades to payments since the SWIFT network." 
Google is simultaneously developing GCUL (Google Cloud Universal Ledger), a permissioned Layer-1 network for institutional ﬁnance, with 
CME Group as the ﬁrst pilot partner. The network positions itself as "credibly neutral" infrastructure: "Tether won't use Circle's blockchain, and 
Adyen probably won't use Stripe's blockchain. But any ﬁnancial institution can build with GCUL." 
Apple, Meta, Amazon, and Walmart are all reportedly evaluating stablecoin strategies. Apple has held early-stage discussions with Circle and 
Worldpay about Apple Pay integration. Meta hired Ginger Baker as VP of Product with crypto experience in May 2025 to explore creator payouts 
via stablecoins, a quiet revival of the institutional knowledge from the failed Diem project. Amazon is evaluating the issuance of stablecoins for 
cross-border supplier payments, while Walmart is exploring a USD-backed stablecoin to reduce its dependence on payment processors across its 
e-commerce and physical retail operations. 
What started as a crypto-native experiment has become a full-scale institutional land grab. Banks, card networks, ﬁntechs, and cloud platforms are 
all racing to build stablecoin infrastructure, and the payments layer is rapidly maturing. The merger of tech, ﬁnance, and AI will dominate 2026 as 
agent-driven commerce scales these rails. But payments are only half the equation. As the infrastructure commoditizes, the diﬀerentiation shifts to 
what holders actually earn. This is where crypto-native stablecoins see their opening. 
Falling rates threaten Treasury-backed stablecoins 
Tether retains all the yield from its $135 billion in Treasury reserves, which is why it earns approximately $15 billion in annual interest. Circle 
shares some yield with platforms like Coinbase, but the returns remain within the distribution network rather than being distributed to all holders. 
In contrast, yield-bearing stablecoins pass reserve returns directly to anyone who holds the token. By November 2025, the category had grown 
1400% year-over-year to $17.6 billion. 
               
 


---

## Page 72

71 
 
Yield-bearing stablecoins backed by U.S. Treasuries have seen rapid onchain adoption, led by products like BUIDL and USYC. The early wave of 
growth was fueled by elevated front-end yields, which made tokenized Treasury exposure especially attractive. But after three rate cuts in 2025, 
the Fed funds rate now sits at 3.50-3.75%, and the trajectory points lower. Polymarket is pricing multiple additional cuts in 2026, and Kevin 
Hassett, widely seen as the next Fed chair, argues that there is "plenty of room" to cut further. As yields compress, Treasury-backed stablecoins 
will lose appeal, while alternatives oﬀering competitive returns from crypto activity or real-world assets like GPUs become relatively more 
attractive. 
               
 


---

## Page 73

72 
 
Crypto-native stablecoins are poised to grow as rates fall 
The 2026 macro environment favors crypto-native yield strategies. As policy rates fall and risk-free yields compress, marginal capital tends to 
move out of the risk curve. In crypto, that reallocation historically shows up as increased leverage and more onchain activity, which expands the 
set of crypto-native yield streams available to onchain farmers. 
The clearest example is perpetual futures funding. Perps continuously balance long and short demand through periodic funding payments. In 
risk-oﬀ regimes, funding tends to hover near zero or turn negative (as in 2022), while in risk-on environments it turns persistently positive as 
traders pay premiums for leverage. During the late 2024 rally, ETH perpetual funding reached 20% annualized on some venues. For strategies 
positioned to capture this spread, such as delta-neutral spot and perp shorts, funding serves as a core revenue source that scales with demand for 
leverage. 
               
 


---

## Page 74

73 
 
Beyond perpetuals, crypto oﬀers yield mechanisms that operate largely independent of policy rates. Locked-token arbitrage, DEX/lending fees, 
and liquidity premiums on vested tokens all emerge from market structure rather than macro conditions. Holders seeking liquidity before their 
tokens vest often sell at steep discounts, typically 10%–25% and sometimes more than 50% percent in stressed markets. A hedged buyer can 
capture that discount as tokens unlock. In this sense, crypto generates yield from the frictions and incentives embedded in its own infrastructure. 
What further strengthens crypto-native yield is composability, where protocols compound opportunities by routing collateral and leverage through 
interconnected systems. Protocols stack strategies like building blocks and allow capital to move between opportunities at internet speed. A 
yield-bearing token from one platform can serve as collateral in another, and that collateral then feeds leverage loops that route into new yield 
strategies. Ethena demonstrated how composability can transform yield into a system-wide engine, and newer stablecoins are expanding this 
model into broader, more specialized strategies. 
2025 was a breakout year for Ethena’s USDe 
Ethena's USDe was the fastest-growing stablecoin of 2025. By October, USDe's market cap had peaked at $14.8 billion and ranked third behind 
               
 


---

## Page 75

74 
USDT and USDC. The October 10 liquidation event triggered a wave of redemptions, and the market cap retraced to $7.2 billion by year's end. 
 
Ethena’s yield is derived from a delta‑neutral basis trade. When a user deposits $100 of ETH to mint USDe, the protocol goes long that ETH 
while simultaneously opening a $100 short position in ETH perpetual futures. The price exposures are oﬀset, leaving a market-neutral position 
worth $100, regardless of where ETH trades. Yield primarily comes from the funding payments collected on the short-perp position. 
               
 


---

## Page 76

75 
 
Ethena oﬀers two tokens: 1) USDe (the base stablecoin) and 2) sUSDe (a staked version that accrues yield). By holding sUSDe, users receive the 
ongoing returns from the basis trade. In 2025, sUSDe yielded an average of 6%, about 60% higher than the current 3‑month T‑bill rate of 3.73%. 
Ethena has leaned into crypto’s composability. sUSDe is accepted as collateral across Aave, Morpho, and other lending markets where users can 
deposit sUSDe, borrow stablecoins against it, mint more USDe, stake it, and loop the trade. Pendle allows users to tokenize and trade future 
sUSDe yield separately from the principal. Within a year of launch, sUSDe was integrated across L1 and L2 networks and embedded in dozens of 
DeFi strategies. 
Ethena has also expanded its product suite to adapt to diﬀerent rate environments. USDtb is a tokenized T-bill product that lets the protocol park 
collateral in Treasuries when crypto funding rates compress. When funding spikes, Ethena leans into the basis trade. The protocol dynamically 
allocates between crypto-native yield and traditional yield based on market conditions. As rates fall and crypto activity expands, Ethena is 
positioned to capture demand on both sides of the trade. 
A new generation of long-tail stablecoins in 2026 
               
 


---

## Page 77

76 
The yield-bearing stablecoin market is poised to fragment and expand in 2026. Ethena captured the funding rate trade at scale, but it won't be the 
only project that succeeds. A new crop of protocols are targeting alternative yield sources, from GPU compute to locked token arbitrage to 
specialized lending markets. Each oﬀers a diﬀerent risk/reward proﬁle, collectively pointing to a future where yield-bearing stalbecoins serve 
every corner of the cryptoeconomy. 
Neutrl 
Neutrl is a market-neutral synthetic dollar protocol designed to turn OTC discounts into onchain dollar yield. It issues NUSD, a fully 
collateralized synthetic dollar that users can mint 1:1 with stablecoins such as USDC, USDT, or USDe, and then deploy across DeFi as a standard 
stable asset. Under the hood, Neutrl allocates capital into strategies like OTC arbitrage, funding-rate and basis trades, and other delta-neutral 
structures, aiming to generate yield that is structurally uncorrelated to simple lending spreads or token emissions. The protocol hedges market 
exposure on its discounted altcoin purchases with oﬀsetting derivatives positions, so returns come from locked-in spreads and funding premia 
rather than directional price moves. 
 
               
 


---

## Page 78

77 
On top of NUSD, Neutrl oﬀers sNUSD, a staked, yield-bearing version that represents a claim on the protocol’s underlying strategy portfolio. 
NUSD stakers receive sNUSD and accrue yield automatically via an appreciating sNUSD:NUSD exchange rate, with returns sourced from the 
protocol’s market-neutral trading and arbitrage activity. Neutrl enforces cooldowns and optional lockups around unstaking, and manages liquidity 
via a reserve system that balances instant redemptions with queued withdrawals. 
Neutrl's edge is supply-driven rather than speculative. Token unlocks create a predictable supply of motivated sellers, and 2026's unlock calendar 
is expected to exceed $32 billion. As long as teams and investors require liquidity before their tokens vest, Neutrl has a yield source that is 
independent of interest rates, leverage demand, or market direction. 
USDai 
USDai is a synthetic dollar protocol that ﬁnances AI infrastructure by channeling DeFi liquidity into GPU-backed loans for emerging AI 
companies. Users mint USDai, a fully collateralized and redeemable synthetic dollar, by depositing stablecoins, which the protocol leverages to 
secure high-yield loans backed by tokenized physical assets, such as GPUs, through its innovative CALIBER framework. The protocol combines 
baseline Treasury bill yields with yields from infrastructure lending, creating returns that are uncorrelated with traditional crypto volatility or Fed 
policy rates.  
               
 


---

## Page 79

78 
 
The protocol issues sUSDai as a yield-bearing staked version of USDai, where holders accrue value through an appreciating exchange rate 
powered by loan interest and idle capital parked in T-bills. USDai has already surged to a market cap of $650 million, and AI compute demand 
will surge regardless of Fed rate decisions or crypto market swings. If this thesis holds, USDai delivers truly uncorrelated yield, untethered from 
the macro forces shaping most stablecoin strategies. 
Cap 
While protocols like Ethena, Neutrl, and USDai chase specialized yields, Cap aggregates liquidity across fragmented stablecoins. Its stablecoin, 
cUSD, functions as a credibly neutral synthetic dollar minted 1:1 with compliant stablecoins like USDC, USDT, or USDe, pooling them into a 
uniﬁed reserve that enables onchain swaps between assets without intermediaries or preferential treatment. This aims to solve stablecoin 
fragmentation by delivering "singleness of money" through veriﬁable smart contracts, positioning cUSD as an open settlement layer for 
frictionless value transfer in a multi-stablecoin world. 
               
 


---

## Page 80

79 
 
On top of cUSD sits stcUSD, a yield-bearing staked token that decentralizes capital allocation via a competitive network of operators backed by 
restakers' delegated capital. stcUSD enforces veriﬁable downside protection through shared security networks, where operators must secure credit 
delegations to participate, which ensures transparent risk coverage and perpetual yield optimization invariant to market conditions. As the 
stablecoin market grows, Cap's architecture scales to capture composability premiums across DeFi, and cements cUSD as the default rail for 
neutral, high-velocity settlements. 
Yield has always been crypto’s killer app 
Yield has always been crypto's killer app. Terra's Anchor Protocol attracted $18 billion into UST by oﬀering a 20% APY, Olympus DAO reached 
a $4 billion market cap on the back of 8,000% rebasing rewards, and during DeFi Summer in 2020, TVL exploded from under $1 billion to over 
$10 billion in months as users chased liquidity mining rewards while the Fed held rates at zero. None of it lasted. Anchor was subsidized, 
Olympus ran on reﬂexive tokenomics, and DeFi Summer depended on inﬂationary emissions. Each eventually collapsed or faded into irrelevance. 
But the pattern held: oﬀer a compelling yield, and capital will follow. 
               
 


---

## Page 81

80 
 
The diﬀerence in 2025 is that the yields are sourced from real cash ﬂows, rather than token inﬂation. Perp funding rates, borrowing interest, 
arbitrage spreads, and liquidity premiums all generate returns from actual cryptoeconomic activity rather than token printing. The yields are more 
modest, typically 5% to 15% rather than thousands of percent, but they're grounded in cash ﬂows that can persist. And the macro setup looks 
familiar. The Fed is easing, traditional yields are compressing, and the relative appeal of onchain returns is rising. The last time this happened, it 
triggered DeFi Summer. This time, the infrastructure is more mature, and the capital is more sophisticated. The conditions for another yield-driven 
expansion are forming, but with a sturdier foundation underneath. 
RWAs 
Stablecoins have given the dollar an internet-native payments rail, but real-world assets (RWAs) are turning that rail into balance sheet 
infrastructure. In this section, real-world assets refer to oﬀchain ﬁnancial and real assets, such as government debt, credit, commodities, funds, and 
equities, that are issued or wrapped as tokens on public networks. 
The promise of RWAs is that they can earn yield, serve as collateral, and settle on the same crypto rails as native tokens. The reality is more 
               
 


---

## Page 82

81 
nuanced. Not all RWAs are created equal, and the degree of composability depends on how issuers navigate securities regulations. 
At one end of the spectrum sit fully permissioned RWAs. These tokens can only be held and transferred by whitelisted addresses, limiting 
composability to approved venues like Aave Horizon or internal settlement systems. At the other end are fully permissionless RWAs that transfer 
freely and integrate across DeFi without restrictions. Between these two categories sits a growing category of soft-restricted tokens, products like 
Ondo's USDY, which technically prohibit U.S. persons but rely on passive attestations rather than onchain enforcement. These trade with 
near-permissionless liquidity in practice. 
The regulatory environment largely determines where a given RWA lands on this spectrum. Issuers targeting U.S. investors face SEC registration 
requirements that push toward permissioned structures. Those willing to exclude U.S. persons, at least on paper, can access broader DeFi 
composability. As regulatory frameworks like the GENIUS Act mature, we expect the boundaries between these categories to shift. 
 
As of Nov. 30, 2025, tokenized RWAs held onchain reached $18.1 billion versus $300 billion in stablecoins, with approximately 550,000 RWA 
               
 


---

## Page 83

82 
holders compared with more than 205 million stablecoin holders. 
These ﬁgures reﬂect data from RWA.xyz, which distinguishes between “Distributed Assets” and "Represented Assets." Distributed Assets can 
move to wallets outside the issuing platform. Represented Assets cannot. But this binary undersells the complexity. Within distributed assets, there 
are meaningful gradations of usability. 
Some distributed RWAs can move to external wallets but remain non-transferable between users. Franklin Templeton's tokenized funds fall into 
this category, where holders custody the asset in their own wallet but cannot send it to another address. Others are transferable, but only between 
permissioned wallets, which restricts secondary liquidity to whitelisted counterparties. A third category, including products like Ondo's USDY, are 
technically restricted from U.S. persons but trade with near-permissionless liquidity in practice, relying on attestations rather than onchain 
enforcement. Only a small subset of tokenized RWAs are fully composable across DeFi without restrictions. 
In the ﬁgures that follow, unless stated otherwise, we refer to distributed assets. But readers should understand that "distributed" does not always 
mean "freely transferable" or "DeFi-composable.” 
               
 


---

## Page 84

83 
 
The RWA mix is more concentrated than the tokenization narrative suggests. U.S. Treasuries alone account for roughly half of the distributed 
RWA value, with sovereign debt and private credit dominating overall. Commodities and institutional funds form the next tier, while equities and 
corporate bonds remain small in absolute terms. This reﬂects where tokenization has found immediate product-market ﬁt, particularly in ﬁxed 
income and credit that can be easily integrated into onchain yield, collateral, and treasury workﬂows. As ﬁnance and crypto continue to converge, 
onchain RWA distribution will increasingly mirror TradFi market caps, with Treasuries and blue chip assets leading before equities and corporates 
scale. 
               
 


---

## Page 85

84 
 
U.S. Treasuries are the largest and most widely adopted RWA category. Tokenized U.S. Treasury products (both Distributed and Represented) 
expanded from $2.6 billion in November 2024 to $9.2 billion one year later, a more than three-fold increase. These instruments package T-bill and 
government money market exposure into tokens that can circulate with stablecoins and be deployed as collateral across DeFi and centralized 
venues. Non-U.S. government debt remains much smaller in absolute terms but is scaling quickly. Value rose from $88 million to $618 million, a 
roughly seven-fold increase YoY, reﬂecting issuer demand to replicate the tokenized cash product in multiple currencies. Treasuries' dominance 
will intensify as TradFi pipelines like DTCC route trillions onchain. 
               
 


---

## Page 86

85 
 
Corporate bonds remain a proof-of-concept category, but have grown from $11 million to $19 million YoY. Private credit did not register 
meaningfully onchain until 2025, yet it already accounts for $1.9 billion across more than 66,000 holders. These pools ﬁnance SME loans, 
receivables, and other oﬀchain borrowers and oﬀer yields that sit well above Treasuries, which makes them a natural next step for investors who 
already hold stablecoins and tokenized bills. 
               
 


---

## Page 87

86 
 
The tokenized commodity market cap (both Distributed and Represented) grew 244% YoY to $3.6 billion, with Tether Gold and Paxos Gold as the 
dominant products and more than 167,000 holders using them as digital bullion and collateral. Institutional funds have expanded even faster. 
Assets in tokenized institutional and alternative funds have increased from approximately $312 million to $2.7 billion in a year, representing a 
nearly nine-fold rise, as asset managers convert existing strategies into tokenized share classes that can integrate into crypto rails without requiring 
a reinvention of their investment process. 
               
 


---

## Page 88

87 
 
Tokenized public equity and ETF-like products have grown from $9 million to $633 million in a year. These instruments provide oﬀshore users 
and DeFi protocols with synthetic access to listed names and indices, while maintaining underlying custody in traditional venues. Private equity 
tokenization adds several hundred million dollars more but remains niche. The equity segment proves there is demand for tokenized securities, yet 
the asset mix as a whole makes clear that the ﬁrst serious wave of tokenization is about cash, sovereign risk, and credit rather than wholesale 
migration of global equity markets. 
Depository Trust & Clearing Corporation (DTCC), the settlement backbone of U.S. securities markets, received a no-action letter from the SEC 
authorizing it to tokenize DTC-custodied assets in a controlled production environment. The initial rollout, expected in the second half of 2026, 
will cover U.S. Treasuries, selected equities, and index-tracking ETFs, with transfers restricted to registered wallets. While these assets will 
remain permissioned at launch, the approval marks a meaningful inﬂection point: U.S. market infrastructure is preparing to operate on blockchain 
rails under existing securities law. As SEC Chair Paul Atkins noted, U.S. ﬁnancial markets are “poised to move onchain,” reinforcing the view that 
tokenization is evolving into a settlement and balance sheet upgrade rather than a parallel ﬁnancial system. 
               
 


---

## Page 89

88 
 
The distribution of RWAs across blockchains is concentrated. Ethereum hosts $11.7 billion in RWA value, or a 64% share, reﬂecting its deep 
custody, liquidity, and DeFi infrastructure. BNB Chain follows at $1.6 billion and 9% share, while Solana, Avalanche, Arbitrum, and Stellar each 
host between $640 million and $775 million, or 3-4% each. Polygon, Aptos, ZKsync, and XRP Ledger, and complete the top ten with smaller 
footprints. While tokenization is becoming multichain, most asset value still aggregates around Ethereum and a handful of secondary hubs where 
user demand or strategic partnerships justify support. 
Permissioned L1s like Canton will likely attract institutional-grade assets that require strict compliance, which could erode public chains' lead in 
high-value ﬂows. Ethereum maintains its edge in retail and DeFi liquidity, but issuers may route trillions of dollars to private rails for enhanced 
privacy and regulatory compliance. Bridges and standards will bridge the gap over time in order to prevent silos.

## Page 90

Expansion of the Multichain World 
Key Themes 
● Ethereum reaﬃrmed itself as the settlement layer for institutional crypto, dominating stablecoins and RWAs while accelerating the pace of 
network upgrades under more cohesive leadership. 
● Rollups dominate Ethereum’s execution layer, yet L2 tokens continue to underperform, exposing a growing disconnect between usage 
growth and value capture as revenues fail to justify extreme P/E multiples. 
● L2s are pivoting toward enterprises and institutions while increasing focus on revenue generation. Competition is consolidating around 
incumbents with liquidity and distribution, though Robinhood Chain and MegaETH could shake up the sector in 2026. 
● Prop AMMs advanced Solana’s vision of creating an onchain Nasdaq as the network dominated onchain spot volume, surpassing Binance 
on SOL-Stablecoin volume at times. The next opportunity is leveraging protocol upgrades to recapture perps volume. 
● The question going forward is not “which chain will win,” but which ecosystems are compounding long-term adoption in market segments 
that matter to them. 
Introduction 
Crypto is multichain. Activity and adoption is spreading across Layer-1s (L1) and Layer-2s (L2) alike. In our Multichain section, we cover 
Ethereum as the leading settlement layer, the L2 ecosystem as Ethereum’s venue for transaction throughput, and Solana as the leading 
high-performance execution environment. Various narratives emerged throughout 2025, such as Ethereum losing relevance to rollups while ETH 
underperformed, or Solana winning retail and speculative activity amid the TRUMP launch and the ensuing memecoin frenzy. Regardless of 
narratives or price action, the reality when looking back on the year is that each ecosystem is continuing on a strong growth trajectory. 
The data paints a more coherent picture. Ethereum is the default platform for regulated, institutional activity and tokenized ﬁnancial infrastructure. 
Core contributors are accelerating protocol development through major upgrades that expand rollup capacity and tighten economic alignment. L2s 
continue to absorb nearly all of Ethereum’s transaction velocity, with competition intensifying among incumbents like Base, Arbitrum, and 
Optimism. Meanwhile, Solana is sustaining its dominance in high-velocity consumer and trading activity, especially across spot volume as 
onchain liquidity matures. 
The question going forward in an ever-expanding multichain world is not “which chain wins,” but which ecosystems are compounding long-term 
adoption in market segments that matter to them. Of course, Ethereum, L2s, and Solana are not the only networks that are core to the multichain 
world. A recap of other chains and infrastructure providers and their respective outlooks are covered in detail within this section, several of which 
               
 


---

## Page 91

90 
Messari partners with for Protocol Services. 
Ethereum 
Author: Drexel Bakker 
Throughout 2025, Ethereum’s strategic direction and long-term value were frequent targets of debate. Questions about whether Ethereum still 
retains a meaningful moat, whether L2s weaken or strengthen the network, and whether alternative execution environments would erode 
Ethereum’s relevance all became recurring themes. If you followed only the narrative, you might assume Ethereum spent the year losing ground. 
The data tells a diﬀerent story, as Ethereum quietly reinforced its position at the center of onchain activity. It remained the dominant settlement 
layer for the categories institutions care about: stablecoins, real-world assets (RWAs), and tokenized ﬁnancial infrastructure. These are the use 
cases that brought large, risk-averse players into crypto for the ﬁrst time, and they overwhelmingly chose Ethereum. Not because of marketing or 
momentum, but because Ethereum already had a proven track record of security as well as the liquidity, tooling, compliance pathways, and 
developer depth required to support them at scale. 
Stablecoin supply expanded signiﬁcantly in 2025, growing 49% YoY from $207.34 billion to $309.96 billion, with the majority of that growth 
settling on Ethereum. The most widely adopted tokenization pilots, whether for treasuries, credit products, or money-market instruments, 
launched ﬁrst on Ethereum rails. The categories with real institutional traction and cash ﬂows concentrated on Ethereum, even as other chains 
competed aggressively on speed, cost, and incentives. 
TVL Moat 
               
 


---

## Page 92

91 
 
Despite new L1s like Berachain and Monad launching in 2025 and Solana memecoins capturing the lion's share of retail attention, Ethereum’s 
TVL dominance was unphased throughout the year. Starting at 56% at the beginning of the year, it reached a low of 51% in early May before 
rallying to a high of 62% in mid-August and ﬁnishing the year at 58%. As mercenary capital chases the next opportunity and new chain, 
Ethereum’s TVL moat continues to be sticky year after year. 
               
 


---

## Page 93

92 
 
Stablecoins 
Stablecoins were one of the leading narratives of 2025, and the entrance of new issuers helped grow crypto’s total stablecoin supply to over $300 
billion. USDT and USDC continued to dominate, ﬁnishing the year with 60% and 25% market share, respectively, but their roles diverged 
signiﬁcantly. USDT remained the preferred instrument for global liquidity and crypto-native ﬂows, while USDC saw renewed institutional 
adoption as Circle improved its compliance tooling and treasury integration. The return of net USDC issuance, after a prolonged period of 
contraction, marked one of the clearest demand signals for regulated onchain dollars in years. 
New entrants also found traction. PayPal’s PYUSD expanded its footprint across exchanges and payment platforms, helped by the company’s 
distribution rails. Stripe and Visa deepened their stablecoin integrations, bringing onchain settlement closer to mainstream commerce. On a 
smaller scale, several regional banks experimented with tokenized deposit models, blurring the line between stablecoins and traditional money 
infrastructure. 
               
 


---

## Page 94

93 
 
Among the six largest chains by stablecoin market share, Ethereum ended 2025 with $167.28 billion in stablecoins onchain, up 47% YoY, 
representing 59% of total stablecoin market capitalization. While USDT and USDC continued to dominate Ethereum’s stablecoin mix accounting 
for 83% of supply, 2025 also saw greater diversiﬁcation. Both USDS and USDe surpassed $5 billion in circulating supply, placing them among the 
ﬁve largest stablecoins on Ethereum. 
RWAs 
RWAs were one of the clearest areas where institutional behavior in 2025 diverged from the broader skepticism surrounding Ethereum. Tokenized 
treasuries crossed new highs as BlackRock expanded its BUIDL fund, which became one of the fastest-growing onchain products of the year. 
Franklin Templeton scaled its onchain money-market fund (FOBXX), reinforcing Ethereum’s role as the default environment for tokenized 
money-market instruments. JPMorgan’s Kinexys platform expanded its use of Ethereum-based networks for intraday repo and programmable 
payments, while WisdomTree launched additional tokenized funds directly on Ethereum's rails. All of this shows that although TradFi players 
have been slow to adopt crypto rails, when they do dip their toes in, Ethereum’s track record of uptime, security, and decentralization matters. 
               
 


---

## Page 95

94 
 
The beginning of 2025 raised signiﬁcant questions about Ethereum’s direction, as development seemed primarily focused on L2s, and 
stakeholders felt that Ethereum L1 was being neglected. This perception was exacerbated by the ETH/BTC ratio falling to new lows alongside a 
perceived lack of urgency from the Ethereum Foundation (EF). However, a leadership transition in March brought on a renewed sense of focus as 
Aya Miyaguchi moved from Executive Director to President, focusing on global representation and long-term strategy. Operational leadership has 
been passed to co-executive directors Hsiao-Wei Wang and Tomasz Stańczak, bringing deeper protocol research experience and client 
development expertise into day-to-day execution. 
That same mindset shaped Ethereum’s institutional engagement. In January, the EF supported the launch of Etherealize, Ethereum’s ﬁrst 
coordinated, corporate-facing initiative. Led by Vivek Raman, Etherealize serves as a central touchpoint for institutions, combining education, 
tooling, and product development to make regulated onchain activity easier to deploy at scale. 
The response marked a shift in tone and focus. Without abandoning its decentralized development model, Ethereum began to place greater 
emphasis on execution and clarity, particularly around the role of L1. Rather than treating the base layer as a passive settlement endpoint, core 
               
 


---

## Page 96

95 
contributors increasingly framed it as the economic and security anchor upon which everything else depends. 
Community pressure played a meaningful role in that recalibration. As rollups grew, critics warned that Ethereum risked becoming too abstracted 
from its own value capture and too slow to address practical constraints such as throughput, fees, and MEV. Instead of dismissing those critiques, 
developers incorporated them into the roadmap. 
That shift was evident in 2025’s delivery cadence. For the ﬁrst time in Ethereum’s history, the network shipped two major upgrades in a single 
year, Pectra and Fusaka. Together, they marked a departure from the slower, more gradual pace that had followed the Merge, signaling a renewed 
willingness to ship meaningful protocol improvements. 
The Signiﬁcance of Fusaka 
Fusaka addressed the most important constraint in Ethereum’s scaling path: how to expand rollup throughput without turning node operation into 
an arms race. After blobs were introduced as a purpose-built data type for L2s, the key question became whether Ethereum could support higher 
blob volumes without forcing every node to download and store everything. 
Fusaka introduced coordinated changes to improve blob scaling and economics: PeerDAS distributes blob data so each node stores only a fraction, 
about one-eighth in the target design, while cryptographic guarantees preserve availability, enabling up to 8x theoretical blob scaling without 
overburdening operators; Blob-Parameter-Only (BPO) forks remove a coordination bottleneck by allowing blob targets and maximums to be 
adjusted between hard forks, turning scaling into a gradual, tunable process; and EIP-7918 introduces a reserve-price mechanism tied to execution 
costs to prevent blob fees from collapsing, ensuring a responsive fee market in which rollups pay a meaningful share of network resources. 
Beyond L2 scaling, Fusaka also hardened Ethereum’s execution layer. Caps on transaction gas size, tighter limits on historically risky computation 
paths, explicit block-size ceilings, and support for history expiry all reduced worst-case behavior and made future gas-limit increases safer. 
Looking Ahead: Ethereum in 2026 
2026 is poised to be a pivotal year for Ethereum. Over the past year, the EF, Etherealize, and core developers have appeared to respond 
meaningfully to community frustration, and the network’s direction feels more coherent than it has in several years. 
Although it’s still in the EIP coordination phase, Glamsterdam, the next major Ethereum upgrade, is likely to bring continued improvements to 
base layer economics as gas fees for smart contract execution are changed to reﬂect real resource costs. Glamsterdam will also introduce 
enshrined proposer-builder separation, which separates the entities responsible for block building and block proposing at the consensus layer, an 
important step to reduce reliance on centralized relays and further decentralize every part of the network. 
Another signiﬁcant development for 2026 is Kohaku, a new privacy initiative that plans to integrate privacy features directly into Ethereum wallets 
and developer tooling. Championed by Vitalik himself, Kohaku will enable users to shield transactions and funds, isolate dApp-speciﬁc addresses, 
and create stealth or one-time addresses. By building Kohaku as an open-source SDK, developers and existing EVM wallets will be able to 
               
 


---

## Page 97

96 
incorporate privacy as a ﬁrst-class feature of Ethereum. 
Deﬁning what ETH is remains diﬃcult, and that ambiguity is unlikely to disappear in 2026. ETH sits at the intersection of monetary asset, 
productive commodity, and technology platform, which complicates how it trades across diﬀerent market environments. It does not beneﬁt from 
the same singular narrative that has helped BTC attract consistent macro-driven ﬂows. 
Still, 2025 marked a clear shift. Both the market’s treatment of ETH and the development trajectory of Ethereum moved in a more disciplined 
direction. Additionally, all the data continued to show that Ethereum is one of the few networks with a durable long-term moat, as TVL, stablecoin 
market share, and RWA deployment were all dominated by Ethereum. That doesn’t resolve ETH’s identity problem, but it does suggest a healthier 
foundation than a year ago. Going into 2026, the outlook is best described as cautiously optimistic, driven by the fact that institutions are 
increasingly choosing Ethereum as their onchain platform of choice, and by the clarity that has emerged around Ethereum’s development direction 
over the past year. 
Layer-2s 
Authors: Patryk Krasnicki, AJC, Evan Zakhary, and Hayden Booms 
Ethereum’s L2 ecosystem continued on its strong growth trajectory in 2025. Going back to March 2024, Ethereum’s Dencun upgrade introduced 
data blobs via EIP-4844, which drastically reduced the cost to transact on rollups while boosting their economics by increasing sequencer proﬁt 
margins. The cost to transact continued a trend toward zero this year as blob capacity doubled with the Pectra upgrade and EIP-7691 in May. Most 
recently, in December, the Fusaka upgrade introduced PeerDAS and EIP-7892, which are expected to increase throughput even further. L2 
dominance over transactions per second (TPS) relative to Ethereum breached 95% for the ﬁrst time in November. While Ethereum still holds the 
vast majority of assets, transaction velocity has been wholly outsourced to rollups. 
               
 


---

## Page 98

97 
 
Transactional capacity on L2s has gone parabolic, with total TPS averaging 50 in November 2023, and 325 in November 2025. The EVM has 
proliferated to the point that the world’s largest institutions are using it, and Ethereum’s rollup-centric roadmap has been achieved in many ways. 
Ethereum has scaled horizontally because of rollups, but the value of increased scale actually accruing to ETH the asset was called into question 
more than ever this year. Many have questioned whether L2s are “parasitic” to Ethereum as transaction fees are captured by centralized sequencer 
operators, assets are natively issued on rollups, and transactional activity ﬂows away from Ethereum. 
The price of ETH underperformed relative to BTC from 2022 until April 2025, when the ETH/BTC ratio bottomed as Pectra went live. Still, 
because of Ethereum’s focus on rollups over years of sustained underperformance, sentiment amongst many ETH tokenholders remains against 
the proposition that L2s are value accretive to ETH. 
               
 


---

## Page 99

98 
 
Over $37 billion in assets are housed on rollups. The value of stablecoins and BTC on rollups has grown 33% and 121% YTD. The “Others” 
category in the TVS chart above, which includes L2 tokens native to their respective rollups, has fallen 51% in value YTD. 
However, the long-term growth of TVS, and other DeFi metrics, hasn’t translated to chain revenue growth of the same magnitude. L2 tokens 
underperformed in 2025 and trade at extreme P/E ratios. Due to underperformance from altcoins in 2025 along with the emerging “revenue meta,” 
there is mounting pressure by the market for revenue growth from high-proﬁle L2s like Arbitrum and Optimism. 
For years, many from Ethereum’s L2 ecosystem expected tech stacks from Arbitrum, Optimism, ZKsync, and Polygon to power rollups for 
application-speciﬁc use cases as the world’s largest enterprises moved onchain. However, some of this year’s biggest splashes were stablechains 
like Stripe’s Tempo and Circle’s Arc opting for L1 architectures over L2, showing that launching a rollup is not the only route being considered by 
large enterprises. Additionally, liquidity and activity continued to concentrate amongst existing, leading L2s like Base and Arbitrum One in 2025, 
while smaller rollups struggled to sustain meaningful usage. Rollups like Pirate Nation and Polygon zkEVM were deprecated entirely this year, 
highlighting that while it is easier than ever to launch a rollup, that doesn’t mean it'll ﬁnd PMF. 
These trends are likely to persist into 2026. The price to transact on L2s will continue to fall, pushing transactional dominance by L2s relative to 
               
 


---

## Page 100

99 
Ethereum to above 99% long-term. Leading rollups should remain dominant as institutions continue to launch tokenized products onchain on 
venues with pre-existing liquidity and users, though new rollups like Robinhood Chain and MegaETH could shake things up. Regardless, L2s will 
aggressively attempt to capture interest from enterprises and institutions in 2026, while focusing on scaling revenue generation due to today’s 
market dynamics. 
As for project-speciﬁc developments, Arbitrum is creating a self-sustaining revenue engine as the launch of Robinhood Chain looms. Optimism is 
shifting focus toward institutions and regrowing OP Mainnet as Base continues its dominance. MegaETH is launching with the promise of 
next-gen performance that will enable a new class of real-time applications, while going to market with a native stablecoin that may serve as an 
example for how other L2 ecosystems could capture more value. ZKsync is doubling down on its “bank stack” thesis by productizing 
institutional-grade privacy and compliance, positioning the ZK Stack as a credible venue for regulated onchain workﬂows. Mantle is leaning into 
Bybit’s distribution and a vertically integrated yield stack to become a banking-oriented execution layer. 
Arbitrum 
Arbitrum had one of its strongest years in 2025, growing its dominance across several key metrics, locking in major partnerships for new 
Arbitrum Chains, and solidifying its long-term strategy for growth and self-sustainability. The success of Arbitrum One, and its DeFi leadership, is 
demonstrated by its $16.83 billion in TVS, $2.82 billion in DeFi TVL, and a stablecoin market cap of $8.52 billion. 
               
 


---

## Page 101

100 
 
Arbitrum One is host to the largest L2 deployments of blue chip DeFi protocols like Aave ($1.12 billion in TVL), Uniswap ($348.3 million), Fluid 
($193.7 million), and Arbitrum-native DeFi protocols like GMX ($357.4 million) and Variational ($71.4 million). The Hyperliquid Bridge sources 
all of its USDC liquidity ($4.24 billion) from Arbitrum One, driving its stablecoin market cap to be the highest amongst L2s at $8.61 billion. 
This DeFi leadership is set to expand with Robinhood Chain, in what can be argued as Arbitrum’s main catalyst going into 2026. As detailed in 
our report on Arbitrum Everywhere, Robinhood Chain will be one of the Arbitrum Chains within the Arbitrum Orbit ecosystem, of which there 
are 47 live on mainnet, 14 in testnet, and 12 in development. 
Securing Robinhood was a major win that will likely end up being a key moment in Arbitrum’s maturation. With Robinhood Chain set to launch 
in 2026, Arbitrum could have its Base-equivalent. Base makes up 82% of the Optimism Superchain’s TVL, but that level of dominance from 
Robinhood Chain is unlikely due to the focus Arbitrum places on Arbitrum One. Still, Robinhood Chain could bring the next wave of onchain 
activity to the Arbitrum Orbit ecosystem by merging a world-renowned ﬁnancial institution with the onchain capabilities of the Arbitrum Nitro 
tech stack. 
               
 


---

## Page 102

101 
 
Robinhood has already dipped their toes on Arbitrum One, where nearly 1,500 tokenized securities are live today, having reached a cumulative 
market cap of over $13 million. This is part of Arbitrum’s app-to-chain model that allows builders to go-to-market ﬁrst on Arbitrum One to 
bootstrap a user base and iterate on product while maintaining the ﬂexibility to migrate to a specialized Arbitrum Chain later. 
Despite all this positive momentum, L2 tokens like ARB have struggled. The token’s circulating market cap rank fell from 45th to 59th in 2025. 
However, Arbitrum-aligned entities like Arbitrum Foundation have formulated a long-term strategy meant to achieve sustained growth and 
ultimately, self-sustainability. As detailed in our report on Arbitrum's Economic Engine, ArbitrumDAO is building a Digital Sovereign Nation that 
generates revenue through digital resources like blockspace and execution environments, invests in ecosystem investment programs and economic 
zones of opportunity, and leads to compounding demand for its products, while growing its asset base. 
               
 


---

## Page 103

102 
 
ArbitrumDAO reached an all-time high monthly revenue of $4.4 million in October. 93% of ArbitrumDAO’s revenue generation in 2025 was 
driven by its blockspace, including transaction fees paid to Arbitrum One’s sequencer and its Timeboost auctions. Additionally, Arbitrum Chains 
are required to share 10% of sequencer proﬁt with ArbitrumDAO. This revenue was miniscule in 2025, accounting for less than 1% of total 
revenue, but could expand exponentially with the launch of Robinhood Chain in 2026. 
Finally, Arbitrum is a prime candidate to introduce a native stablecoin in 2026. MegaETH will be the ﬁrst L2 to do this with USDm, while 
Hyperliquid has already done so with USDH, as was explored in our report, Hyperliquid: Stablecoin Wars. Arbitrum is already heavily 
incentivizing stablecoin activity through the DeFi Renaissance Incentive Program (DRIP), which has resulted in a dramatic increase in the market 
cap, liquidity, and borrowing of stablecoins on Arbitrum One.

Assume ArbitrumDAO was to launch a native stablecoin and incentivize its growth to account for just 5% of Arbitrum One’s stablecoin market 
cap (excluding the USDC in the Hyperliquid Bridge) of $4.23 billion. The result would be a stablecoin with $211.4 million in backing assets 
generating $7.4 million in annual yield with an underlying U.S. Treasury rate of 4%, which would further strengthen Arbitrum’s economic engine.

Optimism 
Optimism lost momentum in 2025, and the price of OP has reﬂected that. While the token’s circulating market cap rank fell from 50th to 81st this 
year, Optimism is very much still a leading L2 ecosystem alongside Arbitrum. The OP Stack powered 62% of transactions across L2s and of 14% 
of all transactions in crypto in November.

## Page 105

Base still carries the Optimism Superchain, leading the 33 OP Chains in most metrics, as seen in our State of the Superchain H1 2025 report. 
Securing Base required Optimism granting up to 118 million OP tokens to Coinbase (worth about $187.7 million at the time) over the span of six 
years. Base has contributed $34.5 million back to the Optimism Collective via Superchain proﬁt sharing. While a crude measurement due to price 
ﬂuctuations, it counts the ~11,500 ETH that has been contributed while assuming an ETH price of $3,000. 
The deal, and having Base as an integral part of the Superchain, has also done wonders in boosting the market’s perception of Optimism. That 
said, questions about Base’s future as part of the Optimism Superchain arise at times. This is just speculation, as the details of the deal are not 
fully public and any shake up would be years away. Still, it is a fair question to wonder when the Superchain’s next-largest rollups by contributed 
revenue, OP Mainnet and Unichain, contributed just $3.4 million and $0.5 million compared to Base’s $10.8 million.

## Page 106

Optimism did secure a notable win in 2025 with Ronin opting to migrate to the Optimism Superchain over competing ecosystems in September. 
That said, high-proﬁle launches from the past year like Ink, Soneium, and Unichain have contributed limited amounts of revenue back to the 
Optimism Collective. Ronin should quickly become the top gaming-focused OP Chain after it launches in 2026. Still, with the market pressure on 
projects to generate revenue intensifying, and competitors like Arbitrum putting increased focus in that area, Optimism will feel the pressure to

highest-potential vectors for revenue for the Optimism Collective. 
Optimism’s co-founder, Jing Wang, recently posted about Optimism’s historical lack of focus and suboptimal token performance. Optimism has 
clearly shifted its strategy in recent months toward large enterprises, institutions, and OP Mainnet itself. If 2025 was the year of attracting big 
name OP Chains, 2026 is set to be the year of attracting institutional liquidity and new users to the Superchain and OP Mainnet, which has drifted 
into the shadows for too long and could reemerge in 2026.
