to become an active infrastructure provider. This is powerful because apps no longer need to bootstrap their own security or liquidity. Instead, they 
inherit a uniﬁed, secure standard instantly, allowing them to focus entirely on distribution and user acquisition while Flow handles the "heavy 
lifting." 
The enshrinement of FCM transforms the protocol’s business model by monetizing automation. Because the FCM relies on the onchain scheduler 
to constantly rebalance portfolios and compound yields, it generates a high baseline of transactions. Once these automated strategies push the 
network past ~250 TPS, the fees burned are designed to exceed new issuance, making FLOW net deﬂationary. This directly links the protocol's 
activity to the token's value, creating a self-sustaining economic engine. 
               
 


---

## Page 160

159 
To stay up to date with Flow, refer to Messari’s quarterly reports written by Jonny Kreiser (@jonnytoshi). 
Monad 
What Went Right in 2025 
 
On November 24, 2025, Monad launched its mainnet after three years of development. Monad raised $244 million since its ﬁrst round in February 
2023, before raising an additional $187.5 million through Coinbase’s new ICO mechanism in the week leading up to TGE. The MON token serves 
as the native currency of Monad, enabling transaction fee payments and staking to secure the network. At mainnet launch, MON’s total initial 
supply was set at 100 billion tokens, of which 7.5% was oﬀered during the ICO at a $2.5 billion valuation. There was demand for the token, with 
the sale being 1.47 times oversubscribed as Coinbase users committed over $276 million in just a ﬁve-day period. 
One of the most interesting aspects of Monad’s launch is the size of its ecosystem. There were 74 apps live on day one, including blue chip apps 
like Uniswap, Curve, Morpho, alongside many Monad-native apps. Today, that number has grown to over 100. 
2026 Outlook 
               
 


---

## Page 161

160 
In 2026, Monad could quietly show that there is still demand for new blockspace, especially EVM-compatible blockspace with 
faster-than-Ethereum block times that does not sacriﬁce on decentralization. In less than a month, Monad has accumulated over $400 million in 
stablecoins on its network and over $200 million in TVL. 
Two sectors on Monad that are primed for success in 2026 are DeFi and consumer. In DeFi, Monad oﬀers Kuru, an onchain spot CLOB, and 
Perpl, a perpetuals DEX hoping to be a category leader. On the consumer side, they have RareBetSports, a daily fantasy sports app, Levr.Bet, a 
leveraged sports betting platform, and Kizzy, a social betting app. 
Monad’s metrics may well trend upwards and by the end of 2026, a healthy ecosystem could solidify on the network. 
Modular Chains 
Authors: Gunkan, Jeremy Koch, Jake Koch-Gallup, and Jonny Kreiser 
Avalanche 
What Went Right in 2025 
Avalanche had a pivotal year in 2025, marked by steady institutional momentum and the expansion of regulated ﬁnancial infrastructure across the 
network. The introduction of jurisdiction-backed stablecoins, including Japan’s JPYC, Korea’s KRW1, and Wyoming’s FRNT, showcased 
Avalanche’s growing relevance for compliant payments and settlement. Through these initiatives, Avalanche is positioning itself as a trusted venue 
for day-to-day settlement and cross-border transfers, supported by collaborations with major ﬁnancial institutions such as SMBC in Japan and 
Woori Bank in Korea. Together, these eﬀorts reﬂect a broader strategy to provide national and regional stablecoin issuers with a high-performance 
blockchain environment tailored to regulated payment use cases. 
Institutional engagement also deepened through VanEck’s Purpose Built Fund, which is dedicated to investing in projects across the Avalanche 
ecosystem and reﬂects the growing interest from traditional asset managers in the network’s long-term potential. Together, these developments 
positioned Avalanche as an L1 that was clearly focused in 2025 on advancing its institutional alignment. 
               
 


---

## Page 162

161 
 
 
This momentum extended into RWAs, where Avalanche emerged as a leading venue for tokenized ﬁnancial products. Deployments from 
BlackRock, VanEck, SkyBridge, and Grove Finance highlighted the growing willingness of established ﬁrms to experiment with onchain credit, 
fund structures, and enterprise-grade settlement. Grove’s $250.0 million onchain credit program and SkyBridge’s tokenization of $300.0 million in 
assets were notable milestones that contributed to Avalanche’s RWA TVL increasing by over 495% in 2025. 
Avalanche also continued to deliver on gaming in 2025. MapleStory N’s arrival on its Henesys L1 drew more than one million users ahead of 
launch, while Pulsar and the GUNZ ecosystem strengthened Avalanche’s reputation as a scalable home for high-throughput gaming. Lastly, 
FIFA’s dedicated chain broadened Avalanche’s footprint in global sports and entertainment. 
2026 Outlook 
Avalanche enters 2026 with several catalysts that can reshape both network performance and token economics. The most consequential is the 
formation of its Digital Asset Treasuries, AVAT and AVAX One, which together target up to $1.70 billion in long-term AVAX accumulation.  
Bitwise, Grayscale, and VanEck each submitted applications for Avalanche-based spot ETF products in 2025. These ﬁlings introduce a potential 
               
 


---

## Page 163

162 
new channel for capital inﬂows, and any resolution in 2026 will be important to watch as investors gain clearer access to Avalanche through 
regulated market structures. The coming year shifts the focus from forming institutional relationships to demonstrating how these developments 
can translate into real economic activity onchain. 
Avalanche also enters 2026 with meaningful technical improvements following the Granite upgrade. This enhancement strengthens Avalanche’s 
multi-chain architecture through epoch-based P-Chain views, making Interchain Messaging more predictable and cost-eﬃcient. Although Granite 
was launched late in 2025, its beneﬁts will be felt throughout 2026 as developers adopt these capabilities and Avalanche continues to reﬁne 
performance across its expanding L1 ecosystem. 
Avalanche succeeds in 2026 by translating its institutional groundwork and technical upgrades into deeper liquidity, ecosystem growth, and more 
direct value capture for AVAX. The partnerships, infrastructure, and execution pathways are already in place. This year becomes about deepening 
relationships with the institutions Avalanche is already working with, while strengthening its ties to existing L1s and continuing to provide an 
environment that enables them to scale and attract users. At the same time, Avalanche needs to remain an attractive destination for new protocols 
seeking to launch their own L1s on the network. 
NEAR 
What Went Right in 2025 
NEAR made progress on its long-standing technical vision in 2025, translating years of architectural bets into visible execution. The network 
demonstrated throughput at a scale few L1s can credibly claim, publicly showcasing performance that crossed the one million TPS threshold 
under test conditions. This milestone reinforced the viability of NEAR’s sharded design and async execution model, validating that scalability was 
not merely theoretical but operationally achievable. 
               
 


---

## Page 164

163 
 
NEAR Intents also took shape as a unifying abstraction layer, reframing the user experience around outcomes rather than transactions. NEAR 
Intents has processed over $7 billion in all-time volume. By abstracting away chain-speciﬁc complexity, Intents positioned NEAR as a 
coordination layer capable of routing user intent across chains and services, aligning with broader industry trends toward intent-centric 
architectures. 
On the governance side, NEAR successfully rolled out the House of Stake in 2025. Following a partnership with Gauntlet and Agora, the new 
governance portal went live on mainnet on Aug. 7, 2025. Introducing onchain governance through veNEAR and an endorsed delegate model, the 
House of Stake marked a shift from informal coordination to a more structured and transparent governance process. This helped clarify 
decision-making pathways between token holders, delegates, and the NEAR Foundation, reducing ambiguity around protocol stewardship. 
2026 Outlook 
Looking ahead to 2026, NEAR is positioned to build on its momentum by deepening the real-world use of Intents as a foundational cross-chain 
and agent-native layer that abstracts away blockchain complexity, enabling seamless transactions and interactions across multiple networks and AI 
agents. In 2026, we hope to see the output of the partnership with Stanford Open Lab, exploring how veriﬁable AI agents can eﬀectively assist 
decentralized decision-making while maintaining human oversight. NEAR’s governance is evolving through ongoing AI research with Stanford 
               
 


---

## Page 165

164 
OpenLab via the House of Stake, exploring how intelligent systems can assist decentralized decision-making. This collaboration underscores 
NEAR’s ambition not just to scale infrastructure, but to pioneer how AI and Web3 can best combine, laying the groundwork for novel models of 
decentralized coordination in 2026. 
Polygon 
What Went Right in 2025 
 
Polymarket was Polygon’s clearest success in 2025, evolving from a niche prediction market into one of the largest sources of onchain 
transactional demand. The shift became apparent during the 2024 U.S. election cycle, when notional trading volume scaled from hundreds of 
millions to more than $3 billion per month across October, November, and December. Cumulative trading volume rose from $192.4 million at the 
end of 2023 to $14.43 billion by the end of 2024. 
Critically, 2025 did not retrace to pre-election levels. Monthly volumes stabilized in a new range of roughly $1.10 to $2.65 billion from January 
               
 


---

## Page 166

165 
through September, before moving above $4 billion in October and November. By the end of November 2025, cumulative notional volume 
reached $36.46 billion, meaning Polymarket processed $22.03 billion in 2025, a 57% YTD increase with one month still remaining. 
Polymarket’s growth in 2025 underscored Polygon’s strengths as an execution layer for consumer-scale applications. Polygon’s low fees and 
predictable execution allowed sustained, high-frequency transaction ﬂow to scale into the billions of dollars per month without degrading user 
experience. At that scale, Polymarket served as clear proof that Polygon can reliably support high-volume, consumer-facing activity, strengthening 
Polygon’s payments thesis heading into 2026. The same attributes that enabled Polymarket’s growth, low fees, stable execution, and deep 
stablecoin liquidity, underpin Polygon’s emerging role as a settlement environment for everyday payment ﬂows. 
2026 Outlook 
 
Polygon enters 2026 with a more credible payments narrative than at any prior point. Stablecoin rails, merchant processors, and consumer-facing 
ﬁntech integrations all expanded meaningfully through 2025, and the payments-app transfer data shows Polygon PoS absorbing real settlement 
volume rather than sporadic transactional bursts. 
               
 


---

## Page 167

166 
The payments app's throughput is already at over $1 billion per month. Tracked payments apps on Polygon PoS processed $369 million in 
January, $736 million in September, and exceeded $1 billion in October and November, totaling $6.4 billion in YTD transfer volume. Growth was 
broad-based across processors, including Avenia, Paxos, Revolut, BlindPay, Cobo, Coinﬂow, and a long tail of regional ﬁntechs. 
Stablecoin user penetration is deep and expanding. Stablecoin P2P unique addresses on Polygon have grown to 20.9 million, up from 8.1 million 
in 2024, while cumulative USDC/USDT addresses on Polygon PoS exceed 98 million (55.3 million USDC + 42.7 million USDT). This provides a 
large addressable base for apps that automate spending, cross-border transfers, and ﬁat-adjacent payments. 
Merchant and ﬁntech rails are consolidating around Polygon. Paxos-powered ﬂows (supporting entities like PayPal and Mercado Pago), Circle 
integrations, CEX-issued stablecoin ramps, LATAM neobanks, and on/oﬀ-ramp processors all increased in 2025. On the merchant/card side, 
processors such as MoonPay, Coinbase Commerce, Bitso, Lemon Cash, Buenbit, Rain, and Bank Frick are facilitating a growing share of ﬂows. 
Much of this activity now comes from non-crypto-native users transacting through custodial wallets and ﬁntech interfaces. As these channels 
scale, a growing share of Polygon’s stablecoin movement is routed through consumer apps, cards, CEX ramps, and payment processors rather 
than direct wallet-to-wallet transfers. 
In 2026, the focus shifts from proving that onchain payments work on Polygon to scaling them into a durable, multi-billion-dollar monthly 
category. With Q4 2025 already sustaining more than $1 billion in monthly throughput, the next phase is scaling this activity as processors deepen 
integrations and as ﬂows shift from one-oﬀ transactions into recurring consumer spend. Under this progression, Polygon’s monthly payments 
volume could reasonably reach the $2.5-$3.0 billion range in 2026, driven not by single breakthrough events but by the steady maturation of card 
programs, merchant rails, CEX ramps, and regional ﬁntech corridors. 
To stay up to date with Polygon, refer to Messari’s quarterly reports written by Jake Koch-Gallup (@immutablejacob). 
Tezos 
What Went Right in 2025 
Etherlink was Tezos’ clearest success in 2025, moving from a small-scale L2 with $1.5 million in TVL at the beginning of 2025 to the central hub 
for transactions, fee revenue, and DeFi by year-end. Year-to-date, Etherlink processed 49.6 million transactions versus 38.9 million on Tezos L1, 
accounting for 56% of total network activity. Over the same period, Etherlink generated 170,170 XTZ in fees compared to 48,100 XTZ on Tezos 
L1, generating 78% of protocol revenue. 
               
 


---

## Page 168

167 
 
DeFi TVL was one of the clearest indicators of users adopting Etherlink. DeFi TVL grew from $1.5 million at the start of 2025 to $67.7 million. 
Compared to Tezos L1’s TVL of $32.1 million, Etherlink’s TVL was 2.1x higher at year-end. Most of Etherlink’s TVL sat in a handful of core 
platforms. As of November 2025, Curve had $31.5 million in TVL, Superlend held $12.9 million, and the leading RWA protocols, Spiko and 
Uranium.io, together held $18.9 million. By year-end, most DeFi users on Tezos were participating on Etherlink rather than on the L1. 
User behavior in 2025 explains why Etherlink pulled ahead. Most new incentives (Apple Farm), DeFi deployments (Curve, Superlend), and RWA 
products (Spiko, Uranium.io) all launched on Etherlink, so liquidity and attention naturally followed. Additionally, EVM tooling made it easier for 
teams to migrate and for users to reuse existing wallets and workﬂows, while network upgrades reduced eﬀective fees and bridge times compared 
to interacting directly on the L1. Over the year, Etherlink averaged 4,610 daily active addresses, compared to 2,150 on the L1, as almost all new 
ﬁnancial activity chose the rollup. 
Additionally, the base layer evolved to support this setup. In 2025, Tezos reduced block times to 6 seconds, shortened cycles to roughly one day, 
and began paying bakers who ran DAL nodes. Shorter block times, faster cycles, and data availability backed by the Data Availability Layer 
(DAL) provide Etherlink with a faster and more secure settlement anchor for publishing and ﬁnalizing L2 activity. As a result, Tezos ended 2025 
               
 


---

## Page 169

168 
resembling an L2-ﬁrst stack, where Etherlink handles most of the execution and DeFi, and Tezos L1 focuses on security, governance, and data. 
2026 Outlook 
Tezos enters 2026 with Etherlink as the main activity hub and Tezos L1 as the settlement, governance, and DAL layer. The goal now is to scale 
Etherlink activity even further and surpass nine ﬁgures in TVL. 
In 2026, the priority will be to make Etherlink usage more stable and less dependent on event-driven campaigns, such as Apple Farm Season 1 and 
Season 2. On Etherlink, this means shifting from incentive-driven TVL to deeper liquidity in Curve, lending markets, and RWA assets, and 
potentially introducing new DeFi products like perps or vault strategies. On Tezos L1, the focus is shared infrastructure and governance that 
maintains Etherlink as the main liquidity and application hub. If those goals are met, Tezos would exit 2026 with Etherlink as a stable, mid-tier 
EVM environment and DeFi ecosystem with a base layer that supports Etherlink and any future Tezos X rollups. 
To stay up to date with Tezos, refer to Messari’s quarterly reports written by Jonny Kreiser. 
Stablechains 
Authors: Jake Koch-Gallup and Youssef Haidar 
Introduction 
Stablechains are purpose-built blockchains optimized for stablecoin use cases. They introduce features such as deterministic settlement, 
stablecoin-denominated fees, and complaint conﬁdentiality mechanisms. Rather than competing for existing crypto-native activity, their success 
depends on whether they can unlock new categories of users and payment ﬂows that are poorly served by general-purpose blockchains today. 
Viewed through this lens, we are selectively bullish. Stablechains that prioritize ﬁat integrations, regulatory compliance, and distribution are 
well-positioned to outperform their peers in the short to medium term. Arc and Tempo stand out within this cohort because they are built 
alongside the institutions and platforms most likely to adopt them. In contrast, many other stablechains are developing infrastructure in 
anticipation of demand, while Circle and Stripe already sit directly in the ﬂow of stablecoin issuance, payments, and settlement. 
We do not expect activity on general-purpose blockchains to drastically shift to stablechains in 2026. Solana, Base and similar networks will 
continue to capture speculative and DeFi activity, while TRON is likely to remain a major venue for inter-exchange and P2P USDT transfers, 
particularly in emerging markets, given its existing distribution and low-cost settlement. 
Arc and Tempo are eﬀectively competing with messaging networks like SWIFT, payment processors, and payment rails like ACH, not DeFi 
protocols. The degree of upside depends on these networks’ ability to bring these oﬀchain ﬂows onchain via purpose-built stablechains. 
Why Build a Stablechain? 
A natural objection is why Arc and Tempo need their own L1 instead of launching as Ethereum L2s or as applications on general-purpose 
               
 


---

## Page 170

169 
blockchains. The answer hinges on three recurring themes: control of the stack, alignment with ﬁat settlement norms, and strategic positioning. 
First, both Circle and Stripe view ﬁnancial infrastructure as a core component of their competitive advantage. If they deploy on another 
blockchain, the network architecture and roadmap may not prioritize payments or align with the ﬁrm's internal goals. More importantly, a 
meaningful share of economic value created by those payment ﬂows would accrue to an external base layer rather than to the issuer or platform 
originating the activity. By owning the L1, they can treat the chain as dedicated infrastructure, tailor it to their customers, and align value capture 
with the ﬂows they originate. 
Second, regulated ﬁnancial activity places very diﬀerent demands on settlement infrastructure than crypto-native applications. For banks, payment 
service providers (PSPs), and regulated ﬁntechs, settlement must be deterministic, costs must be predictable, and sensitive transaction data must 
be handled in a way that satisﬁes regulatory and contractual expectations. These properties are required to meet service-level agreements (SLAs), 
audit standards, and legal obligations. While L1s and L2s are slowly adopting features like gas abstraction and privacy, they still rely on 
probabilistic ﬁnality and force developers to depend on a patchwork of third-party middleware and compliance tooling. Stablechains can 
implement these features directly into the chain in ways that more closely mirror existing ﬁat settlement systems. 
Third, large enterprises require accountable counterparties rather than purely permissionless infrastructure. While a blockchain can provide 
transaction ﬁnality, it cannot oﬀer the dedicated support teams, compliance guarantees, and legal recourse that deﬁne enterprise relationships. 
Integrating Arc or Tempo bridges this gap by pairing onchain settlement with an SLA-backed operating model anchored to regulated partners such 
as Circle or Stripe. 
Taken together, these design and distribution choices point toward a more segmented blockchain landscape. General-purpose chains will continue 
to dominate speculative activity and crypto-native ecosystems. Stablechains, by contrast, will focus on settlement-heavy use cases where 
predictability, accountability, and integration with ﬁat systems are more important than open-ended composability. Arc and Tempo are currently 
the clearest expressions of this specialized design space. 
A Tale of Two Stablechains 
Although Arc and Tempo are both built around stablecoins, they target diﬀerent users and occupy distinct positions in the value chain. 
               
 


---

## Page 171

170 
 
Arc is Circle’s attempt to build an institutional operating system for tokenization, B2B payments, and institutional FX, with USDC at its monetary 
base. It targets highly regulated ﬁnancial institutions such as banks, broker-dealers, PSPs, and asset managers. For these users, Arc supports 
onchain issuance and settlement, conversion across ﬁat-backed stablecoins through StableFX, and cross-border payments within established 
regulatory and operational norms. 
Arc is also positioned favorably to banks and PSPs that directly compete with Stripe. A cross-border PSP, card network, or acquiring bank is 
unlikely to standardize settlement on a Stripe-aligned chain, but may be comfortable with Circle as a regulated and neutral issuer and 
infrastructure provider. That neutrality, combined with deterministic sub-second ﬁnality, opt-in conﬁdential transfers with view keys, and a 
permissioned validator set comprising known institutions, gives Arc a credible pitch as an institutional-grade L1. Arc has partnered with over 110 
issuers, payment ﬁrms, ﬁnancial institutions, analytics & RPC providers, exchanges, market makers, and developer tools. Arc is being shaped in 
direct collaboration with the institutions that are most likely to operate, govern, and settle meaningful volume on the network in production. 
               
 


---

## Page 172

171 
 
Tempo sits on the opposite end of the user spectrum. It is designed as a payments-ﬁrst chain that augments Stripe’s existing rails. Its natural users 
are Stripe-native merchants and platforms, ﬁntechs that do not directly compete with Stripe, online marketplaces, or payroll platforms that need 
predictable, low-cost payouts. From this perspective, Tempo plays the role of an onchain settlement layer rather than a general-purpose 
blockchain. 
Technically, Tempo’s architecture reinforces that positioning. The network eliminates the need for a native gas token by allowing fees to be paid 
directly in any supported USD-denominated stablecoin through an enshrined AMM, ensuring that merchants and PSPs can transact and settle in 
the same unit of account. At the token layer, the TIP-20 standard embeds compliance controls and payment metadata directly into the asset itself, 
allowing issuers to carry existing operational and regulatory workﬂows onchain. At the execution layer, dedicated payment lanes and a native 
stablecoin DEX help keep routine transfers predictable and eﬃciently routed, even during periods of elevated network activity. 
By abstracting away gas management, fee volatility, and payment congestion, Tempo removes many of the operational frictions that slow 
               
 


---

## Page 173

172 
enterprise adoption. As a result, its path to scale runs through embedded distribution rather than organic crypto-native usage. 
D.R.E.A.M (Distribution Rules Everything Around Me) 
Distribution is a key factor in determining success for a stablechain. Stripe already sits in the ﬂow of funds for millions of merchants and 
platforms. If “settle in stablecoins on Tempo” becomes an option in Stripe’s interface rather than a standalone integration, Tempo immediately 
gains access to a large user base that cares about settlement times and costs, not about crypto infrastructure. KlarnaUSD, launched as the ﬁrst 
bank-issued stablecoin on Tempo, is an early example of this pattern: a consumer ﬁntech that doesn’t compete directly with Stripe, using Tempo 
to improve cross-border payments for its existing customers. 
The segmentation between Arc and Tempo can be summarized: 
● Arc is best positioned for regulated institutional ﬂows. Its natural adopters are banks, asset managers, FX and capital markets 
infrastructures, and PSPs or card networks that compete with Stripe but are comfortable with Circle as a neutral issuer and infrastructure 
provider. For this group, deterministic ﬁnality, conﬁdential yet auditable transfers, and integration with yield-bearing collateral (USYC) and 
other tokenized assets are more important than transparency or composability. 
● Tempo is best positioned for Stripe-aligned users and startups. Its natural adopters are merchants, SaaS businesses, gig and marketplace 
platforms, and consumer ﬁntechs that already rely on Stripe or similar infrastructure. For them, the value lies in cheaper cross-border 
settlement, programmable payouts, and the ability to ignore gas tokens and FX routing entirely. 
Arc and Tempo are not direct competitors. While they both are stablechains, their distribution networks and user bases only partially overlap. The 
constraint for both is not throughput; it is convincing the right institutions and platforms to leverage their stablechain as a settlement rail in 
production rather than an R&D initiative. 
Not All Stablechains are Created Equal 
               
 


---

## Page 174

173 
 
Taken together, the diﬀerences in distribution and target users leave us cautiously optimistic about the stablechain sector. Stablechains that are 
tightly wired into real-world distribution via banks, PSPs, or large platforms, and native stablecoin integrations from the issuer have a plausible 
path to relevance. Arc and Tempo both clear that bar. Arc has a credible shot at becoming the default rail for institutional FX, tokenized assets, 
and B2B payments if Circle partners with leading ﬁnancial institutions and maintains regulatory goodwill. Tempo has a chance of becoming the 
optimal payment rail for payouts and merchant ﬂows if Stripe chooses to route even a modest share of its business through it and if regulators 
remain comfortable with consumer-facing stablecoin settlement. 
We are skeptical of stablechains that lack either issuer adjacency or real distribution, regardless of technical sophistication. Without corridors, 
direct mint and redeem access, and integration into existing ﬁat workﬂows, there is no clear path from technical eﬃciency to meaningful adoption 
as a settlement layer. Over the next year, this will make most stablechains look underwhelming relative to general-purpose blockchains on 
headline metrics, such as TVL and volume. 
The thesis for Arc and Tempo does not depend on near-term optics. It depends on whether, a few years from now, a meaningful share of the 
world’s FX, treasury, and payment ﬂows are settling on stablechains and not legacy systems. On that speciﬁc question, our view is that Arc and 
               
 


---

## Page 175

174 
Tempo are currently the two most credible contenders. 
Bitcoin Layers 
Author: AJC 
A Year of Disappointment 
One of the most disappointing sectors in 2025 has been Bitcoin layers, a broad category that includes L2s, sidechains, state chains, and other 
networks aligned with either Bitcoin (the network) or BTC (the asset). After the surge in activity around Ordinals (NFTs) and Runes (tokens) 
directly on Bitcoin in late 2023 and early 2024, momentum shifted toward protocols aimed at scaling Bitcoin or expanding its functionality. 
For the purposes of this report, networks that support a bridged version of BTC have been split into three diﬀerent categories: 
● Alt L1s & More (i.e., Ethereum, Solana, Arbitrum, etc.) - Networks that support bridged version(s) of BTC, but the underlying network is 
sovereign from the Bitcoin network. 
● Sidesystems (i.e., Stacks, Rootstock, Core, etc.) - Networks that support bridged versions(s) of BTC and provide oﬀchain execution for 
Bitcoin users. 
● Bitcoin Native (i.e., Lightning Network) - Networks that support bridged version(s) of BTC and allow users to unilaterally exit the oﬀchain 
system back to Bitcoin. 
Bridged BTC 
               
 


---

## Page 176

175 
 
Initially, there appeared to be strong demand for Bitcoin layers. At the beginning of the year, 47,900 BTC were bridged to Sidesystems, and an 
additional 5,100 BTC were bridged to Bitcoin Native protocols, totaling 53,000 BTC bridged to Bitcoin layers. Over the course of 2025, however, 
bridged BTC has steadily ﬂowed out of Sidesystems, as bridged BTC is down 22% to 37,500 BTC. 
Bridged BTC to Bitcoin Native protocols have grown modestly, up 6% to 5,400 BTC, but not nearly enough to oﬀset losses from side systems. 
Perhaps a bigger indictment is not the decline in bridged BTC to Bitcoin layers, but the fact that BTC bridged to Alt L1s & More increased over 
the same period, rising 12% to 327,500 BTC. 
Taken together, users are currently better served by Alt L1s & More for their BTC programmability needs. Despite renewed interest in 
Bitcoin-aligned execution environments, over 88% of all bridged BTC remains on networks that are sovereign from Bitcoin. 
               
 


---

## Page 177

176 
 
A closer look at Bitcoin layers reveals a widening divergence in performance across protocols. Merlin still holds the leading position for bridged 
BTC among Bitcoin layers, with 14,900 BTC. However, its share has gradually eroded, down 36% year-to-date, as a result of its “Very High” trust 
assumptions and limited diﬀerentiation relative to other Bitcoin layers. Absent major changes, it's unlikely that Merlin will be able to maintain its 
bridged BTC advantage over time. 
A notable standout in 2025 was Stacks. Bridged BTC on Stacks increased 269% year-to-date to 4,600 BTC, making it the third-largest Bitcoin 
layer by the amount of bridged BTC. This growth was driven primarily by the rollout of sBTC in the ﬁrst half of 2025, Stacks’ native bridged 
version of BTC, as detailed in our State of Stacks H1 2025 report. Outside of Stacks, no other Bitcoin layer experienced meaningful growth in 
bridged BTC. Bitcoin layers are increasingly competing for a shrinking pool of capital rather than growing the category as a whole. 
DeFi TVL 
               
 


---

## Page 178

177 
 
Bridged BTC, while an important metric, is not the sole measure of success for a Bitcoin layer. DeFi TVL is equally important, as it captures not 
only BTC that is being used productively rather than passively held, but also other onchain assets that participate in DeFi activity. 
 
Viewed through this lens, several insights emerge. Notably, the largest Bitcoin layer by DeFi TVL is Starknet. Despite having only 2,200 BTC 
bridged, Starknet leads all Bitcoin layers with $224.2 million in DeFi TVL. Starknet’s DeFi TVL has beneﬁted signiﬁcantly from its “BTCFi” 
initiative, which launched in October and allocated 100 million STRK ($11.7 million) in incentives toward BTC-related DeFi activity on the 
network. The second-largest Bitcoin layer by DeFi TVL is Rootstock, one of the oldest Bitcoin layers, which oﬀers a relative maturity advantage 
over newer entrants. The only other Bitcoin layer with more than $100 million in DeFi TVL is Stacks, which similarly beneﬁts from its longer 
operating history. 
As a whole, however, 2025 was a diﬃcult year for DeFi TVL across Bitcoin layers. The total DeFi TVL has declined 67% year-to-date, falling 
from $2.25 billion to $753.2 million. While multiple factors contributed to this contraction, a primary driver has been the exhaustion of incentive 
               
 


---

## Page 179

178 
programs launched by various Bitcoin layers in 2023 and 2024. As these incentives waned, mercenary capital, largely motivated by yield rather 
than a desire to hold or earn passive returns on BTC, migrated toward more attractive opportunities outside of Bitcoin layers. 
2026 Outlook 
 
So far, Starknet’s BTCFi initiative appears to be gaining traction. At the beginning of 2025, just $9.3 million of BTC was bridged to Starknet. 
Following the launch of BTCFi, that ﬁgure increased by 2,055% to $201.1 million. Excluding STRK, BTC derivatives are now the largest bridged 
asset on Starknet, surpassing stablecoins ($155.5 million), ETH derivatives ($86.4 million), and RWAs ($2.8 million). That said, incentives are a 
meaningful driver of this activity, and it remains to be seen whether this demand persists once incentives taper. 
Ultimately, Starknet’s success hinges on its ability to bridge the gap between Ethereum’s mature DeFi ecosystem and BTC. If it can consistently 
route BTC liquidity into Ethereum-native applications while maintaining a user experience and risk proﬁle that BTC holders are willing to accept, 
Starknet could emerge as a critical execution environment. In doing so, it would occupy a unique position within the Bitcoin ecosystem, carving 
               
 


---

## Page 180

179 
out a defensible and highly valuable niche that few other Bitcoin layers are structurally capable of addressing. 
 
Outside of Starknet, another area to monitor for the Bitcoin ecosystem are the upcoming launches of Alpen and Citrea. Alpen is a proposed 
validity rollup on Bitcoin that uses a SNARK-based system to prove the correctness of batched state transitions, while Citrea follows a similar 
approach, combining a zkVM with recursive SNARKs to compress and prove oﬀchain execution. Alpen and Citrea will likely be the ﬁrst 
Bitcoin-native rollups to reach mainnet. What diﬀerentiates rollups from earlier Bitcoin layers is that they aim to make Bitcoin the canonical 
source of truth for system state, rather than merely an asset used within an oﬀchain execution environment. Although they still won’t be able to 
guarantee complete trustlessness, they represent the most direct attempts to bring trust-minimized, execution-layer scalability to Bitcoin while 
preserving its settlement guarantees. 
               
 


---

## Page 181

180 
Infrastructure Trends 
Authors: Austin Weiler, Youssef Haidar, and Alice Hou 
Oracles 
In 2025, the oracle sector shifted towards institutional use cases and continued to be led by Chainlink in total value secured (TVS). 
 
Chainlink’s TVS market share increased from 52% ($34.1 billion) on January 1 to 62% ($48.6 billion) on November 30. That growth was driven 
primarily by the expansion of Aave V3. Aave accounts for 67% ($31.5 billion) of Chainlink’s TVS. Aave’s TVL increased 60% year-to-date from 
$19.7 billion to $31.5 billion, directly lifting Chainlink’s TVS. 
Chainlink’s TVS also beneﬁted from TRON discontinuing its use of the WINkLink oracle and migrating to Chainlink on May 15. That transition 
added $5.5 billion in TVS as Chainlink began securing JustLend, TRON’s largest DeFi application at the time. The shift reshaped the oracle 
               
 


---

## Page 182

181 
landscape. WINkLink entered 2025 as the third-largest oracle by TVS with a 10% ($6.4 billion) market share on January 1, but its share has since 
fell to 0%. 
Outside of Chainlink and WINkLink, the competitive picture remained largely static. Chronicle held second place throughout the year, with TVS 
market share increasing modestly from 10% ($7.1 billion) on January 1 to 11% ($8.8 billion) on November 30. Pyth moved in the opposite 
direction, with TVS market share declining from 9% ($6.2 billion) to 6% ($4.5 billion) over the same time frame. 
In the second half of the year, oracle providers expanded beyond their main role of securing onchain protocols into delivering ﬁnancial data for 
institutional use cases. This shift reﬂected a broader push to compete with incumbent market data providers such as Bloomberg Terminal and S&P 
Global Market Intelligence by oﬀering low-latency crypto and traditional ﬁnancial data that can be consumed both onchain and oﬀchain. 
Pyth moved directly into this opportunity. On September 24, Pyth launched Pyth Pro, a permissioned data service for $10,000 per month that 
oﬀers 1ms update latency across more than 2,200 price feeds, including crypto assets, equities, futures, commodities, and interest rates. Adoption 
accelerated quickly as in Q3, Pyth Pro had 28 active subscribers, up from eight in Q2, pointing to early institutional demand and initial signs of 
PMF. 
Institutional demand matters because data budgets at traditional ﬁnancial ﬁrms materially exceed what DeFi usage alone can support. In 2025, a 
single Bloomberg Terminal cost roughly $25,000–$30,000 per year per user, and Bloomberg Terminal generated an estimated $10.6 billion in 
revenue in 2024. If oracle providers capture even a small portion of that revenue by replacing or complementing incumbent data services, 
institutional revenue will surpass what oracles generate today from securing DeFi protocols alone. 
Chainlink pursued a broader strategy in 2025 focused on institutional and DeFi data connectivity. Throughout the year, Chainlink partnered with 
multiple ﬁnancial institutions to bring regulated assets and market data onchain. This included its June 24 partnership with Mastercard, which 
enabled more than three billion cardholders to purchase crypto assets directly onchain through the Chainlink-powered Swapper app. Also on 
August 11, Chainlink partnered with ICE, the parent company of the NYSE, to bring forex and precious metals data onchain. Together, these 
partnerships expanded Chainlink’s role beyond a DeFi oracle into an institutional data oracle that can support regulated ﬁnancial products across 
blockchains. 
In 2026, we believe Chainlink will continue to dominate onchain TVS through its entrenchment across large DeFi protocols, while institutional 
data services and partnerships will emerge as the primary revenue growth driver for oracle providers. 
Cross-chain / Interoperability 
In 2025, the cross-chain landscape shifted toward trust-minimized security models, native asset interoperability, and institutional-grade 
infrastructure. 
               
 


---

## Page 183

182 
 
THORChain made up 67% of all cross-chain volume year-to-date. The protocol launched an enshrined oracle, eliminating dependencies on 
external oracles and reducing systemic risks for users. TRON and Solana are now supported networks, signiﬁcantly widening the protocol’s 
addressable market. THORChain’s dominance in 2025 was bolstered by a surge in volume following the $1.5 billion security breach of the Bybit 
exchange in February. During the week ending March 2, the protocol processed a record $4.66 billion in swaps, including over $1 billion in a 
single day, as perpetrators utilized THORChain’s permissionless infrastructure to move assets. Despite the ﬂows illicit nature, the event served as 
a high-stakes “stress test,” demonstrating the protocol’s ability to facilitate multi-billion, non-custodial swaps with zero downtime. 
A rising star in 2025 was NEAR Intents. NEAR Intents processed $6.8 billion in cross-chain volume, surging 57,055% year-to-date. In total, 
NEAR Intents made up 15% of YTD cross-chain volume. The surge in demand was driven by a desire for permissionless interoperability 
infrastructure and one-click swaps that abstract away the complexities of cross-chain transactions. Users sign the transactions they want, and a 
solver network competes to source liquidity across chains and execute the route, so the wallet can deliver a single swap experience instead of a 
multi-step cross-chain ﬂow. 
THORChain and NEAR Intents have captured 83% of all cross-chain volume year-to-date, signaling strong user preferences toward 
               
 


---

## Page 184

183 
trust-minimized chain abstraction. 
LayerZero increased its cross-chain volume by 809% year-to-date. The team acquired Stargate for $110 million in August 2025. The acquisition 
transforms ZRO into a productive asset by redirecting 50% of Stargate’s revenues toward buybacks, a ﬁgure set to rise to 100% in March 2026. 
This tethers the token’s performance directly to real-world usage, ensuring that every dollar of bridge volume generates persistent, non-speculative 
demand for ZRO. 
LayerZero’s omnichain fungible token (OFT) standard has been well-received by exchanges and asset issuers. In December 2025, Kraken 
partnered with LayerZero to power its wrapped bitcoin product, kBTC. Beyond crypto-native assets, LayerZero is making inroads within the Real 
World Asset (RWA) sector. PayPal adopted the standard to unify PYUSD liquidity across Ethereum and Solana, ensuring a seamless user 
experience for global payments. Similarly, IQ and Frax utilized the tech to launch KRWQ, the ﬁrst multi-chain Korean Won stablecoin, while 
Dinari integrated it to enable compliant, cross-chain transfers for tokenized securities. 
In March 2025, Circle released Cross-Chain Transfer Protocol (CCTP) V2. This upgrade shifted USDC from a collection of isolated network 
tokens into a truly uniﬁed global balance, no longer relying on fragmented liquidity pools and vulnerable wrapped assets. 
With the introduction of "Hooks," cross-chain composability evolved from a multi-step friction point into an invisible infrastructure layer, 
enabling users to sign transactions that bridge funds and immediately deploy them on another chain. This removes the need to wait or switch 
networks, making the entire cross-chain process feel like a single, instant interaction.Wormhole transitioned away from a wrapped token model to 
support native tokens using the Native Token Transfer (NTT) standard. NTT was the backbone for the launch of Monad’s native bridge. Sunrise, a 
gateway that uniﬁes liquidity between Solana and Monad, demonstrated the power of NTT, enabling instant, native liquidity at launch without the 
fragmentation typically seen in bootstrapping new ecosystems. 
Chainlink continued to fortify its institutional moat. In December 2025, Chainlink’s CCIP was selected by Coinbase as the exclusive bridge 
infrastructure for over $7 billion in wrapped assets, such as cbBTC and cbETH. This partnership further reinforces Chainlink as an industry 
standard for institutional cross-chain infrastructure. 
In 2026, we anticipate that the sector will increasingly focus on privacy-preserving interoperability. THORChain’s upcoming Monero integration 
will enable users to swap between transparent assets, such as BTC, and XMR, without the need for centralized intermediaries. Simultaneously, 
Chainlink’s Conﬁdential Compute will enable institutions to verify sensitive and proprietary data within secure enclaves (TEEs) while settling 
results onchain, bridging the gap between the need for privacy and auditability when it launches next year. 
Privacy 
Privacy has long been one of crypto’s most compelling narratives. It is recognized as important in principle, yet historically underprioritized in 
practice. For years, privacy was treated as a niche capability at best and a regulatory risk at worst, often associated with money laundering or 
attempts to evade oversight. 
               
 


---

## Page 185

184 
That perception is beginning to shift. While uncertainty remains, the regulatory environment around privacy tools has become more friendly. In 
March 2025, OFAC removed Tornado Cash from its sanctions list, even as co-founder Roman Storm was later convicted of operating an 
unlicensed money transmitting business, and jurors deadlocked on the money laundering and sanctions charges. In December 2025, President 
Trump also expressed willingness to consider a pardon for Keonne Rodriguez, the CEO of the privacy-focused Bitcoin wallet Samourai. 
Onchain activity is also beginning to reﬂect this shift in sentiment. Market interest has returned to privacy networks, and established projects like 
Zcash have experienced a sharp surge in shielded value since late 2024. The price of ZEC climbed over 672% YoY from $60 to $459, and 
shielded ZEC now accounts for 65%of total TVL across the privacy sector. 
 
This resurgence indicates that the need for privacy is no longer theoretical. As more value moves onchain, full transparency shifts from an 
advantage to a liability. Broadcasting positions, counterparties, execution prices, or treasury movements in real time is not viable for large market 
participants. Traditional ﬁnance has long relied on dark pools and OTC desks to protect sensitive trading information. As traditional assets are 
increasingly tokenized and institutional investors continue to experiment with onchain ﬁnance, privacy is becoming a requirement rather than an 
               
 


---

## Page 186

185 
optional feature. 
Earlier eﬀorts to bring privacy onchain struggled because they imposed high costs on users and developers. Users were often required to leave 
familiar ecosystems, pay additional bridging costs, or accept a degraded user experience. Developers faced the need to learn new languages or 
build within isolated stacks that lacked interoperability with the broader DeFi landscape. Privacy on its own was rarely a compelling product. 
Privacy paired with a seamless onchain experience is. 
In 2025, the design approach is shifting. Privacy is increasingly being built as embedded infrastructure rather than as a separate destination. 
Instead of forcing migration, projects are integrating privacy into environments where users already operate. The Ethereum Foundation has 
launched initiatives focused on wallet-level and application-level opt-in privacy, aiming to make privacy feel native rather than an add-on. Its 
Kohaku initiative integrates protocols like Railgun into an open source SDK to target default privacy across Ethereum-connected wallets such as 
MetaMask. At the same time, protocols such as Oasis and Phala are positioning privacy as a modular tool that can be integrated directly into 
existing applications. 
Technical progress is also accelerating. Zama has launched the fhEVM coprocessor, allowing developers to write conﬁdential smart contracts in 
Solidity while operating on encrypted data. Privacy Pools introduce the concept of association sets, enabling users to prove that funds are not 
linked to illicit activity without revealing full transaction histories. Outside of crypto, Google Wallet has adopted zero-knowledge proofs (ZKPs) 
for age veriﬁcation, allowing users to prove eligibility without disclosing personal data. 
Privacy in 2025 is not about absolute anonymity. It must be context-aware, programmable, and capable of supporting regulatory requirements. The 
protocols most likely to succeed will not pursue complete opacity, but will enable selective disclosure that is both practical and secure. Achieving 
this will require continued progress in protocol design, policy alignment, and user experience, so that privacy becomes an expected standard of 
digital systems rather than a radical exception. 
Decentralized Internet Finance 
Authors: Sam Ruskin, AJC, and Eric Manoukian 
Key Themes 
● Prop AMMs and CLOBs will replace passive AMMs as the standard DEX architecture, oﬀering better execution and tighter spreads as 
onchain infra scales. 
● Modular lending protocols, such as Morpho, will outpace monolithic lenders by oﬀering ﬂexible, isolated vaults that are preferred by 
institutions and neobanks. 
● Equity perps are positioned to break out in 2026, oﬀering global, high-leverage access to stocks while sidestepping oﬀchain regulatory 
               
 


---

## Page 187

186 
friction. 
● Yield-bearing stablecoins will displace passive stables as core DeFi collateral, closing the gap between reserve yield and user returns. 
● DeﬁBanks will emerge as crypto’s answer to neobanks, bundling save, spend, and borrow features into self-custodial, high-margin apps. 
Introduction 
DeFi is no longer niche. The space has scaled with real users, meaningful revenue, and sharper protocol teams focused on sustainable value 
capture. DeFi no longer feels like a chaotic frontier; it feels like a professionalized battleﬁeld. 
Part of the reason this cycle felt so diﬀerent is that the controlling players have changed. TradFi recreated DeFi’s surface features, packaged them 
as compliant instruments, and sold them to retail under their own brand. These shifts are not failures of DeFi, but signals of its maturity. 
Institutions like Robinhood, Stripe, and BlackRock are not embracing DeFi out of ideology. They are reacting to its superior margins, faster 
iteration, and threat to their legacy business models. Like any good trade, once validated, it becomes crowded, and asymmetric upside is harder to 
ﬁnd. 
We predict that the next era of DeFi will not be purely deﬁned by yield or speculation, as was the case in previous cycles. It will be deﬁned by its 
ability to withstand geopolitical whiplash, growing economic insecurity, and bad‑faith actors exploiting crypto for personal gain. 
Spot DEXs are DeFi's Most Competitive Sector 
Spot Volumes Hit ATHs in 2025 
               
 


---

## Page 188

187 
 
Decentralized exchanges (DEXs) continued to gain ground against centralized exchanges (CEXs) in 2025. Year-to-date, DEX volumes reached 
$4.53 trillion, equivalent to 16% of CEX volumes ($29.04 trillion). This marks the third straight year in which DEX volume growth has outpaced 
CEX volume growth. DEX volumes were 10% of CEX volumes in 2024 and 8% in 2023. As blockchains continue to scale and infrastructure 
improves, more volume moving onchain remains the most sureﬁre trend for spot DEXs. 
Year-over-year, daily average spot DEX volumes are up an impressive amount as well. In 2024, the daily average volume was $7.04 billion, while 
in 2025 it has climbed to $13.51 billion, a 92% increase. Memecoin mania crescendoed in January with the launch of TRUMP, which brieﬂy set 
an all-time monthly volume record at $556.52 billion. That high was later eclipsed in October ($563.74 billion) after the market reached a local 
peak and experienced the largest deleveraging event on record on October 10, 2025. 
               
 


---

## Page 189

188 
 
Ethereum no longer represents the majority of onchain volume the way it did in past cycles. Over the past two years, both Solana and BNB Chain 
have emerged as premier venues for spot trading, accounting for 26% and 20% of onchain volume over the last three months. Ethereum and its 
L2s are not far behind, at 19% and 17%, respectively. Even so, as volumes become more distributed across blockchains, competition for them has 
never been ﬁercer. 
               
 


---

## Page 190

189 
 
Uniswap remains the top DEX by volume, yet its overall market share has gradually declined. In 2024, Uniswap accounted for 28% of all DEX 
volumes; in 2025, that share dropped to 21%. Notably, this trend isn’t just the result of volumes shifting away from Ethereum. Uniswap is also 
facing increased competition on Ethereum itself. In Q4’24, Uniswap V2 and V3 combined for 66% of Ethereum DEX volume, but so far in 
Q4’25, Uniswap V2, V3, and V4 together account for only 50%. The biggest driver has been the emergence of Fluid as a leading venue for 
stablecoin swaps. In Q4’25, Fluid captured 21% of Ethereum DEX volume, up dramatically from just 3% in Q4’24. 
Volumes on Solana have also undergone a dramatic shift in 2025. In Q4'24, Raydium accounted for nearly half of Solana’s volumes, at 47%. In 
Q4’25, however, Raydium’s share fell to 15%. Raydium’s share of volume has declined as a result of other DEXs growing signiﬁcantly on Solana, 
such as prop AMMs. The leading prop AMM, HumidiFi, now ranks third across all DEXs by volume over the past three months and has become 
the top DEX on Solana. 
Outside of the top individual DEXs, there’s also a signiﬁcant long tail of protocols capturing a meaningful share. Over the past three months, 
DEXs outside the top six have averaged $6.82 billion in daily volume, or roughly 43% of all DEX activity during that period. Despite DEX 
volumes consistently growing year over year, no single protocol has emerged as the clear, dominant winner. Instead, the market continues to 
               
 


---

## Page 191

190 
exhibit a pattern of rotation, where each year a new DEX seemingly emerges, gains traction, and establishes a signiﬁcant market share, only to 
lose market share thereafter. The result is an ecosystem where growth is broad-based, competition is ﬂuid, and incumbency provides far less 
advantage than in previous cycles. 
 
Interestingly, despite DEX volumes reaching all-time highs, total DEX TVL has actually decreased in 2025. On January 1, DEX TVL sat at 
$22.36 billion; it has since fallen by 21% to $17.62 billion. Part of the reason for this has been greater capital eﬃciency. In 2024, spot DEXs 
averaged a daily turnover rate of 36% (daily volume divided by DEX TVL). In 2025, average daily turnover has nearly doubled, reaching 63% so 
far, meaning each dollar of liquidity is supporting almost twice as much trading activity as last year. 
There are two main reasons behind this surge in daily turnover. First, as blockchains scale and transaction costs continue to fall, the range of 
economically viable trading opportunities expands dramatically. When transactions cost $1,000, many arbitrage paths or microstructure 
ineﬃciencies simply cannot be captured. However, when the same transaction costs a penny, countless small opportunities become actionable, 
resulting in more trades, arbitrage, and volume throughout the ecosystem. 
               
 


---

## Page 192

191 
 
The second reason has been the shift away from traditional AMMs to prop AMMs and CLOBs. Prop AMMs are programmable liquidity pools 
that utilize oﬀchain price oracles for real-time price discovery to oﬀer tighter spreads and better prices for traders. Furthermore, since Prop AMMs 
don’t oﬀer passive liquidity, it also means they don’t have any TVL. Prop AMMs have had the most traction on Solana, whose high throughput, 
low latency, and cheap transaction costs have enabled them to ﬂourish. As mentioned earlier, HumidiFi, the largest Prop AMM on Solana, has 
quickly risen to third-largest DEX by volume in all of crypto. 
 
The other DEX architecture that has gained meaningful traction in 2025 is the Central Limit Order Book (CLOB). CLOBs are the default trading 
structure for CEXs and are, by design, the most eﬃcient matching model for price discovery. However, they require active market making, 
something that historically has been extremely diﬃcult to support onchain. High transaction costs and slow block times made it nearly impossible 
to run a performant, low-latency orderbook on most blockchains. 
Take Ethereum, for example. With a 12-second block time, providing liquidity on a CLOB is inherently risky. A market maker’s quote can easily 
               
 


---

## Page 193

192 
become stale before it is included in a block, increasing the risk of toxic ﬂow. And if the price moves against you, you are essentially forced into a 
bidding war with MEV bots to cancel your order in time, driving up gas costs and reducing the economic viability of quoting at all. These 
constraints meant that, for most of crypto’s history, onchain CLOBs simply couldn’t compete with AMM designs. 
 
This dynamic began to shift with Hyperliquid’s introduction of “cancel prioritization,” a mechanism that lowers execution risk for market makers 
by ensuring their cancel orders are processed ﬁrst. This innovation made quoting on an onchain CLOB far more practical and helped revive 
volume in orderbook-based DEX architectures in 2025. 
Combined, Prop AMMs and CLOBs facilitated $57.18 billion in volume in November, accounting for 14% of activity over that period. While still 
a minority share, this slice has been steadily growing as traders look for tighter spreads and better execution than traditional AMMs can oﬀer. If 
spot DEXs are going to keep gaining ground on CEXs, Prop AMMs and CLOBs will need to take on a larger share of onchain volume, especially 
for major assets like BTC, ETH, SOL, and stablecoins. These are the pairs where execution quality matters most, and where an improved market 
structure can have the greatest impact on overall DEX competitiveness compared to CEXs. 
Fortunately, Prop AMMs on Solana and Hyperliquid’s spot orderbook have already shown that it’s possible to build onchain markets that can 
compete with CEXs. As a result, these market types are likely to expand beyond the ecosystems where they ﬁrst encountered PMF. Ethereum L2s 
are a natural next step for Prop AMMs, and ultra-fast, low-latency chains like MegaETH should, in theory, be capable of supporting a fully 
onchain CLOB. Just as we saw with the AMMs of earlier cycles, there’s no guarantee that the leaders in Prop AMMs or spot CLOBs today will be 
the leaders of tomorrow. 
Building Moats 
               
 


---

## Page 194

193 
 
Spot DEXs are, without a doubt, one of the most competitive markets in crypto. With new entrants appearing every year and no single protocol 
consistently able to grow its market share, it’s worth taking a closer look at where value actually accrues in this sector. The volumes are large, and 
the activity is real, but the question of who captures the upside is far less straightforward. Examining the broader ecosystem around spot DEXs, 
wallets, trading bots, launchpads, aggregators, and the DEX protocols themselves helps clarify where economic moats can actually be built. 
The most commoditized segment of the stack is DEX aggregators. While DEX aggregators like Jupiter, 1inch, and CoWSwap provide a very 
useful service, it is a service that is tough to monetize. For example, Jupiter facilitated $47.40 billion in volume in November but generated only 
$660,000 in revenue from spot-related activity (with over 90% of its revenue instead coming from its perps protocol). That translates to an 
abysmally small margin of just 0.0014%. Despite commanding substantial volume, the economics of aggregation naturally push revenues toward 
zero as aggregators compete on routing quality and fees. 
Better, but still not great, businesses in the DEX stack are the DEXs themselves. Meteora, Raydium, PancakeSwap, and Fluid, all behemoths in 
terms of onchain volume, operated at margins of just 0.01% to 0.02% in November. A slight outlier was Aerodrome, which posted a margin of 
               
 


---

## Page 195

194 
0.07%. Importantly, though, Aerodrome runs a ve(3,3) model and distributes far higher incentives ($132.16 million in annualized token emissions) 
than traditional AMMs. In that context, the higher margin may simply be necessary to oﬀset the higher incentive burden. 
Nevertheless, DEXs only become viable businesses at an extreme scale. With margins hovering in the single-digit basis-point range, the only way 
to generate real revenue is to push massive volumes, and even then, competitive pressure makes it hard to build durable moats purely from spot 
trading fees. 
Ultimately, we believe there are three primary moats that can be built around spot DEXs. The ﬁrst are wallets. Since wallets are closest to the user, 
they can eﬀectively monetize by oﬀering convenience. Some users prefer not to open a browser within their wallet, type in a DEX or aggregator 
URL, connect their wallet, and navigate through multiple interfaces. They’d much rather tap twice and buy or sell whatever asset they want. It’s a 
simple business, but compared to the rest of the DEX stack, it has a surprisingly high margin. 
In November, Phantom generated $9.46 million in revenue on $993.2 million in swap volume, a margin of 0.95%. And despite not even clearing 
$1 billion in monthly volume, Phantom earned more revenue than every single DEX except Aerodrome. That is, in essence, the power of 
distribution and convenience. Even modest volumes can translate into meaningful revenue when you sit directly on the user’s path. 
The second moat, trading bots, sits at the opposite end of the spectrum. While wallets eﬀectively monetize unsophisticated retail through 
convenience, trading bots monetize sophisticated retail through execution. The saying “time is money” has never been more literal than in crypto, 
especially for users trading highly volatile assets like memecoins. A diﬀerence measured in milliseconds, not seconds, can determine whether a 
trade nets hundreds of thousands in proﬁt or ends in a total loss. Bots that can consistently guarantee top-tier execution can justify charging much 
higher margins for that service. Axiom is a prime example. In November, it generated $18.74 million in revenue on $1.63 billion in volume, a 
margin of 1.15%, the highest among this entire cohort. 
The last moat is asset issuance, which is currently best exempliﬁed through memecoin launchpads. While a user may not necessarily care whether 
their cbBTC purchase is routed through a DEX on Ethereum or a DEX on Solana, assuming they get the same execution, that same user will 
absolutely care where a memecoin is launched. As a mental exercise, imagine launching today’s viral memecoin on pump.fun on Solana, as well 
as on Fanko.tech on Sanko (yes, that’s a thing). Which one do you think ends up with the higher market cap? 
Launchpads have proven to be high-margin businesses because they oﬀer users distinct, unique assets that can’t be easily replicated elsewhere. For 
this reason, they can charge surprisingly high margins. Pump.fun generated the most revenue in this group, reaching $34.92 million in November, 
with a margin of 0.51%. Four.meme, a launchpad on BNB Chain, maintains an even higher margin of 1.05%, partly because it has been endorsed 
by Binance. 
While this logic is currently best exempliﬁed by memecoins, we believe it will expand to all net-new assets. A few DEXs are already moving in 
this direction. Uniswap recently unveiled its Continuous Clearing Auction (CCA) protocol, and Aerodrome launched Ignition, both of which 
allow projects to launch their tokens directly through the respective DEX. 
Looking ahead to 2026, capturing durable value in spot DEXs increasingly requires verticalization. Participants across the stack will bundle 
               
 


---

## Page 196

195 
execution, distribution, and issuance into increasingly uniﬁed products. While these eﬀorts will originate from diﬀerent starting points, user-facing 
platforms like wallets versus execution-centric protocols like DEXs and aggregators, the end goal is to control more of the trading lifecycle to 
capture value beyond spot fees. 
Separately, this shift toward vertical integration opens the door to new monetization models. As reliance on basis-point fees becomes less viable, 
leading platforms may experiment with subscriptions or premium execution tiers. In 2026, the ability to move beyond transaction-based revenue 
may prove to be a key diﬀerentiator between protocols that merely facilitate activity and those that capture lasting value. 
Isolated Lending vs. Modular Lending 
 
Lending and borrowing in DeFi have long been dominated by monolithic protocols, such as Aave. However, over the past year, modular designs 
like Morpho and Euler have gained traction, posing the ﬁrst credible challenge to Aave’s dominance on Ethereum. 
               
 


---

## Page 197

196 
Strictly speaking, monolithic lending protocols use shared liquidity pools where all deposits fund all loans across supported assets. For example, 
USDC deposits can back loans collateralized by ETH, WBTC, or others. This design enables deep liquidity and low-maintenance capital 
deployment, but it also introduces protocol-wide risk. If one collateral asset fails, it can impact the solvency of the entire system, which is why 
monolithic protocols are conservative in what assets are supported to be supplied, used as collateral, or borrowed, as well as the parameters, 
including max loan-to-value, max borrow, etc. 
Modular lending protocols take a diﬀerent approach by separating risk across isolated lending markets. Each market or vault operates 
independently, with custom parameters often managed by third-party curators. If a vault with risky collateral generates bad debt, it does not 
impact other vaults, such as those based on ETH and USDC. This structure enables ﬂexible experimentation with a wide range of risk proﬁles 
while maintaining the integrity of the broader system. 
 
The long tail of assets continues to grow, and with it, demand to borrow against them. Modular lending protocols are best positioned to serve this 
market. While modular lending grew 40% year to date, its share still trails far behind monolithic protocols, which remain dominant despite a 5% 
               
 


---

## Page 198

197 
decline. 
 
The top Aave markets are ETH/BTC wrappers and stablecoins. This is partially due to the power law of onchain lending. The majority of 
collateral consists of BTC, ETH, and USDC/T. Additionally, Aave has only 120,800 monthly active users, whereas Morpho has 311,700 users. 
This suggests that the average wallet on Aave has more collateral and may beneﬁt from deeper liquidity for major assets. 
That said, modular lending is poised for growth. The rise of RWA-backed loans, competitive stablecoin yields, and distribution through strategic 
partnerships will likely drive more activity toward customizable, isolated lending markets. 
               
 


---

## Page 199

198 
 
Aave currently leads in RWA deposits, with $360 million, compared to Morpho, which has $240 million. We predict demand for RWA borrowing 
will increase in 2026 and believe Morpho’s model has structural advantages for lenders and RWA issuers. 
Aave operates via diﬀerent markets, or instances of its protocol that are entirely separate from one another. Aave’s V3 Horizon deployment is 
permissionless at the protocol level, but access to the underlying RWA tokens is permissioned. Markets are siloed from its main protocol, due to 
compliance requirements and to create diﬀerent risk proﬁles. Lenders in Horizon cannot tap into the broader liquidity of V3 Core but still assume 
shared risk across all RWAs within Horizon. In contrast, deployments like Aave V3 Prime and V3 Core exist to isolate risk proﬁles without 
restricting access through permissioning. 
Morpho takes a diﬀerent approach. The base protocol remains permissionless, while compliance is handled at the vault and market level. Issuers 
control access to their RWA vaults, enabling them to meet regulatory requirements while protecting lenders from cross-asset contagion. This 
structure enables greater ﬂexibility for institutions and more granular risk segmentation, albeit at the tradeoﬀ of albeit at the tradeoﬀ of fragmented 
liquidity and a higher burden on issuers to manage risk, compliance, and user access on a vault-by-vault basis. 
               
 


---

## Page 200

199 
 
Over the last year, Morpho vaults have yielded better returns on USDC compared to monolithic lending protocols. On Aave or Compound, risk 
parameters are conservative to protect the entire pool of assets. Because Morpho’s markets have isolated risk, vault curators can set more 
aggressive loan-to-value ratios and get higher leverage. Users of modular lending protocols enjoy greater ﬂexibility due to the variety of vault 
curators and a structural advantage that allows them to seek higher yields (with higher risk) if desired. 
               
 


---

## Page 201

200 
 
Finally, modular lending platforms can serve as backend liquidity layers for CEXs and neobanks. They oﬀer capital-eﬃcient infrastructure for 
funds, custodians, and institutions, facilitating distribution and making it easier to oﬀer lending and borrowing without building proprietary rails. 
The partnership between Morpho and Coinbase has already driven nearly $1 billion in new deposits to Morpho. Coinbase reportedly custodies 
more than 2.3 million BTC (over 10% of the total supply), and of that supply, 1.1 million BTC are customer-facing (indvidiuals and Coinbase 
Prime), pointing to a $100 billion addressable market for BTC-backed lending. 
Morpho has also secured integrations with platforms like Lemon, and promotes its SDK as a plug-and-play solution for institutions seeking to 
access onchain credit markets. Given these distribution channels and institutional tailwinds, we expect modular lending protocols like Morpho to 
outpace monolithic models such as Aave. 
The Future is Equity Perps 
Perpetual futures have cemented themselves as one of crypto’s most mainstream products. They oﬀer global, 24/7 access to synthetic leverage on 
               
 


---

## Page 202

201 
any asset, with no need for custody, settlement, or centralized clearing. Onchain perps now routinely settle hundreds of billions in monthly 
volume, driven by crypto-native traders seeking directional exposure, yield, or hedging instruments. Unlike other DeFi use cases that rely on 
slower retail adoption, perps found immediate product-market ﬁt among speculators and power users. 
 
Hyperliquid has emerged as the category leader. Since launching HyperCore in 2023, the protocol has processed over $2.8 trillion in volume 
year-to-date, capturing nearly 40% of the perp DEX market. This dominance is largely due to its performant CLOB engine, expansive asset 
listings, and smart use of airdrops and point incentives to bootstrap liquidity. Today, Hyperliquid ranks among the most proﬁtable DeFi apps on 
any chain, with transaction fees and taker spreads generating sustained revenue across cycles. Its growth has helped normalize perps as a core part 
of the crypto stack, alongside DEXs, lending, and staking. Looking ahead to 2026, we anticipate that equity perps will emerge as a major new 
theme, and Hyperliquid will may attract the majority of the ﬂow. However, don’t rule out Lighter, Aster, and edgeX, as they’ve carved out a 
sizeable market share for themselves at the end of 2025. 
               
 


---

## Page 203

202 
 
According to Cboe, 0DTEs (options contracts that expire the same day) have grown from 24% of total SPX option volume in 2021 to 60% as of 
May 2025. This reﬂects a shift in retail behavior toward high-leverage, short-duration trades, as opposed to passive exposure. Traders, especially 
younger and highly online demographics, appear to be gravitating toward faster, more reﬂexive market exposure, where outcomes depend heavily 
on timing and the behavior of other participants. In the crypto world, we often refer to this as PvP trading. 
               
 


---

## Page 204

203 
Perps improve on the 0DTE model by oﬀering a simpler, global, always-on instrument for short-term directional bets. They trade continuously, 
avoid the complexity of multi-dimensional option pricing, and can represent any equity or index. As perps have become more popular, we’ve seen 
more instances of equity-backed perps onchain through protocols like Ostium, Trade[XYZ], and Vest Markets. While they lack options’ 
convexity, equity perps could be more intuitive for retail users and unlock global access to U.S. equity exposure. 
 
The thesis for onchain equities has existed for years, but early implementations fell short. Platforms like Ostium validated demand but struggled to 
scale; at its peak, equities represented just 13% of Ostium’s open interest. This, in part, may have been due to a lack of liquidity, weak underlying 
infrastructure, and inferior distribution. 
In October 2025, Hyperliquid launched HIP-3, which enabled teams to permissionlessly launch perps markets on their order book (HyperCore). 
This meant equity perps could access the same liquidity, matching engine, and frontend as the rest of Hyperliquid perps. 
               
 


---

## Page 205

204 
 
Trade[XYZ], developed by the team behind Unit, was the ﬁrst to launch equity perps on Hyperliquid. In its ﬁrst full month, the protocol processed 
$7.7 billion in volume and now ranks as the eighth-largest perpetual DEX by open interest. 
A few factors make equity perps a timely and unique opportunity. Hyperliquid has created one of the most liquid onchain environments for perps, 
driven by a robust CLOB and an airdrop that catalyzed retail engagement. Unlike AMM-based models like GMX, which struggled to scale 
liquidity, Hyperliquid oﬀers market makers tight spreads and consistent volume, making it the ﬁrst DEX where equity perps can viably scale. 
Importantly, equity perps on Hyperliquid are oﬀered to the same users already trading crypto. As Trade[XYZ] expands its product suite across 
equities, indices, and tokens, it positions itself as a full-spectrum trading platform with a robust selection of tickers. 
There’s also a regulatory wedge. Equity perps remain oﬀ-limits to U.S. retail due to their similarity to Contracts for Diﬀerence (CFDs), which 
bypass clearinghouses and violate CFTC rules. That opens a clear path for Hyperliquid and Trade[XYZ] to serve international users while 
regulators work to deﬁne the perimeter for compliant perp markets. 
Equity perps are closer than ever to ﬁnding product-market ﬁt, and Hyperliquid and Trade[XYZ] are currently best positioned to capture that 
momentum. Still, two structural risks could threaten the long-term thesis. 
               
 


---

## Page 206

205 
 
First, Hyperliquid’s lead is narrowing. As of November 30, its share of perp DEX open interest has fallen to 55%, down from a peak above 90%. 
New competitors, such as Aster (20%) and Lighter (15%), are gaining ground, likely due to their point-based incentive programs. While demand 
may prove temporary, the shift signals that traders do not have loyalty to any one platform. 
Second, insider trading poses a unique risk to equity perps. Events like earnings and M&A carry high signal-to-noise ratios, and DEXs oﬀer a 
near-perfect venue for informed ﬂow: no KYC, high leverage, and low surveillance. Without suﬃcient retail activity to absorb losses, market 
makers could widen spreads or pull liquidity to avoid being exploited. This risk becomes more concerning as equity perps scale and attract 
institutional attention. 
Despite these risks, it seems we are in a "goldilocks" zone. The tech is ready, liquidity is present, and regulation is lagging behind. This creates a 
window for platforms like Trade[XYZ] to establish themselves as the leading venue for equity perps before the regulatory perimeter catches up. 
Increased demand for Exogenous Yield 
               
 


---

## Page 207

206 
Our view is that yield-bearing dollars backed by exogenous cash ﬂows (excluding treasury-bill revenue) will become a core collateral and savings 
asset in 2026. Exogenous yield in DeFi refers to returns whose source is identiﬁable oﬀchain cash ﬂows, such as institutional private credit, 
infrastructure revenue, or tokenized real estate income. As of December 11, 2025, tokenized U.S. Treasuries have reached a capitalization of $8.9 
billion, and current U.S. Treasuries yield 3.7%. At the same time, USDC and USDT holders still receive zero native yield, while Circle reports 
quarterly reserve income of roughly $711 million and Tether reports multi-billion-dollar annual proﬁts, largely from interest on Treasury 
portfolios that back their stablecoins. The result is a persistent spread between what stablecoin reserves earn and what stablecoin users receive, 
which creates a clear opening for exogenous yield wrappers that transmit these cash ﬂows to token holders. 
 
USD.Ai and Maple Finance are two successful examples of yield-bearing dollars that generate exogenous cash ﬂows through liquid, ERC-4626 
vault shares. USD.Ai issues a U.S. Treasury-backed stablecoin, USDai, that can be staked into sUSDai, an ERC-4626 vault share that earns yield 
from GPU-collateralized AI infrastructure loans while keeping idle capital in Treasury bills as a base return. Maple’s syrupUSDC/T product is 
also a yield-bearing ERC-4626 vault share that represents deposits into segregated institutional credit pools, which originate overcollateralized 
               
 


---

## Page 208

207 
loans to trading ﬁrms and market makers against liquid digital-asset collateral, with legal segregation of pool assets and receivables. 
Both protocols have already attracted meaningful capital. USD.AI has $669.5 million in TVL, including $286.8 million in sUSDai, yielding a 
30-day APY of 9.7%. Maple manages $4.5 billion, including $2.4 billion in syrupUSDC/T earning an average 30-day APY of 5.5%. 
 
Exogenous, variable yields create a second layer of demand beyond simply holding these assets. Holders of sUSDai, syrupUSDC, and similar 
products need tools to either lock in current yields or speculate on how those yields will change as credit conditions, borrower demand, or AI 
hardware utilization shift. In traditional markets, this role is ﬁlled by interest rate swaps and bond futures. 
Onchain, this function is fulﬁlled by Pendle, which turns yield-bearing assets into explicit, tradeable interest rate instruments. Pendle wraps assets 
such as syrupUSDC and, prospectively, sUSDai into a standardized SY token, then splits that SY into a Principal Token (PT) and a Yield Token 
(YT) in a one-to-one ratio. Buyers of Principal Tokens lock in a ﬁxed rate on exogenous yield streams, and buyers of Yield Tokens obtain 
leveraged exposure to the ﬂoating component of those yields and can trade views on future borrowing demand and credit spreads in those sectors. 
               
 


---

## Page 209

208 
Pendle’s V2 protocol has already seen signiﬁcant growth, with TVL at $3.7 billion, as the rise of yield-bearing assets, such as Ethena, emerged 
this year. As more exogenous revenue products come to market in 2026, the set of markets that Pendle can list expands. Each new market brings 
additional demand for allocators and traders to speculate on the forward yield for that speciﬁc asset. 
DayFi, a product by Daylight, is expected to launch in early 2026 and is a non-pegged yield coin backed by real-world energy assets. It is an 
income-generating asset that provides exposure to decentralized energy infrastructure while integrating directly with DeFi markets. 
Unlike soft-pegged stablecoins like sUSDai or syrupUSDC, DayFi doesn’t use the USD (or any ﬁat) peg. It represents a claim on revenue from a 
portfolio of residential solar and battery systems installed across the U.S. Capital raised through DayFi funds the energy infrastructure, which is 
held by a bankruptcy-remote special purpose vehicle (SPV). Revenue is generated from three primary sources: 
1. Long-term power purchase agreements (PPAs) with homeowners. 
2. Participation in virtual power plant (VPP) markets. 
3. Government incentives, including tax credits and renewable energy certiﬁcates.
