Payments are metered and contract-based, aggregated into a diversiﬁed, amortizing cash ﬂow portfolio. 
It’s likely we will see more forms and models of exogenous revenue in 2026 and beyond. While crypto users’ base today is quickly accumulating 
tokenized RWAs and seeking exposure to them onchain, these projects could be attractive to non-crypto natives who seek to invest in these unique 
business models. 
Credit for Non-Crypto Businesses 
               
 


---

## Page 210

209 
 
Demand for loans onchain increased in 2025. Today, 65% of onchain loans are backed by native crypto assets, while 35% are backed by RWAs. 
The majority of RWA-backed loans today are backed by home equity and originate on Figure. While crypto-backed lending onchain remains the 
dominant sector, we project a rise in demand for both categories throughout 2026. 
Crypto-Backed Loans 
While the major lending and borrowing platforms, such as Aave and Morpho, continue to facilitate the majority of crypto-backed loans, growth is 
accelerating for consumer credit for payments. 
               
 


---

## Page 211

210 
 
Demand for crypto cards has risen dramatically, with over $400 million in monthly spending volume (excluding CEX cards) driven by friendly 
regulation that has allowed banks to issue cards for crypto companies. However, the current infrastructure is ineﬃcient. The majority of existing 
crypto cards function as prepaid debit cards. This model forces users to sell their assets to fund the card, triggering taxable events and preventing 
that capital from earning yield in DeFi. 
The next evolution in this sector is the "true credit" model, which allows users to borrow stables against crypto collateral and spend those stables 
oﬀchain. ether.ﬁ card is an example of this mechanism, and it oﬀers two distinct advantages: 
1. Tax Eﬃciency: Users spend cash via a loan rather than liquidating crypto, avoiding capital gains taxes on every transaction. 
2. Capital Eﬃciency: The deposited crypto remains in a vault where it continues to earn yield, oﬀsetting the cost of credit. 
Additionally, ether.ﬁ sets the parameters for what collateral is accepted into the vault (USDC, USDT, DAI, USDe) and uses a strategy manager 
(Seven Seas) to safely allocate collateral into DeFi with professional risk management. The vault has managed to avoid any liquidations, a crucial 
               
 


---

## Page 212

211 
requirement for consumers to consider DeﬁBanks as a replacement for traditional banks. 
If platforms can successfully introduce no/low-cost loans backed by crypto at scale, they will oﬀer a capital-eﬃcient alternative that could replace 
traditional bank accounts. 
RWA-backed Loans 
RWA-backed loans onchain are currently dominated by home equity, with the vast majority of loans originating on Figure. We believe that home 
equity loans will grow, as will other forms of private credit, such as merchant lending. 
 
Home Equity Lines of Credit (HELOCs) have proven to be the most successful RWA implementation to date. Figure users alone account for $14.1 
billion in active home equity loans. The onchain model oﬀers tangible beneﬁts over traditional banking by creating a transparent marketplace that 
reduces origination time. These eﬃciencies allow lenders to pass down an average of 1.25% in cost savings to borrowers. 
While Figure is the largest non-bank HELOC lender globally, the debt issued on its underlying Provenance blockchain exists in isolation from the 
               
 


---

## Page 213

212 
broader DeFi ecosystem. Holders of this debt cannot currently leverage it in a composable manner. We expect this to change in 2026 as protocols 
develop bridges to bring these assets onchain, such as Kamino Multiply, which will allow this substantial RWA collateral to ﬂow into general 
DeFi liquidity pools 
We anticipate there will also be demand for RWA-backed loans from merchants. Many small businesses struggle with 30-60 day settlement times, 
and have less cash on hand as a result. U.S.-based merchants may have an easier time securing loans, given the maturity of U.S. banking 
infrastructure and the depth of liquidity available. However, international merchants may struggle to obtain the same level of liquidity from their 
local banks and access the same loans as U.S. merchants. 
               
 


---

## Page 214

213 
 
Global merchant cash advances are expected to grow at a 6.6% CAGR over the next four years. While U.S. merchants beneﬁt from deep banking 
infrastructure, international merchants often face insurmountable hurdles due to the lack of standardized credit scoring and local liquidity. 
Creditworthiness assessment remains subjective and fragmented across diﬀerent jurisdictions. 
               
 


---

## Page 215

214 
Additionally, creditworthiness varies by country, and the creditworthiness of a company can also be subjective, depending on the organization that 
evaluates it. Merchants could beneﬁt from a single standard, programmatic evaluation, and automated allocation of private credit based on cash 
ﬂow. 
Onchain credit protocols resolve this by establishing a single, programmatic standard for evaluating creditworthiness based on transparent onchain 
cash ﬂow. This automated allocation of private credit can service the rising demand from international merchants who are currently underserved 
by their local banking systems. 
DeﬁBanking 
Neobanks have disrupted consumer ﬁnance by eliminating physical branches and oﬀering sleek, mobile-ﬁrst interfaces. These products lowered 
fees, improved user experience, and unlocked access to ﬁnancial services for millions. But under the hood, neobanks remained tightly coupled to 
legacy infrastructure. Most rely on partner banks for custody and payments, meaning user funds are siloed inside omnibus accounts, constrained 
by settlement delays, and ultimately governed by traditional rails. 
Centralized exchanges (CEXs) have extended the neobank model to the crypto space. They dominate ﬁat onramps, hold the majority of circulating 
stablecoins, and provide a wide suite of ﬁnancial services. However, their custodial structure means they inherit many of the same limitations as 
neobanks, including permissioned access, delayed ﬁnality, and opaque risk models. Today, no product oﬀers a fully onchain banking experience 
end-to-end. 
A DeﬁBank is a non-custodial crypto bank. It reconstructs traditional ﬁnancial services using decentralized protocols, allowing users to save, 
spend, earn, and borrow through self-custodial infrastructure. Unlike CEXs or neobanks, a DeﬁBank does not rely on a banking license or 
third-party custody. Therefore, user funds are held directly in bearer assets, such as stablecoins, that settle atomically onchain and can be veriﬁed 
by anyone. Because funds are self-custodied and stablecoins should be backed 1:1, DeﬁBanks cannot operate on a fractional reserve banking 
model. 
DeﬁBanks shift the trust model from "trust the institution" to "verify the code." Settlement takes seconds, not days. Treasury revenue from 
stablecoins or payment rails can be returned to users. Access is governed by protocols, not institutions. The result is a ﬁnancial system that is 
transparent, eﬃcient, and global by default. 
How a DeﬁBank Works 
               
 


---

## Page 216

215 
 
Traditional banks and neobanks primarily focus on four key behaviors: saving, spending, earning, and borrowing. DeﬁBanks replicate each of 
these pillars using onchain tools. 
1. Save 
Saving is the foundational use case of any bank. Traditional institutions monetize deposits by lending them out, while oﬀering users a fraction of 
the returns. Neobanks improved the interface, but still route deposits through partner banks, meaning users exchange ownership for IOUs. 
DeﬁBanks retain the self-custody model. Stablecoin deposits are backed 1:1, stored in the user’s wallet, and the yield can be distributed 
transparently. They may leverage intermediaries like Bridge to manage compliance or ﬁat conversion, but the savings product itself is 
               
 


---

## Page 217

216 
non-custodial. Unlike traditional models, DeﬁBanks use yield as a loss leader to attract liquidity, then monetize across higher-margin verticals. 
2. Spend 
Most neobanks and CEXs rely on prepaid debit cards for crypto spending. These cards convert assets to ﬁat and route them through the same 
costly intermediaries that underpin credit card networks. Every $100 spent might cost a merchant $2 or more. 
DeﬁBanks start with the same infrastructure: partner banks, Cards-as-a-Service platforms, and stablecoin orchestrators. However, the ultimate 
goal is peer-to-peer wallet payments that bypass card networks entirely. With onchain send functions, users can transfer funds directly to 
merchants at near-zero cost. While this reduces per-transaction revenue, it opens the door for DeﬁBanks to capture the entire payments stack and 
reroute savings to both merchants and users. 
3. Earn 
Neobanks collapsed the boundary between savings and brokerage by embedding investment products in a single interface. This unlocked new 
revenue streams, but most rely on payment for order ﬂow or spread-based monetization. 
DeﬁBanks earn revenue from DEX protocol fees, order routing, and user ﬂow. These margins are meaningfully higher than those of traditional 
banks, particularly among “fee-insensitive” users who prioritize convenience and security. Some wallets generate over $8.00 per $1,000 in trading 
volume, a sharp contrast to legacy brokers. 
DeﬁBanks can also manage MEV internally, routing transactions directly to searchers or private relays to reduce slippage and protect user 
execution. This vertical generates high-frequency, high-margin revenue with minimal balance sheet risk. 
4. Borrow 
Borrowing unlocks liquidity from existing capital, either by posting collateral or underwriting creditworthiness. Traditional banks dominate this 
vertical through mortgages and credit cards. Neobanks, without banking licenses, are largely excluded. 
DeﬁBanks combine onchain collateral with oﬀchain attestations. A DeﬁBank can underwrite loans using a user’s token holdings, DeFi activity, 
escrowed RWAs, or even veriﬁed oﬀchain cash ﬂows. As zk-proof infrastructure and decentralized identity mature, users may unlock more 
favorable rates by building a “trust score” that travels with them across applications. 
This approach supports new markets, such as immigrant mortgages, undercollateralized lending, and portfolio-based credit. The opportunity is 
massive as U.S. mortgage debt exceeds $13 trillion and global unsecured credit tops $5 trillion. A system that can oﬀer transparent, programmable 
credit lines with veriﬁable solvency presents a meaningful threat to legacy lenders. 
               
 


---

## Page 218

217 
 
CEXs remain the dominant neobanks in crypto. They control user onboarding, custody, and trading. However, DeFi banks are now viable 
alternatives because their infrastructure is catching up, and their fundamental economics are superior. 
Three key factors give DeﬁBanks an edge: 
1. Yield Advantage: DeFi-native stablecoin funds generate higher returns than marketing-subsidized neobank yields. Products like USDai 
and DayFi employ strategies such as treasury yields, delta-neutral trading, and collateralized debt. These funds are onchain, composable, 
and programmable. 
               
 


---

## Page 219

218 
2. Cost Structure: By bypassing payment processors and card networks, DeﬁBanks eliminate layers of fees. Smart contracts execute 
settlement directly. The only cost is gas, which can often be rebated or subsidized. This unlocks new pricing models and merchant 
economics that traditional banks cannot match. 
3. Global Access: A DeﬁBank works anywhere with an internet connection. While ﬁat onramps still present a challenge, aggregators like 
Transak, MoonPay, and Ramp are closing that gap. In the long term, permissionless infrastructure will onboard users in Nigeria, Argentina, 
or the Philippines just as easily as it does in the U.S. or EU. 
The neobank revolution was built on a better interface. The DeﬁBank revolution is built on a better backend. 
By combining self-custody with composable yield, programmable payments, and real-time ﬁnality, DeﬁBanks oﬀer a full-stack alternative to both 
neobanks and CEXs. Although the model is still evolving, its structural advantages are evident. As stablecoin infrastructure expands and 
regulation provides clarity, DeﬁBanks may become the dominant distribution layer for crypto banking services. 
There is no reason a savings account, trading app, credit card, and remittance platform cannot be bundled into a single permissionless wallet. In 
fact, DeﬁBanks already exist—they just need to be packaged and scaled. 
Decentralized AI 
Authors: Chris Davis and Armita 
Key Themes 
● The relentless demand for compute, combined with the advancing capabilities of open-source models, provides decentralized compute 
networks with new revenue streams. 
● Decentralized data foundries that carve out a wedge by dominating a single frontier use case will become the most proﬁtable entities in the 
deAI stack. 
● DeAI labs will attract cult followings around diﬀerentiated, medium-sized open-source models, as this category continues to demonstrate 
strong model–market ﬁt. 
● Darwinian networks will lead the destigmatization of crypto through a virtuous cycle that attracts both top-tier talent and institutional 
demand. 
● AI agent co-pilots will bundle the DeFAI stack into a uniﬁed terminal, leveraging a powerful data ﬂywheel to challenge the dominance of 
established consumer front-ends. 
● As prediction markets scale, AI agents oﬀer a path to sustained information aggregation, more stable liquidity, and improved calibration, 
reducing structural biases without altering market fundamentals. 
               
 


---

## Page 220

219 
Introduction 
In traditional markets, AI has unquestionably been the hottest sector of 2025. The S&P 500 has seen a continuation of a multi-year trend, with 
recent reports revealing that since the release of ChatGPT in 2022, AI-related stocks have gained 165%, compared to just 24% for non-AI stocks 
over the same period. 
In private markets, venture capital continues to ﬂow aggressively into the sector. AI investment in the ﬁrst three quarters of 2025 was 62% higher 
than the full-year total for 2024, and 135% higher than in 2023. In Q3 alone, AI deals accounted for 63% of all U.S. VC funding. 
However, investor interest in decentralized AI (“deAI”) hasn’t kept pace with broader AI enthusiasm. Since peaking at 16% in Q3 2024, deAI’s 
share of total crypto venture investment has been on a downward trajectory, falling to just 9% in Q3 2025. Public markets have reﬂected a similar 
sentiment, with the sector’s share of the total altcoin market cap falling 56% since the beginning of the year. 
Despite lackluster investor interest, the sector is arguably better positioned than ever. Major trends in the broader AI industry are providing 
external tailwinds that we believe will signiﬁcantly accelerate adoption and innovation across the deAI stack: 
● Data: As public data sources are exhausted, new frontier use cases emerge, and post-training becomes a key focus, the demand for 
specialized, high-quality datasets is rising. Cryptoeconomic structures enable collection to scale rapidly while preserving privacy and 
provenance. 
● Compute: The relentless demand for compute, combined with the advancing capabilities of open-source models, is opening new demand 
vectors and enabling novel architectures for decentralized compute networks (“DCNs”). 
● Training: Advancements in test-time compute scaling with reinforcement learning have unlocked decentralized training methods that can 
produce highly capable models. 
● Agents: As autonomous agents become increasingly integrated into the digital economy, emerging standards such as x402 and ERC-8004 
are establishing crypto as the optimal infrastructure for agentic ﬁnance. 
Furthermore, AI is increasingly embedding itself into the crypto economy. This impact can be felt at every layer of the stack, from smart contract 
development and security up through consumer DeFi front-ends. 
The thesis is simple: Decentralized AI’s Age of Enlightenment is upon us. Let’s dive in. 
The Compute Economy 
In late September, Citigroup raised its forecast for data infrastructure spending to surpass $2.8 trillion through 2029, roughly in line with 
NVIDIA’s projection of $3-4 trillion by 2030. These numbers directly reﬂect the AI industry’s insatiable appetite for compute. In recent months, 
GPUs have become bargaining chips in international aﬀairs, and any information about who’s buying and selling them to whom sends shockwaves 
throughout public markets. 
               
 


---

## Page 221

220 
But, why is compute in such high demand? Without getting too far in the weeds, demand for compute is a function of the three scaling laws in AI: 
● Pre-training Scaling: Increasing training dataset size, model parameter count, and compute leads to predictable improvements in model 
intelligence and accuracy. 
● Post-training Scaling: Models can continue to improve in accuracy, eﬃciency, and specialization after initial pre-training by utilizing 
techniques such as ﬁne-tuning and reinforcement learning. 
● Test-time Scaling: At inference time, models can leverage various methodologies that use more compute per query to achieve better 
results. 
 
Leading AI labs aim to squeeze every last drop of juice from these laws, with the compute required to train frontier models increasing by roughly 
4-5x per year, according to EpochAI. However, training is just the ﬁrst step. Once models are in production, the demands of model usage, i.e., 
inference, begin to kick in. 
Inference demand has skyrocketed over the past year. In addition to the baseline demand driven by rapidly rising AI adoption, this trend has 
               
 


---

## Page 222

221 
been further ampliﬁed by the immense popularity of reasoning models. Reasoning models combine RL-trained deliberation with test-time scaling, 
enabling performance to improve by “thinking longer.” This results in using up to 10x more tokens to respond to the same prompts as 
non-reasoning models. Downstream of these models are agents, which frequently use an even greater number of tokens due to tool calls. 
In January, the release of one reasoning model in particular changed the course of open-source model development and usage: DeepSeek-R1. R1 
demonstrated that open-source models could rival the performance of top-tier closed models, such as OpenAI's o1 and Claude 3.5 Sonnet, in 
high-value tasks like coding. Since R1, the open-source landscape has seen a proliferation of highly capable models, compounding on the 
innovations brought about by DeepSeek. 
AI companies constantly balance inference cost and performance, striving to optimize the user experience while maximizing their margins. This 
has led many companies to transition to open-source models, which consistently account for roughly 30% of OpenRouter traﬃc. Leading 
Inference-as-a-Service companies Together.ai and Fireworks have capitalized on this, each generating over nine ﬁgures in annual recurring 
revenue this year, with a substantial portion coming from serving open-source model inference. 
This brings us to decentralized compute networks. 
A Whole New World 
In past years, decentralized compute networks (“DCNs”) have struggled to ﬁnd product-market ﬁt. DCNs positioned themselves as cheaper 
alternatives to leading cloud providers, such as AWS and Azure; however, even during the 2023 GPU shortages, customers never came knocking. 
These networks couldn’t capitalize on the newly rising demand for inference because closed-source models had too great a lead over open-source 
models. 
DCNs now ﬁnd themselves in a comﬁer environment. With a surplus of highly capable open-source models and soaring demands for inference, 
these networks are presented with two new avenues for monetization to supplement their GPU cloud business. 
               
 


---

## Page 223

222 
 
First, networks can attempt to carve out a wedge in the retail Inference-as-a-Service market, competing against consumer-facing platforms like 
Together.ai or Fireworks. This year, multiple DCNs have launched products in this vein: io.net released IO Intelligence on February 12, and Akash 
launched AkashML on November 24. However, only Chutes has seen breakout adoption, scaling from 410 million tokens per day in late January to 
consistently processing over 100 billion tokens per day. 
This ﬁrst path is challenging. It forces DCNs to compete not just on price, but also on Developer Experience (DX) and reliability, areas where 
centralized providers have historically excelled. 
The second avenue is for DCNs to focus on becoming a wholesale supply layer for the industry. Rather than ﬁghting for individual developers, 
DCNs can focus on aggregating raw compute supply to sell in bulk to Inference-as-a-Service startups. This allows the DCN to specialize in 
low-cost supply generation, while leaving user acquisition, veriﬁcation, and API management to specialized aggregators. 
Crucially, networks that pursue this path will need to prioritize veriﬁcation. API providers will only integrate decentralized supply if they can 
cryptographically guarantee the integrity of the compute without sacriﬁcing performance. 
Verifying Inference 
               
 


---

## Page 224

223 
Veriﬁable inference provides proof that model outputs correspond exactly to those generated by the speciﬁed model, using the supplied data and 
following the prescribed computation steps. 
In high-stakes contexts such as ﬁnancial decision-making or medical diagnostics, reliability and transparency are non-negotiable. Similarly, 
developers who pay handsomely to access leading models need assurance that they are receiving the genuine product and expected performance. 
This is especially crucial for DCNs, as users place their trust in a distributed network of contributors worldwide. 
Three primary approaches have emerged to address veriﬁability, zkML, Optimistic ML, and teeML, each with their own trade-oﬀs across cost, 
latency, and security: 
● zkML: zkML uses cryptographic proofs to verify that an inference was computed correctly without revealing the underlying model or data. 
It oﬀers the strongest guarantee, but introduces signiﬁcantly higher cost and latency. 
● Optimistic ML: Optimistic ML assumes inferences are valid unless challenged by “watchers” who can submit fraud proofs if an error is 
detected. It’s cheaper than zkML but still ineﬃcient, as users must wait through a challenge period before results are ﬁnalized. 
● teeML: teeML involves deploying LLM models within Trusted Execution Environments (TEEs) and providing onchain attestations for 
computations. While this approach is far cheaper and faster than other alternatives, it still comes with some trust assumptions, such as 
proper TEE and program conﬁguration. 
Outside of intentional tampering, veriﬁcation is further complicated by the non-deterministic nature of AI. Even when the same model processes 
identical inputs on the same hardware, results can vary. Some sources of variability are controllable, but others, such as those stemming from 
ﬂoating-point arithmetic, have proved much more challenging. 
               
 


---

## Page 225

224 
 
This year, EigenCloud extended their stack to include two new veriﬁable compute services: 
● EigenAI: A veriﬁable LLM inference API that combines cryptoeconomic veriﬁcation with a technical breakthrough enabling deterministic 
processing. 
● EigenCompute: A veriﬁable oﬀchain compute service that allows developers to run applications or agents within a trusted execution 
environment (TEE). Each deployment is recorded onchain and assigned a unique wallet. 
Unlocking deterministic inference is a big deal, and positions Eigen well to handle agentic ﬁnance use cases, where consistent execution is key. 
Builders are already working in some of these areas since its release: 
● Prediction Market Agents: Agents capable of interpreting real-world data to execute bets or resolve markets. 
● Trading Agents: Agents that reason over data with veriﬁable reasoning quality, ensuring prompts remain unmodiﬁed and trades are 
executed deterministically. 
Other DCNs have approached the veriﬁability issue in a variety of ways, with many settling on TEEs, such as Chutes, Phala, and io.net. 
               
 


---

## Page 226

225 
New Architectures for Inference 
Another trend in open-source this year has been the increasing intelligence of smaller models. Recently, we’ve seen models with between four 
billion and 40 billion parameters catching up to models nearly ten times their size. Unlike large language models that require expensive hardware 
to run, these models can be run on consumer-grade GPUs, unlocking new network architectures. 
 
These emerging network architectures coordinate small models running on dispersed, heterogeneous consumer hardware to serve inference. The 
core thesis is that by utilizing ranking and reputation systems among specialized nodes, the network can, in aggregate, produce superior responses 
compared to a single monolithic model. 
Fortytwo, a protocol built on Monad, scales inference using this approach by networking small language models (SLMs) across consumer laptops 
and desktops. The protocol leverages idle computing power to run decentralized inference tasks in the background, allowing users to contribute to 
the network without disrupting their primary device usage. 
               
 


---

## Page 227

226 
Functionally, the system distributes a single prompt across multiple nodes. These nodes independently generate responses and then rank each 
other’s outputs, eventually merging the best results into a ﬁnal consensus answer. Nodes accrue reputation based on how closely their evaluations 
align with the consensus ranking. This reputation is incrementally updated onchain, allowing the network to weigh high-quality nodes more 
heavily in future tasks. 
Beyond standard inference, the network creates a data ﬂywheel. Nodes collaboratively generate domain-speciﬁc question-and-answer pairs, which 
serve as training data for specialized open-source SLMs. These ﬁne-tuned models are then integrated back into the network, creating a 
self-evolving swarm that grows smarter with every iteration. 
In October, the team released the ﬁrst model trained on this swarm-generated dataset. Strand-Rust-Coder-14B is a Qwen2.5-Coder-14B model 
ﬁne-tuned for Rust programming using 200,000 Q&A pairs generated by the network in just under nine days. The team reports that the model 
achieved 43–48% accuracy on Rust-speciﬁc benchmarks, outperforming signiﬁcantly larger proprietary models on these tasks. 
While initial results have been impressive, Fortytwo acknowledges that the consensus process introduces latency. This naturally narrows the total 
addressable market. However, we expect the protocol could ﬁnd demand in high-stakes verticals where accuracy and reduced hallucination are 
prioritized over raw speed. In this sense, the ideal use cases are ones where "waiting 20 seconds for a 5% better answer" is a feature, not a bug. 
The Path Forward for DCNs 
We believe DCNs are better positioned today than ever before. The explosion of open-source models, paired with surging demand for compute, 
has fundamentally altered the trajectory for these networks. Unlike previous cycles, they’re now well-equipped to capitalize on the growing market 
of small-to-medium-sized AI startups looking for cost-eﬀective ways to scale. 
The coming months will reveal whether the optimal path for inference services is to compete on retail product experience or to focus on wholesale 
supply. Notably, some of the most commercially successful inference-focused DePINs that initially pursued the product route, such as Prodia and 
Hyperbolic, appear to have pivoted toward utilizing centralized compute providers this year. Prodia, for instance, stated this shift was necessary to 
achieve top-tier performance, noting that “GPU is not as commoditized as it seems on paper.” DCNs will have to continue grappling with the 
reality that achieving retail-grade reliability often conﬂicts with using purely decentralized supply. 
Swarm inference, meanwhile, stands in contrast to the broader DCN inference landscape. While most networks compete on developer experience 
(DX), the swarm architecture represents a contrarian bet: sacriﬁcing latency in exchange for accuracy. However, these networks are still in their 
infancy and need to ﬁrst prove that the value proposition holds up when the rubber meets the road. 
Finally, discussions with industry players reveal that a major barrier to scaling is the lingering stigma around crypto. We believe this has forced 
many DCNs to ﬁght an uphill battle on the demand side. Consequently, securing reputable clients serves not just as revenue, but as a critical 
endorsement of trust. This is an underappreciated catalyst that could spark unexpectedly rapid growth. 
               
 


---

## Page 228

227 
Data at the Frontier 
As the corpus of untapped publicly available data dries up and frontier use cases accelerate, data requirements are shifting from simple, unimodal 
inputs (such as static images or text) to complex, multimodal formats. 
This shift creates new monetization opportunities for crypto companies that can collect valuable data at scale. At a high level, we categorize these 
networks into two types: 
● Active: Users explicitly perform tasks where data collection is the primary objective. 
● Passive: Data is generated automatically as "digital exhaust" from a user’s existing activity. 
Tracking the exact revenue and partnerships of these companies is challenging, as agreements with large enterprises and frontier labs are typically 
shielded by NDAs. However, given the magnitude of current demand and early traction, we believe these networks are well-positioned to become 
one of the most proﬁtable entities in the decentralized AI stack in 2026. 
Active Data Collection 
As AI evolves, data annotation companies that initially focused on labeling structured, unimodal data, such as basic text for sentiment analysis, are 
expanding their oﬀerings. Nowadays, many of these companies also generate scarce, hard-to-source data, such as robotics demonstrations, niche 
post-training environments, and complex model evaluations. 
The opportunity for these companies is enormous. This year alone, Surge AI reportedly generated $1.4 billion in revenue with a team of just 121 
people and no venture funding. Meanwhile, Scale AI sold a 49% stake to Meta for $14.8 billion, while generating approximately $870 million in 
annual revenue. However, the competition is ﬁerce. Providers that are slow to deliver or fail to meet quality standards risk jeopardizing 
relationships with valuable clients. 
Over the past year, there has been an increase in companies adopting blockchain payment rails and cryptoeconomic structures to gain a 
competitive edge. These implementations typically oﬀer one or more of the following advantages: 
1. Improved Payments: Cross-border stablecoin transactions allow companies to attract supply-side users worldwide with real-time, 
frictionless payments. Additionally, they enable economically viable micropayments, unlocking a granular workforce that traditional 
banking fees would otherwise price out. 
2. Dynamic Incentives: Token-based incentives can be used to fully or partially subsidize payments for work, helping companies bootstrap 
their supply side. 
3. Rapid Scaling: Markets can be spun up instantly to meet long-tail data requests. Optional cryptoeconomic structures can tie compensation 
to performance and reputation, creating mechanisms that deter low-quality participation and encourage sustained, high-quality 
contributions. 
               
 


---

## Page 229

228 
 
Grass was one of the ﬁrst data collection companies to achieve product–market ﬁt by leveraging token incentives to bootstrap supply. The 
company focuses primarily on large-scale multimodal pretraining data, gaining a unique edge through its collection methodology. The network 
utilizes unused user bandwidth to scrape diﬀerentiated data, rewarding passive contributors with tokens for staying online. This year, Grass began 
to ramp up its revenue, scaling from near zero in Q1 to a projected $12.8 million in Q4, driven by repeat business from major AI enterprises. 
Among new entrants, we’re seeing companies carving out initial wedges within speciﬁc domains or modalities, such as Chakra with computer-use 
agents or Posiedon with audio data. 
Currently, the most saturated domain appears to be robotics, which is understandable given the present scarcity and diﬃculty of sourcing data at 
scale. However, we believe that alternative, less crowded frontier domains could provide a better wedge for forming relationships with large 
enterprises and labs. Within that wedge, companies can expand their oﬀering beyond collection and annotation to include high-value RL 
environments or novel benchmarks, dominating the vertical before expanding out. 
Chakra is a prime example of a company deploying this strategy. In late June, Chakra launched Pango, a product designed to crowdsource 
large-scale data for training computer-use agents (CUAs). CUAs operate computers by visually interpreting the interface, reasoning through tasks, 
               
 


---

## Page 230

229 
and taking actions via keyboard and mouse. For enterprises, the potential is signiﬁcant as these agents represent a major step forward in 
automating knowledge work. 
Training CUAs requires collecting and transforming real-world recordings of human computer use. However, due to coordination challenges, 
existing open-source training datasets remain severely limited in both scope and scale. Pango addresses this gap through a Chrome extension that 
enables users to earn USDC by recording their screens while completing speciﬁc tasks (e.g., working on a task for ﬁve minutes in Excel). 
Expanding on their eﬀorts in this space, Chakra used its own collected data to train GLADOS-1, the ﬁrst CUA model post-trained using collective, 
crowdsourced trajectories, and released Dojo, a collaborative reinforcement learning environment suite for developing computer-use agents. 
Passive Data Collection 
Unlike active data collection, passive data collection occurs when data generation is a secondary eﬀect of a company’s primary product or service 
oﬀering. The friction is near zero, allowing for massive scale. One of the most promising examples emerging this year is Shaga. 
 
               
 


---

## Page 231

230 
Shaga is a DePIN network that turns idle gaming PCs into a distributed cloud gaming platform from users providing their computing power. As a 
byproduct, Shaga also produces Gameplay-Action Pair (GAP) data, which is a synchronized data package that contains two fundamental 
components: 
● Gameplay (Frames): A sequence of video frames capturing the in-game visual output. 
● Action (Controls): The corresponding sequence of player inputs (e.g., keystrokes, mouse movements, controller actions) that directly 
caused those visual frames to be rendered. 
Unlike a standard gameplay video scraped from a streaming site, a GAP dataset provides a direct, veriﬁable link between a player's intent (the 
action) and its consequence (the gameplay). This raw material can be used to teach world models spatial-temporal reasoning, a cognitive ability 
that enables understanding and manipulation of objects in space and time. 
GAP data has a wide range of potential valuations. Internal estimates place a conservative ﬂoor at $1 per hour, while market intelligence indicates 
that data brokers are quoting rates between $50 and $100 per hour. Based on these estimates, Shaga's 259,400 hours of collected gaming data has 
an estimated valuation of up to ~$26 million. 
Regardless of the precise value, OpenAI’s $500 million acquisition oﬀer to gaming company Medal last year provides a benchmark for how 
desirable this data is. If Shaga can continue to scale their network, we believe that the network will be incredibly well-positioned to capitalize on 
the popularity of world models across multiple domains, such as robotics and real-time generated games. 
Competitive Dynamics & Compounding 
As mentioned above, many of the deals these companies are signing include NDAs, limiting their ability to be fully transparent about the state of 
the business. This presents an interesting opportunity. For those who understand the competitive dynamics at play and can identify winners, the 
upside surprise from revenue announcements or partnership disclosures can be signiﬁcant. 
Ultimately, competition in this ﬁeld comes down to data quality and turnaround time. Companies that build robust, scalable quality control 
systems are best positioned to compound growth through high-value partnerships, once they get a foot in the door. But getting in the door is 
challenging. Leading AI companies are ﬂooded with inquiries, and often the most eﬀective approach is to identify a speciﬁc area of demand, 
develop deep expertise in that domain, send a targeted sample dataset, and deepen trust one dataset at a time. 
We believe the year ahead will bring increased attention to this sector as more companies begin announcing partnerships with “leading AI labs” 
(yes, it will very likely be vague). Additionally, we expect to see a growing number of traditional companies integrating cryptoeconomic 
incentives and stablecoin rails to capitalize on the competitive advantages that crypto can oﬀer. 
Decentralized Training Actually Works 
Looking back at last year’s thesis from today’s vantage point makes it abundantly clear just how much progress has been made in this area. DeAI 
               
 


---

## Page 232

231 
labs have transitioned from testing initial methodologies in controlled settings to releasing competitive, mid-sized open models trained on 
heterogeneous GPUs distributed globally. While a full accounting of this past year’s training accomplishments exceeds the scope of this segment, 
we’ll ﬁrst go over the highlights from a few of the sector’s leading labs. 
Prime Intellect 
In 2025, Prime Intellect (“Prime”) leaned fully into the broader shift toward test-time compute with reinforcement learning. Major releases this 
year included SYNTHETIC-1 and SYNTHETIC-2, decentralized runs to generate datasets of veriﬁed reasoning traces, INTELLECT-2, one of the 
ﬁrst 32 billion parameter model post-trained via globally distributed RL, and INTELLECT-3, a 106 billion parameter model that achieves 
state-of-the-art performance for its size across math, code, science, and reasoning benchmarks. Alongside these releases, the team open-sourced 
various infrastructure for deAI development, including TOPLOC, Prime Collective Communications Library, and the Environments Hub. 
In February, the team secured $15 million in funding, with participation from some of the most inﬂuential voices in AI, including Andrej 
Karpathy, Clem Delangue, and Dylan Patel. While this funding provides the company with the necessary resources to continue focusing on 
growth, Prime has also stated that it’s actively exploring novel revenue models centered around licensing markets for models and agents. 
Nous Research 
Like many of the other leading deAI labs, Nous has notably leaned into the RL paradigm. In February, they released DeepHermes 3 Preview, a 
model that uniﬁed reasoning and intuitive language capabilities. Soon after, they introduced Atropos, an asynchronous RL framework that allows 
developers to train models in either custom-built environments or the pre-existing ones available in the repo. 
After Atropos, Nous released their most signiﬁcant project of the year: Psyche Network, a decentralized training orchestrator that builds upon 
their earlier optimization methods, DeMO and DisTrO. The network integrates Solana for node coordination and event logging, with training 
rewards expected in future updates. 
               
 


---

## Page 233

232 
 
By August, Hermes 4 launched as a full line of hybrid reasoning models, leveraging Atropos for various training stages. Alongside these models, 
Nous released a new benchmark, RefusalBench, which tests a model’s willingness to be helpful in various scenarios commonly disallowed by 
both closed and open models. Prompts related to minor speciﬁc harm, 
exploitation and human traﬃcking, and suicide/self-harm were selected as categories for conditional reward inversion, meaning refusals are scored 
positively. 
Most recently, Nous has released Hermes 4.3, a post-trained ByteDance Seed 36B model that was fully trained on the Psyche Network. 
Gensyn 
This year, Gensyn unveiled the results of years of behind-the-scenes development. Over the course of 2025, the team released a series of papers 
detailing various methodologies that underpin their protocol, such as Verde, SkipPipe, and CheckFree. 
The protocol is currently transitioning from testnet to mainnet, with a few core apps that users can engage with: 
● RL Swarm: A framework for collaborative reinforcement learning over the internet. 
               
 


---

## Page 234

233 
● BlockAssist: An open-source AI assistant that learns from your Minecraft gameplay, training locally and running on your device. 
● Judge: A veriﬁable evaluation framework built on Verde that can be used for any context where trustworthy evaluation is critical, such as 
prediction markets. 
● Delphi: A prediction market for machine intelligence. Users purchase stakes in speciﬁc models based on their anticipated performance in 
benchmarks, eﬀectively crowdsourcing a live, ﬁnancialized index of model quality. 
Gensyn’s testnet saw strong participation, with over 500,000 models trained across all applications. As of December 17, the protocol is two days 
into its English auction token sale with $3.49 million committed at a $115 million FDV. 
Pluralis 
Pluralis is tackling the complexities of using model-parallelism in pre-training. One motivation behind this approach is to unlock open-source 
monetization. If a model can be trained collaboratively across multiple participants, no single party ever gains access to the complete model. As a 
result, it doesn’t exist in its entirety anywhere and can be monetized. 
In June 2025, Pluralis released its Protocol Models paper, outlining their training methodology and presenting a controlled proof-of-concept run. 
Then in September, Pluralis extended the method into production with Node-0-7.5B run, the ﬁrst multi-party pretrain that ran in a completely 
open, heterogeneous GPU swarm. The run concluded in mid-October, featuring over 300 active participants, with 1,642 individual GPUs 
contributing from 198 cities worldwide. 
Looking forward, the team plans to keep scaling runs, integrating more features from their recent work on asynchronous training, veriﬁcation, 
unextractibility, and context-parallelism. 
Toward Adoption 
While the progress made this year is undoubtedly impressive, one of the elephants in the room is whether these models will actually see 
meaningful adoption. 
So far, the jury is still out. Downloads of Nous’ Hermes 4 line have fallen short of those of previous releases, with the top model in the series 
receiving roughly 13,000 downloads, compared to over 100,000 for earlier models. Meanwhile, Prime Intellect’s INTELLECT-3 has been 
downloaded over 18,000 times since its release on November 26, a 17,000% increase over INTELLECT-2. 
One explanation for this divergence is timing. Nous’ earlier models entered the market before the surge in open-source development that has 
deﬁned this year. Given how crowded the market has become, it’s much harder to stand out these days. Still, for both Prime and Nous, download 
ﬁgures remain well below the millions recorded by the leading open-source models. 
 
               
 


---

## Page 235

234 
 
On the bright side, many of these models appear well-positioned to capitalize on shifting developer preferences. According to a recent study from 
OpenRouter, medium-sized open-source models (15 billion to 70 billion parameters) are increasingly ﬁnding “model-market ﬁt” as users seek a 
balance between capabilities and eﬃciency. This segment has grown from negligible usage at the start of the year to consistently capturing 
25–30% of weekly open-source model activity on OpenRouter. 
Furthermore, users are showing an increasing willingness to experiment with new architectures. Since early September, no single model has 
consistently held more than 20–25% of OSS token volume. This suggests that if a lab can release a suﬃciently diﬀerentiated (and performant) 
model, it can quickly capture the community's attention. 
Innovation & Monetization 
At this stage, we believe the tools used to build these models are more likely to see widespread adoption than the models themselves. With each 
successive release, labs are open-sourcing more of their training infrastructure, including the frameworks, libraries, and tools used throughout the 
training process. While external adoption of these tools has been limited so far, we expect it to grow as these oﬀerings mature into a more 
cohesive and complete stack. 
However, another (much larger) elephant in the room is the question of whether these companies can establish a path to sustainable revenue. Each 
               
 


---

## Page 236

235 
lab is currently taking a diﬀerent approach. Prime Intellect oﬀers GPU rentals through a standard Neocloud model. Nous monetizes through API 
credits and a chat-based subscription. Gensyn takes a percentage of fees for tasks completed on its network. And Pluralis… well, they’re just 
getting started. While revenue numbers are not publicly available, we believe current amounts are likely modest. 
That said, these labs are likely well-capitalized enough to get through 2026 without needing to focus heavily on monetization. Prime raised $15 
million in February, Nous raised $50 million in April, Pluralis raised $7.6 million in March, and Gensyn is midway through their token sale at the 
time of writing. And besides, Ilya said we’re back to the age of research. 
Still, we believe we’ll see early productization as these labs begin laying the groundwork for more substantial monetization in the future. 
The Agents Are Here 
The deﬁning narrative of deAI last year was the agent mania that took hold of the market in Q4'24. The market indiscriminately bid up agent 
tokens like aixbt and Zerebro, despite oﬀering little more than a provocative X account. As expected, the euphoria turned to panic when the 
mini-bubble burst. The emperor had no clothes, and the market responded accordingly. 
In hindsight, it’s ironic (or ﬁtting?) that 2025’s ﬁnal hurrah of deAI retail interest came less than a month before the release of DeepSeek-R1, an 
event that sparked a wave of open-source development and positioned deAI in a much more favorable environment. Nevertheless, the agent sector 
continued to march forward, shedding the dead weight of opportunistic participants while solidifying its role in both the traditional digital 
economy and the crypto economy. 
Powering The Agent Economy 
As more users leverage AI for everyday tasks, the digital interface that powers commerce is gradually shifting from search to AI, with agents 
compressing the product discovery and purchasing process. McKinsey states that by 2030, the U.S. B2C retail market alone could see up to $1 
trillion in orchestrated revenue from agentic commerce, with global projections reaching as high as $3 trillion to $5 trillion. 
Leading AI labs and payments companies are acutely aware of this opportunity. In September, several organizations released agentic payment 
protocols, most notably Google’s AP2 and OpenAI’s ACP. Built in collaboration with Stripe, ACP focuses on in-chat commerce, while AP2 
enables secure transactions across all agentic payment scenarios. Whereas ACP is product-driven, AP2 serves as a broader standard that supports 
an autonomous agent-to-agent economy, one with the potential to run on crypto rails. 
While traditional payment rails, such as pay-by-card systems with ﬁxed transaction fees, can suﬃce for human-initiated purchases of goods and 
services, these systems are poorly suited to support a digital economy driven by agent-to-agent interactions that require high-frequency, low-cost 
transactions. 
Both crypto-native and traditional businesses recognize this bottleneck. Over the past several months, ﬁntech and AI companies have increasingly 
positioned themselves to capitalize on the integration of stablecoins into agentic commerce: 
               
 


---

## Page 237

236 
● Building: Stripe’s stablecoin-focused blockchain, Tempo, is explicitly designed to accommodate agent payments, with Anthropic and 
OpenAI among its initial design partners. 
● Funding: On September 2, Kite AI, a purpose-built blockchain for agent payments, announced an $18 million Series A round led by 
PayPal Ventures. 
● Collaborating: Google’s AP2 standard includes Coinbase, Circle, Questﬂow, MetaMask, and other crypto companies among its 60+ 
payments and technology partners. 
During this time, one protocol has come to deﬁne the sector: x402. 
 
Developed by Coinbase and launched in May, x402 leverages the existing HTTP 402 code to enable programmatic payments over HTTP via a 
request–response ﬂow. When a client requests a paid resource, the server responds with payment requirements that can be fulﬁlled via blockchain 
rails. This design unlocks instant, per-request payments for content and services, such as data access or AI model inference, initiated by humans 
or agents. Though x402 is still new, it has already been adopted by leading tech enterprises, including Google (through an AP2 extension) and 
               
 


---

## Page 238

237 
Cloudﬂare (via their Agents SDK and MCP integration) 
In mid-October, x402 caught ﬁre within the crypto community. Daily transactions climbed from fewer than 5,000 to a peak of 3.9 million on 
November 3, as a growing crypto-native ecosystem emerged to support its expansion. This ﬁrst surge was driven by speculative activity, as people 
bid up memecoins minted through the protocol. However, amid the frenzy of speculation, some real-world use cases have begun to emerge. 
The most distinct advantage x402 holds over traditional card networks is its ability to facilitate micropayments. Early data indicates that organic 
usage is already coalescing around this value proposition: top protocols are now processing millions of low-value transactions for high-frequency, 
on-demand utilities, such as AI inference and agentic services. 
However, to unlock broader adoption, the network must address critical friction points, including trust and discovery. 
Currently, the EVM is positioning itself as the de facto onchain layer for agent identity and reputation via ERC-8004. This proposed standard 
extends Google’s Agent2Agent Protocol by adding a layer of onchain identity, reputation, and veriﬁability. The protocol leverages an ERC-721 
Identity Registry to make agents immediately discoverable and transferable across applications, allowing them to advertise speciﬁc endpoints and 
cross-chain identities. To establish trust, the system integrates Reputation and Validation Registries that store transparent, onchain feedback scores 
and enable independent veriﬁcation of agent work through mechanisms like zkML and TEEs. 
While x402 holds the potential to radically transform the digital economy, widespread adoption will take time, as it hinges on high-value 
integrations across both the client and server sides. For servers, this will require implementing infrastructure that bridges the gap between 
traditional payment systems and crypto. Setups like Bridge’s integrations with Stripe are best positioned to facilitate this adoption, providing 
traditional companies with tools to easily integrate stablecoin payments. On the client side, adoption could signiﬁcantly accelerate through 
payment integrations into leading chat interfaces, a probable development given the current direction of AI labs and their work on agentic 
payments. 
DeFAI Begins to Fulﬁll Its Promise 
Early this year, we saw budding experimentation at the intersection of AI agents and DeFi, a sector often referred to as “DeFAI.” In our February 
report on the sector, we segmented the stack into three distinct layers (reasoning, execution, and abstraction) and outlined four primary use cases 
being explored: Alpha Agents, Execution Agents, AI-Enabled Abstraction, and Portfolio Optimization. 
At the time, most products were essentially proof-of-concepts. Since then, the sector has transitioned to full production mode. While many early 
projects have quietly shut down shop, others have remained heads down, relentlessly shipping new features and iterating on their product (shout 
out Bankr). 
Additionally, new subsectors are seeing meaningful adoption. Yield optimization agents are ﬁnding product-market ﬁt among retail and 
institutional clients, growing assets under management from tens of thousands to tens of millions. Leading the sector, Giza has generated over 
$3.2 billion in transaction volume across more than 57,000 individual agents, attracting capital from top crypto ﬁrms like Re7. While the protocol 
               
 


---

## Page 239

238 
began with a stablecoin yield optimization oﬀering, it has since expanded to complex Pendle strategies—a shift we believe oﬀers signiﬁcantly 
more value and diﬀerentiation. 
 
Another subsector gaining traction is AI co-pilots. Companies like True North and Surf oﬀer intelligence products that synthesize onchain and 
oﬀchain data to help users navigate markets. For these companies, data is the ultimate competitive diﬀerentiator. Well-constructed data ﬂywheels 
can rapidly improve underlying systems, creating compounding eﬀects that strengthen defensibility. 
Looking forward, we believe there are several possible outcomes for the sector: Vertical Integration, Embedded AI, and Modular Orchestration. 
Vertical Integration 
In this scenario, specialized platforms vertically integrate adjacent DeFAI services. For example, a research co-pilot might integrate native trading 
execution, yield farming, and portfolio management directly into its product. This strategy is analogous to the Bloomberg Terminal model, where 
data, analysis, and execution reside within a single walled garden. If a platform can execute this, it creates a signiﬁcant competitive advantage, 
fueling a data ﬂywheel that makes the product indispensable to users. 
               
 


---

## Page 240

239 
Embedded AI 
Rather than new DeFAI apps winning users, existing dominant interfaces (like Phantom, Axiom, or centralized exchanges) will plug into the best 
systems via API. In this outcome, DeFAI projects become silent backend services, while the front-end retains the customer relationship and the 
fees. This is analogous to Apple embedding models from OpenAI or Google directly into the OS rather than building its own. Given that the 
sector is still in its infancy, this could also take the form of an acquisition, where a major wallet or exchange acquires a leading DeFAI startup to 
gain exclusive access to the technology. 
Modular Orchestration 
Instead of monolithic applications, we observe the emergence of aggregation platforms that coordinate thousands of specialized agents, for 
example, distinct agents for yield optimization or sentiment analysis. Users interact with a single 'Master Agent' that routes tasks to the optimal 
specialist, eﬀectively functioning as a composable app store for intelligence. Virtuals has already deployed this strategy, with Butler acting as a 
master agent that commissions speciﬁc Virtuals agents on the user’s behalf. We expect other aggregation platforms, such as Warden, to follow suit. 
These platforms compete to attract agent developers, with this distribution leverage downstream of agent performance, as users will naturally 
gravitate toward the platforms that deliver the best results. 
Looking Ahead 
We believe the greatest opportunity lies in specialized platforms that vertically integrate and reﬁne their reasoning systems as their data ﬂywheel 
strengthens. Consolidating the entire DeFi experience into a single terminal generates a rich stream of user data. This data can be harnessed to 
hyper-personalize the experience using oﬀchain context, such as decision-making patterns, accepted or rejected ideas, and speciﬁc risk 
preferences. 
The result is a powerful lock-in eﬀect: switching platforms would mean starting over with a "cold start" experience that lacks personalization. If a 
"DeFAI terminal" gains meaningful traction, we will likely see leading front-ends pivot toward an embedded AI strategy to defend their 
positioning. 
That said, this shift will take time to materialize. Trust remains a major barrier to the adoption of DeFAI. While users may feel comfortable 
transacting a few hundred dollars, trading with tens of thousands demands robust guardrails. As a result, it is likely that, for now, trades will 
continue to be executed manually through integrations, e.g., Hyperliquid builder codes. 
Darwinian Networks Draw in Top Global Talent 
Darwinian platforms implement competition-based incentive structures to coordinate resources on a global scale. On these networks, top 
               
 


---

## Page 241

240 
performers receive monetary rewards for advancing the ecosystem, while underperforming participants are naturally ﬁltered out. 
This methodology is particularly powerful when applied to the coordination of intelligence. This year, while leading AI companies oﬀered 
hundreds of millions in annual salaries to poach researchers from rival labs, Darwinian networks produced genuinely competitive results through 
permissionless, meritocratic structures. 
Bittensor: The King of Darwinian Platforms 
Bittensor is an ecosystem of independent networks, known as subnets, that address speciﬁc AI tasks through competitive market dynamics. Each 
subnet includes three participants: 
● Owners: Deﬁne the subnet’s incentive mechanism (e.g., rewarding faster inference or higher-quality data). 
● Miners: Compete to deliver the best solutions and earn rewards based on performance. 
● Validators: Evaluate miner performance against the subnet’s criteria and rank participants accordingly. 
In last year’s theses, we cited Bittensor’s Dynamic TAO upgrade as a key development to keep an eye on. We argued that the network has the 
potential to spawn multi-billion-dollar subnets, with the caveat that reaching its full potential requires onboarding top AI talent. A year later, it 
appears that talent acquisition has outpaced demand for subnet tokens. We believe that this presents a signiﬁcant opportunity. 
In 2025, Bittensor’s subnet ecosystem rapidly transitioned from ideation to product. Platforms are seeing strong growth across multiple verticals, 
with over $20 million in annualized revenue generated from compute-focused subnets alone. Meanwhile, research eﬀorts are translating into truly 
decentralized training runs at a global scale, such as IOTA’s Training at Home. 
               
 


---

## Page 242

241 
 
One subnet that epitomizes Bittensor’s ability to coordinate intelligence and deliver competitive results is Ridges. Ridges incentivizes the 
development of advanced coding agents through a highly structured and performance-driven platform. The Ridges Platform manages agent 
submissions, orchestrates evaluations, and facilitates real-time communication between miners, screeners, validators, and external services. 
Miners submit open-source agents through the platform, where they are initially screened against ﬁve benchmark problems. Agents that 
successfully solve at least three progress to a full validator evaluation. 
The competition follows a winner-take-all model, with the highest-scoring agent receiving all incentives until surpassed. While uncommon among 
subnets, this model has proven highly eﬀective. Rewards are streamed continuously to the top agent, and holding the number-one spot for just two 
days can yield over $100,000. This structure has created a ﬁercely competitive environment that draws top AI and coding talent, including 
researchers from OpenAI and DeepMind. 
Requiring agents to be open-source has created a powerful ﬂywheel of iteration, enabling Ridges to rapidly surpass their competition. In 
September, Ridges’ leading agent outperformed all open-source competitors on the SWE-bench, the most widely used coding evaluation 
framework, which features 2,294 real-world software engineering problems sourced from GitHub issues. Looking ahead, the Ridges team plans to 
               
 


---

## Page 243

242 
productize the subnet via a chat interface, allowing engineers to interact directly with the top-performing agent to solve problems end-to-end. 
Validation from the Outside 
Increasingly, notable organizations across various industries are recognizing the beneﬁts of Darwinian networks. This year, we’ve seen multiple 
industry-leading organizations place their faith in the ability of these networks to deliver superior results. 
Numerai 
Founded in 2019, Numerai is a quantitative market-neutral hedge fund powered by a crowdsourced network of data scientists. At its core is the 
Numerai Tournament, a recurring competition in which participants build machine learning models to predict stock returns using obfuscated 
ﬁnancial datasets. 
Numerai managed roughly $60.0 million in assets in 2022, before scaling to $173.0 million by May 2024 and to over $440.0 million by mid-2025. 
Over the course of 2024, the fund delivered 12 straight positive months and a 25.5% net return. 
In August, Numerai secured a commitment of up to $500 million from JPMorgan Asset Management, marking the ﬁrm’s largest institutional 
backer to date and putting its ﬂagship fund on a path toward nearly $1 billion in deployed capital. 
CrunchDAO 
CrunchDAO connects a global network of ML engineers to enterprises seeking solutions to speciﬁc challenges they face. During a competition, 
participants receive curated, encrypted training datasets from the organization and create models trained with the data. The models are then 
uploaded and ranked based on the scoring function provided by the organization. 
In 2025, CrunchDAO saw steady adoption among the ML community, experiencing 47.5% growth in unique participants, expanding from 7,800 
in January to 10,400 as of October 17. Participants' backgrounds vary widely, from individuals with less than one year of experience working with 
data to PhD-level ML scientists, who currently account for roughly 10% of users. 
On December 9, the Eric and Wendy Schmidt Center launched its latest competition, Obesity ML Competition: Tackling Metabolic Diseases, in 
partnership with Broad Diabetes Initiative, Massachusetts General Hospital, and Beth Israel Deaconess Medical Center. This is the latest in a 
series of competitions launched by the organization, signaling that CrunchDAO’s model has consistently delivered top-tier results, trusted by the 
world’s leading academics and domain experts. 
Looking Forward 
One of the most persistent challenges the crypto industry faces is the 'trust barrier.' Years of negative publicity (some warranted, some not) have 
made outsiders skeptical of working with crypto companies, placing companies at an immediate competitive disadvantage. This creates a systemic 
               
 


---

## Page 244

243 
vicious cycle. Without consistent inﬂows of high-quality talent, the ecosystem remains skewed toward lower-quality actors, which further 
reinforces the stigma. 
We believe Darwinian networks are breaking this loop. Top-tier talent is entering the space, driving results that challenge centralized 
incumbents and attract institutional demand. By establishing legitimacy through this virtuous cycle of talent and demand, they are actively 
destigmatizing the broader industry. 
Given the results we have seen this year, we have strong conviction that this virtuous cycle will continue to strengthen, advancing the maturity and 
reputation of the industry as a whole. 
The Smart Contract Security Dilemma 
AI is a double-edged sword for smart contract development. While AI-assisted coding democratizes the ability to deploy onchain applications, AI 
provides hackers with a powerful tool for identifying and exploiting vulnerabilities in code. 
This exploitation risk was recently highlighted in a study from Anthropic, where researchers developed a new benchmark that evaluates agents’ 
ability to exploit smart contracts. The benchmark, termed SCONE-bench, comprises 405 smart contracts with real-world vulnerabilities exploited 
between 2020 and 2025. Researchers found that the ten models evaluated collectively produced turnkey exploits for 51% of the problems. The 
total amount of funds stolen in the simulation was $550.1 million… Yikes. 
This study is a rude awakening for the industry. If AI systems start regularly draining millions of dollars from protocols, it will be nearly 
impossible to convince anyone to come onchain. Fortunately, an increasing number of teams are developing AI-driven solutions to prevent attacks 
before they occur. 
From Workﬂow to Post-Deployment 
Platforms like Octane integrate AI-powered security directly into the developer workﬂow. By continuously scanning contracts during development 
and proposing automated ﬁxes, these tools aim to identify vulnerabilities before the code reaches the mainnet. 
Complementing this are AI-native adversarial testing systems such as TestMachine, which apply reinforcement learning and guided fuzzing to 
explore attack surfaces that traditional tools often miss. Instead of checking contracts against known vulnerability patterns, these systems behave 
more like autonomous attackers, searching for novel exploit paths. 
Even with rigorous pre-deployment testing, however, vulnerabilities can and do slip through. This has driven growing demand for runtime security 
and transaction-level enforcement, an area led by platforms like Forta. By monitoring onchain activity in real time and, in some cases, blocking 
high-risk transactions before execution, these systems function as an adaptive ﬁrewall for deployed protocols. 
Looking Forward 
               
 


---

## Page 245

244 
As mentioned above, the SCONE-bench study exposes a capability gap that is widening faster than many realize. However, this presents a distinct 
strategic advantage for the industry. Rather than discovering these vulnerabilities through a series of catastrophic, mainnet-draining events, the 
market has been granted a preview of the threat landscape in a controlled environment. For protocols that understand the competitive dynamics at 
play, this “digital ﬁre drill” oﬀers a brief window to front-run the risk. The ability to harden infrastructure against autonomous agents now, before 
they are weaponized at scale, will likely become a deﬁning factor in preserving protocol treasury and reputation. 
Ultimately, protocol viability in this new era comes down to the sophistication of the defensive stack. We believe the coming cycles will drive a 
fundamental repricing of security, moving it from a pre-deployment checklist to a continuous, active mandate. We expect to see a sharp increase in 
capital allocation toward AI-native defense systems, as teams realize that static audits alone are insuﬃcient against dynamic AI adversaries. 
Integrate these adaptive security layers will be necessary to capture institutional volume, establishing the high-trust environment necessary to 
thrive in an increasingly hostile onchain landscape. 
Prediction Market’s Missing Layer: AI 
Prediction markets have re-emerged as a visible segment of the crypto and ﬁntech landscape, drawing renewed attention from media, 
policymakers, and market participants. Platforms such as Polymarket have demonstrated that onchain event markets can capture real-world 
interest across politics, macroeconomics, sports, and crypto-native events, often generating sharp spikes in activity around major news cycles. In 
recent election cycles, prediction market prices have even been referenced alongside traditional polling and forecasting tools, reinforcing their 
relevance as information signals. 
Despite this visibility, prediction markets remain a marginal category within decentralized ﬁnance. By standard onchain metrics, total value locked 
(TVL), sustained liquidity, and capital eﬃciency, the sector remains small relative to established DeFi primitives such as lending, decentralized 
exchanges, and liquid staking. 
               
 


---

## Page 246

245 
 
This disparity is clear at the category level. As of December 15, total DeFi TVL is approximately $118 billion, with liquid staking protocols 
accounting for roughly $55 billion, lending protocols exceeding $62 billion, and decentralized exchanges holding close to $17 billion. In contrast, 
prediction markets collectively hold around $370 million in TVL, representing well under 1% of total DeFi capital. Even Polymarket, the largest 
prediction market by TVL at roughly $277 million, remains small not only relative to core DeFi sectors but also compared to several newer 
ﬁnancial primitives. While category-level TVL estimates may involve some degree of double-counting, the order-of-magnitude diﬀerence is stable 
and unambiguous. 
               
 


---

## Page 247

246 
 
This gap between attention and adoption is structural rather than cyclical. Prediction markets are constrained by binary payoﬀ designs, fragile 
liquidity, and reliance on intermittent human participation. As markets approach resolution, risk concentrates sharply, spreads widen, and order 
books thin. Liquidity provision often depends on short-term subsidies rather than durable participation, limiting scalability and market depth, 
particularly outside of headline events. As a result, prediction markets struggle to support consistent price discovery and capital deployment at 
scale. 
Recent advances in artiﬁcial intelligence introduce a credible mechanism for addressing these constraints. Rather than replacing human judgment 
or governance, AI could be best understood as a new participation layer within prediction markets. Large language models and autonomous agents 
are well-suited to synthesizing global information, updating probabilistic beliefs continuously, and engaging in automated trading behavior. In this 
framing, prediction markets do not primarily need more users; they need more consistent, higher-quality participants. AI has the potential to 
address three structural bottlenecks: information aggregation, liquidity provision, and resolution support. 
Three Areas Where AI Can Enhance Prediction Markets 
               
 


---

## Page 248

247 
● Forecasting and Information Aggregation: AI systems can synthesize large volumes of structured and unstructured data into 
probabilistic signals that improve market calibration. 
● Liquidity Provision and Market Participation: Autonomous agents can act as continuous traders or market makers, reducing spreads and 
stabilizing market depth. 
● Resolution and Governance Support: AI can assist oracle and dispute systems by benchmarking outcomes and surfacing relevant 
evidence, without determining ﬁnal truth. 
Information Aggregation and Forecast Quality 
The Current Problem 
Prediction markets rely on participants to ingest dispersed information and translate beliefs into prices. In practice, this process is uneven and 
slow. Human traders face cognitive, informational, and time constraints, particularly in markets that require synthesizing large volumes of 
unstructured data such as news, social media, governance updates, or macro signals. Empirical research on Polymarket data shows persistent 
pricing biases, delayed convergence, and uneven calibration, especially in low-liquidity or long-horizon markets. Prices often reﬂect sentiment and 
attention rather than fully integrated information until late in a market’s lifecycle. These limitations reduce forecast quality and weaken the core 
value proposition of prediction markets as real-time probability estimators. 
How AI Addresses the Problem 
AI systems, particularly large language models and agent-based forecasting systems are structurally aligned with the informational role prediction 
markets require. They can ingest and synthesize vast volumes of structured and unstructured data, including news coverage, social sentiment, 
governance discussions, and historical precedents, and convert these inputs into continuously updated probabilistic beliefs. 
Crucially, AI does not function as an oracle of truth. Instead, it generates signals that are expressed through trading behavior. Market mechanisms 
then discipline these signals through proﬁt and loss, rewarding well-calibrated forecasts and penalizing error. In this framework, AI improves the 
consistency, coverage, and timeliness of information inputs, while the market remains responsible for validating accuracy. 
Early implementations reﬂect this direction. Research and market commentary highlight AI-driven agents actively identifying systematic biases in 
prediction markets, such as favorite–longshot bias, and exploiting mispricings through automated strategies. New platforms and tools increasingly 
frame prediction markets as probabilistic data layers that can be queried, modeled, and traded against algorithmically, rather than as purely 
human-driven betting venues. 
For example, Allora extends this approach by operating a decentralized inference market in which autonomous agents submit probabilistic 
forecasts and are economically rewarded for accuracy over time. The protocol illustrates how decentralized systems can coordinate competing AI 
forecasts through market-based incentives rather than centralized oracle mechanisms 
Synth illustrates how AI forecasting engines are entering prediction markets indirectly, supplying probabilistic signals used by AI-powered trading 
               
 


---

## Page 249

248 
systems operating on platforms such as Polymarket and Kalshi. 
Liquidity Provision and Market Participation 
The Current Problem 
Liquidity is the central bottleneck in prediction markets. Binary payoﬀ structures concentrate risk as resolution approaches, discouraging 
sustained participation and leading to thin order books and volatile spreads. Unlike spot or derivative markets where liquidity providers retain 
exposure to assets with residual value, liquidity provision in binary markets involves holding inventories that partially expire worthless at 
settlement. 
Empirical analysis of Polymarket shows that liquidity providers systematically withdraw capital as markets converge, precisely when accurate 
price discovery is most valuable. This dynamic increases slippage, slows convergence, and limits the market’s ability to absorb large trades, 
constraining prediction markets’ scalability and institutional relevance. 
How AI Addresses the Problem 
Autonomous AI agents reduce the cost and friction of continuous participation. Unlike human traders, agents can quote prices persistently, update 
beliefs with low latency, and operate across many markets simultaneously. When multiple agents with heterogeneous models and priors interact, 
they generate arbitrage pressure that tightens spreads and improves market depth. 
Academic work on prediction market liquidity shows that forecast accuracy improves materially with deeper and more stable liquidity. AI agents 
help address this constraint by making market-making and arbitrage economically viable without requiring constant human attention. While AI 
does not eliminate the structural risks of binary outcomes, it lowers participation costs and enables more continuous, disciplined trading behavior. 
In practice, AI functions as a participation layer, stabilizing markets through sustained engagement rather than episodic human involvement. 
Gnosis provides agent-native infrastructure through its long-standing work on prediction markets (e.g., conditional tokens), automation tooling 
(e.g., Safe modules), and experimentation with autonomous agents, positioning it as a natural foundation for AI-driven participation. 
Gnosis-based prediction markets such as Omen and its successor Presagio demonstrate how agent-driven participation can persist longer into a 
market’s lifecycle within LMSR-style designs, supporting engagement without removing the underlying payoﬀ asymmetries of binary markets. 
Resolution and Governance Support 
The Current Problem 
Prediction markets ultimately depend on robust resolution mechanisms to determine outcomes. Onchain platforms rely on oracle systems and 
dispute processes designed to handle ambiguity, incentives, and adversarial behavior. These processes can be slow and information-intensive, 
particularly for complex, subjective, or contested events. 
While decentralization reduces reliance on centralized arbiters, it also increases the burden on participants to gather evidence, interpret outcomes, 
               
 


---

## Page 250

249 
and coordinate around resolution. Full automation is neither feasible nor desirable, as many events require judgment and social consensus. 
How AI Addresses the Problem 
AI can support resolution workﬂows without replacing governance. Models can assist by aggregating relevant evidence, benchmarking outcomes 
against historical precedents, identifying inconsistencies across data sources, and ﬂagging anomalous claims during disputes. This reduces 
informational overhead for oracle participants and disputers while preserving incentive alignment and human decision-making. 
Research in AI-driven risk assessment across DeFi demonstrates that models are particularly eﬀective at synthesizing fragmented onchain and 
oﬀchain data, surfacing early warning signals, and improving decision eﬃciency without assuming authority. Applied to prediction markets, these 
same capabilities can improve the scalability and reliability of resolution processes especially as platforms expand into more complex event 
categories. 
In this role, AI enhances governance rather than supplanting it, strengthening trust in outcomes while preserving the core decentralized design of 
prediction markets. 
Early protocol designs reﬂect this support-oriented approach. For example, Opinion integrates AI-assisted tooling into oracle and resolution 
workﬂows to help aggregate evidence, evaluate complex macro data, and reduce informational overhead during market resolution. Importantly, 
these systems do not automate truth determination; instead, they aim to improve the eﬃciency and scalability of existing dispute and governance 
processes while preserving human judgment and incentive alignment. 
Looking Forward 
AI will not replace human judgment in prediction markets, nor should it. Resolution ultimately depends on governance, accountability, and social 
consensus. The role of AI is to strengthen participation, not to determine truth. 
As prediction markets scale, the limits of episodic, human-only participation become increasingly binding. AI agents oﬀer a path to sustained
